<content>A few days back she had reminded him of that oath.</content>
<content>He had answered: "Nonsense!</content>
<content>I couldn't  have sworn any such thing!"</content>
<content>By some awkward fatality he remembered it now.</content>
<content>What queer things men would swear for the sake of women!</content>
<content>He would have sworn it at any time to gain her!</content>
<content>He would swear it now, if thereby he could touch her - but nobody could touch her, she was cold-hearted!</content>
<content>And memories crowded on him with the fresh, sweet savour of the spring wind-memories of his courtship.</content>
<content>In the spring of the year 1881 he was visiting his old school-fellow and client, George Liversedge, of Branksome, who, with the view of developing his pine-woods in the neighbourhood of Bournemouth, had placed the formation of the company necessary to the scheme in Soames's hands.</content>
<content>Mrs. Liversedge, with a sense of the fitness of things, had given a musical tea in his honour.</content>
<content>Later in the course of this function, which Soames, no musician, had regarded as an unmitigated bore, his eye had been caught by the face of a girl dressed in mourning, standing by herself.</content>
<content>The lines of her tall, as yet rather thin figure, showed through the wispy, clinging stuff of her black dress, her black-gloved hands were crossed in front of her, her lips slightly parted, and her large, dark eyes wandered from face to face.</content>
<content>Her hair, done low on her neck, seemed to gleam above her black collar like coils of shining metal.</content>
<content>And as Soames stood looking at her, the sensation that most men have felt at one time or another went stealing through him - a peculiar satisfaction of the senses, a peculiar certainty, which novelists and old ladies call love at first sight.</content>
<content>Still stealthily watching her, he at once made his way to his hostess, and stood doggedly waiting for the music to cease.</content>
<content>"Who is that girl with yellow hair and dark eyes?"</content>
<content>he asked.</content>
<content>"That - oh!</content>
<content>Irene Heron.</content>
<content>Her father, Professor Heron, died this year.</content>
<content>She lives with her stepmother.</content>
<content>She's a nice girl, a pretty girl, but no money!"</content>
<content>"Introduce me, please," said Soames.</content>
<content>It was very little that he found to say, nor did he find her responsive to that little.</content>
<content>But he went away with the resolution to see her again.</content>
<content>He effected his object by chance, meeting her on the pier with her stepmother, who had the habit of walking there from twelve to one of a forenoon.</content>
<content>Soames made this lady's acquaintance with alacrity, nor was it long before he perceived in her the ally he was looking for.</content>
<content>His keen scent for the commercial side of family life soon told him that Irene cost her stepmother more than the fifty pounds a year she brought her; it also told him that Mrs. Heron, a woman yet in the prime of life, desired to be married again.</content>
<content>The strange ripening beauty of her stepdaughter stood in the way of this desirable consummation.</content>
<content>And Soames, in his stealthy tenacity, laid his plans.</content>
<content>He left Bournemouth without having given himself away, but in a month's time came back, and this time he spoke, not to the girl, but to her stepmother.</content>
<content>He had made up his mind, he said; he would wait any time.</content>
<content>And he had long to wait, watching Irene bloom, the lines of her young figure softening, the stronger blood deepening the gleam of her eyes, and warming her face to a creamy glow; and at each visit he proposed to her, and when that visit was at an end, took her refusal away with him, back to London, sore at heart, but steadfast and silent as the grave.</content>
<content>He tried to come at the secret springs of her resistance; only once had he a gleam of light.</content>
<content>It was at one of those assembly dances, which afford the only outlet to the passions of the population of seaside watering-places.</content>
<content>He was sitting with her in an embrasure, his senses tingling with the contact of the waltz.</content>
<content>She had looked at him over her, slowly waving fan; and he had lost his head.</content>
<content>Seizing that moving wrist, he pressed his lips to the flesh of her arm.</content>
<content>And she had shuddered - to this day he had not forgotten that shudder - nor the look so passionately averse she had given him.</content>
<content>A year after that she had yielded.</content>
<content>What had made her yield he could never make out; and from Mrs. Heron, a woman of some diplomatic talent, he learnt nothing.</content>
<content>Once after they were married he asked her, "What made you refuse me so often?"</content>
<content>She had answered by a strange silence.</content>
<content>An enigma to him from the day that he first saw her, she was an enigma to him still....
Bosinney was waiting for him at the door; and on his rugged, good-looking, face was a queer, yearning, yet happy look, as though he too saw a promise of bliss in the spring sky, sniffed a coming happiness in the spring air.</content>
<content>Soames looked at him waiting there.</content>
<content>What was the matter with the fellow that he looked so happy?</content>
<content>What was he waiting for with that smile on his lips and in his eyes?</content>
<content>Soames could not see that for which Bosinney was waiting as he stood there drinking in the flower-scented wind.</content>
<content>And once more he felt baffled in the presence of this man whom by habit he despised.</content>
<content>He hastened on to the house.</content>
<content>"The only colour for those tiles," he heard Bosinney say, - "is ruby with a grey tint in the stuff, to give a transparent effect.</content>
<content>I should like Irene's opinion.</content>
<content>I'm ordering the purple leather curtains for the doorway of this court; and if you distemper the drawing-room ivory cream over paper, you'll get an illusive look.</content>
<content>You want to aim all through the decorations at what I call charm."</content>
<content>Soames said: "You mean that my wife has charm!"</content>
<content>Bosinney evaded the question.</content>
<content>"You should have a clump of iris plants in the centre of that court."</content>
<content>Soames smiled superciliously.</content>
<content>"I'll look into Beech's some time," he said, "and see what's appropriate!"</content>
<content>They found little else to say to each other, but on the way to the Station Soames asked:
"I suppose you find Irene very artistic."</content>
<content>"Yes."</content>
<content>The abrupt answer was as distinct a snub as saying: "If you want to discuss her you can do it with someone else!"</content>
<content>And the slow, sulky anger Soames had felt all the afternoon burned the brighter within him.</content>
<content>Neither spoke again till they were close to the Station, then Soames asked:

<content>"By the end of June, if you really wish me to decorate as well."</content>
<content>Soames nodded.</content>
<content>"But you quite understand," he said, "that the house is costing me a lot beyond what I contemplated.</content>
<content>I may as well tell you that I should have thrown it up, only I'm not in the habit of giving up what I've  set my mind on."</content>
<content>Bosinney made no reply.</content>
<content>And Soames gave him askance a look of dogged dislike - for in spite of his fastidious air and that supercilious, dandified taciturnity, Soames, with his set lips and squared chin, was not unlike a bulldog....</content>
<content>When, at seven o'clock that evening, June arrived at 62, Montpellier Square, the maid Bilson told her that Mr. Bosinney was in the drawing-room; the mistress - she said - was dressing, and would be down in a minute.</content>
<content>She would tell her that Miss June was here.</content>
<content>June stopped her at once.</content>
<content>"All right, Bilson," she said, "I'll just go in.</content>
<content>You, needn't  hurry Mrs.</content>
<content>Soames."</content>

<content>June paused for a moment to look at herself in the little old-fashioned silver mirror above the oaken rug chest - a slim, imperious young figure, with a small resolute face, in a white frock, cut moon-shaped at the base of a neck too slender for her crown of twisted red-gold hair.</content>
<content>She opened the drawing-room door softly, meaning to take him by surprise.</content>
<content>The room was filled with a sweet hot scent of flowering azaleas.</content>
<content>She took a long breath of the perfume, and heard Bosinney's voice, not in the room, but quite close, saying.</content>
<content>"Ah!</content>
<content>there were such heaps of things I wanted to talk about, and now we shan't  have time!"</content>
<content>Irene's voice answered: "Why not at dinner?"</content>
<content>"How can one talk...."

<content>It was from there that the scent of the azaleas came, and, standing with their backs to her, their faces buried in the golden-pink blossoms, stood her lover and Irene.</content>

<content>A wave of the azalea scent drifted into June's face; she felt sick and dizzy.</content>
<content>"Do!</content>
<content>ah!</content>
<content>do!"</content>
<content>"But why?"</content>
<content>"I must see you there - I thought you'd like to help me...."

<content>And she stepped into the open space of the window.</content>
<content>"How stuffy it is here!"</content>
<content>she said; "I can't  bear this scent!"</content>
<content>Her eyes, so angry and direct, swept both their faces.</content>
<content>"Were you talking about the house?</content>
<content>I haven't  seen it yet, you know - shall we all go on Sunday?"'</content>
<content>From Irene's face the colour had flown.</content>
<content>"I am going for a drive that day with Uncle Swithin," she answered.</content>
<content>"Uncle Swithin!</content>
<content>What does he matter?</content>
<content>You can throw him over!"</content>
<content>"I am not in the habit of throwing people over!"</content>
<content>There was a sound of footsteps and June saw Soames standing just behind her.</content>
<content>"Well!</content>
<content>if you are all ready," said Irene, looking from one to the other with a strange smile, "dinner is too!"</content>
<content>CHAPTER II - JUNE'S TREAT
Dinner began in silence; the women facing one another, and the men.</content>
<content>In silence the soup was finished - excellent, if a little thick; and fish was brought.</content>
<content>In silence it was handed.</content>
<content>Bosinney ventured: "It's the first spring day."</content>
<content>Irene echoed softly: "Yes - the first spring day."</content>
<content>"Spring!"</content>
<content>said June: "there isn't  a breath of air!"</content>
<content>No one replied.</content>
<content>The fish was taken away, a fine fresh sole from Dover.</content>
<content>And Bilson brought champagne, a bottle swathed around the neck with white....
Soames said: "You'll find it dry."</content>
<content>Cutlets were handed, each pink-frilled about the legs.</content>
<content>They were refused by June, and silence fell.</content>
<content>Soames said: "You'd better take a cutlet, June; there's nothing coming."</content>
<content>But June again refused, so they were borne away.</content>
<content>And then Irene asked: "Phil, have you heard my blackbird?"</content>
<content>Bosinney answered: "Rather - he's got a hunting-song.</content>
<content>As I came round I heard him in the Square."</content>
<content>"He's such a darling!"</content>
<content>"Salad, sir?"</content>
<content>Spring chicken was removed.</content>
<content>But Soames was speaking: "The asparagus is very poor.</content>
<content>Bosinney, glass of sherry with your sweet?</content>
<content>June, you're drinking nothing!"</content>
<content>June said: "You know I never do.</content>
<content>Wine's such horrid stuff!"</content>
<content>An apple charlotte came upon a silver dish, and smilingly Irene said: "The azaleas are so wonderful this year!"</content>
<content>To this Bosinney murmured: "Wonderful!</content>
<content>The scent's extraordinary!"</content>
<content>June said: "How can you like the scent?</content>
<content>Sugar, please, Bilson."</content>
<content>Sugar was handed her, and Soames remarked: "This charlottes good!"</content>
<content>The charlotte was removed.</content>
<content>Long silence followed.</content>
<content>Irene, beckoning, said: "Take out the azalea, Bilson.</content>
<content>Miss June can't  bear the scent."</content>
<content>"No; let it stay," said June.</content>
<content>Olives from France, with Russian caviare, were placed on little plates.</content>
<content>And Soames remarked: "Why can't  we have the Spanish?"</content>
<content>But no one answered.</content>
<content>The olives were removed.</content>
<content>Lifting her tumbler June demanded: "Give me some water, please."</content>
<content>Water was given her.</content>
<content>A silver tray was brought, with German plums.</content>
<content>There was a lengthy pause.</content>
<content>In perfect harmony all were eating them.</content>
<content>Bosinney counted up the stones: "This year - next year - some time."</content>
<content>Irene finished softly: "Never!</content>
<content>There was such a glorious sunset.</content>
<content>The sky's all ruby still - so beautiful!"</content>
<content>He answered: "Underneath the dark."</content>
<content>Their eyes had met, and June cried scornfully: "A London sunset!"</content>
<content>Egyptian cigarettes were handed in a silver box.</content>
<content>Soames, taking one, remarked: "What time's your play begin?"</content>
<content>No one replied, and Turkish coffee followed in enamelled cups.</content>
<content>Irene, smiling quietly, said: "If only...."
"Only what?"</content>
<content>said June.</content>
<content>"If only it could always be the spring!"</content>
<content>Brandy was handed; it was pale and old.</content>
<content>Soames said: "Bosinney, better take some brandy."</content>
<content>Bosinney took a glass; they all arose.</content>
<content>"You want a cab?"</content>
<content>asked Soames.</content>
<content>June answered: "No!</content>
<content>My cloaks please, Bilson."</content>
<content>Her cloak was brought.</content>
<content>Irene, from the window, murmured: "Such a lovely night!</content>
<content>The stars are coming out!"</content>
<content>Soames added: "Well, I hope you'll both enjoy yourselves."</content>
<content>From the door June answered: "Thanks.</content>
<content>Come, Phil."</content>
<content>Bosinney cried: "I'm coming."</content>
<content>Soames smiled a sneering smile, and said: "I wish you luck!"</content>
<content>And at the door Irene watched them go.</content>
<content>Bosinney called: "Good night!"</content>
<content>"Good night!"</content>
<content>she answered softly....
June made her lover take her on the top of a 'bus, saying she wanted air, and there sat silent, with her face to the breeze.</content>
<content>The driver turned once or twice, with the intention of venturing a remark, but thought better of it.</content>
<content>They were a lively couple!</content>
<content>The spring had got into his blood, too; he felt the need for letting steam escape, and clucked his tongue, flourishing his whip, wheeling his horses, and even they, poor things, had smelled the spring, and for a brief half-hour spurned the pavement with happy hoofs.</content>
<content>The whole town was alive; the boughs, curled upward with their decking of young leaves, awaited some gift the breeze could bring.</content>
<content>New-lighted lamps were gaining mastery, and the faces of the crowd showed pale under that glare, while on high the great white clouds slid swiftly, softly, over the purple sky.</content>
<content>Men in, evening dress had thrown back overcoats, stepping jauntily up the steps of Clubs; working folk loitered; and women - those women who at that time of night are solitary - solitary and moving eastward in a stream - swung slowly along, with expectation in their gait, dreaming of good wine and a good supper, or - for an unwonted minute, of kisses given for love.</content>
<content>Those countless figures, going their ways under the lamps and the moving-sky, had one and all received some restless blessing from the stir of spring.</content>
<content>And one and all, like those clubmen with their opened coats, had shed something of caste, and creed, and custom, and by the cock of their hats, the pace of their walk, their laughter, or their silence, revealed their common kinship under the passionate heavens.</content>
<content>Bosinney and June entered the theatre in silence, and mounted to their seats in the upper boxes.</content>
<content>The piece had just begun, and the half-darkened house, with its rows of creatures peering all one way, resembled a great garden of flowers turning their faces to the sun.</content>
<content>June had never before been in the upper boxes.</content>
<content>From the age of fifteen she had habitually accompanied her grandfather to the stalls, and not common stalls, but the best seats in the house, towards the centre of the third row, booked by old Jolyon, at Grogan and Boyne's, on his way home from the City, long before the day; carried in his overcoat pocket, together with his cigar-case and his old kid gloves, and handed to June to keep till the appointed night.</content>
<content>And in those stalls - an erect old figure with a serene white head, a little figure, strenuous and eager, with a red-gold head - they would sit through every kind of play, and on the way home old Jolyon would say of the principal actor: "Oh, he's a poor stick!</content>
<content>You should have seen little Bobson!"</content>
<content>She had looked forward to this evening with keen delight; it was stolen, chaperone-less, undreamed of at Stanhope Gate, where she was supposed to be at Soames'.</content>
<content>She had expected reward for her subterfuge, planned for her lover's sake; she had expected it to break up the thick, chilly cloud, and make the relations between them which of late had been so puzzling, so tormenting - sunny and simple again as they had been before the winter.</content>
<content>She had come with the intention of saying something definite; and she looked at the stage with a furrow between her brows, seeing nothing, her hands squeezed together in her lap.</content>
<content>A swarm of jealous suspicions stung and stung her.</content>
<content>If Bosinney was conscious of her trouble he made no sign.</content>
<content>The curtain dropped.</content>
<content>The first act had come to an end.</content>
<content>"It's awfully hot here!"</content>
<content>said the girl; "I should like to go out."</content>
<content>She was very white, and she knew - for with her nerves thus sharpened she saw everything - that he was both uneasy and compunctious.</content>
<content>At the back of the theatre an open balcony hung over the street; she took possession of this, and stood leaning there without a word, waiting for him to begin.</content>
<content>At last she could bear it no longer.</content>
<content>"I want to say something to you, Phil," she said.</content>
<content>"Yes?"</content>
<content>The defensive tone of his voice brought the colour flying to her cheek, the words flying to her lips: "You don't  give me a chance to be nice to you; you haven't  for ages now!"</content>
<content>Bosinney stared down at the street.</content>
<content>He made no answer....
June cried passionately: "You know I want to do everything for you - that I want to be everything to you...."
A hum rose from the street, and, piercing it with a sharp 'ping,' the bell sounded for the raising of the curtain.</content>
<content>June did not stir.</content>
<content>A desperate struggle was going on within her.</content>
<content>Should she put everything to the proof?</content>
<content>Should she challenge directly that influence, that attraction which was driving him away from her?</content>
<content>It was her nature to challenge, and she said: "Phil, take me to see the house on Sunday!"</content>
<content>With a smile quivering and breaking on her lips, and trying, how hard, not to show that she was watching, she searched his face, saw it waver and hesitate, saw a troubled line come between his brows, the blood rush into his face.</content>
<content>He answered: "Not Sunday, dear; some other day!"</content>
<content>"Why not Sunday?</content>
<content>I shouldn't  be in the way on Sunday."</content>
<content>He made an evident effort, and said: "I have an engagement."</content>
<content>"You are going to take...."
His eyes grew angry; he shrugged his shoulders, and answered: "An engagement that will prevent my taking you to see the house!"</content>
<content>June bit her lip till the blood came, and walked back to her seat without another word, but she could not help the tears of rage rolling down her face.</content>
<content>The house had been mercifully darkened for a crisis, and no one could see her trouble.</content>
<content>Yet in this world of Forsytes let no man think himself immune from observation.</content>
<content>In the third row behind, Euphemia, Nicholas's youngest daughter, with her married-sister, Mrs. Tweetyman, were watching.</content>
<content>They reported at Timothy's, how they had seen June and her fiance at the theatre.</content>
<content>"In the stalls?"</content>
<content>"No, not in the...." "Oh!</content>
<content>in the dress circle, of course.</content>
<content>That seemed to be quite fashionable nowadays with young people!"</content>
<content>Well - not exactly.</content>
<content>In the....</content>
<content>Anyway, that engagement wouldn't  last long.</content>
<content>They had never seen anyone look so thunder and lightningy as that little June!</content>
<content>With tears of enjoyment in their eyes, they related how she had kicked a man's hat as she returned to her seat in the middle of an act, and how the man had looked.</content>
<content>Euphemia had a noted, silent laugh, terminating most disappointingly in squeaks; and when Mrs. Small, holding up her hands, said: "My dear!</content>

<content>"Come in and have your milk," he said.</content>
<content>"It's been kept hot for you.</content>
<content>You're very late.</content>

<content>June stood at the fireplace, with a foot on the fender and an arm on the mantelpiece, as her grandfather had done when he came in that night of the opera.</content>

<content>"We dined at Soames's."</content>

<content>When the door was closed, old Jolyon dropped his paper, and stared long and anxiously in front of him.</content>

<content>And upstairs in her room June sat at her open window, where the spring wind came, after its revel across the Park, to cool her hot cheeks and burn her heart.</content>
<content>CHAPTER III - DRIVE WITH SWITHIN
Two lines of a certain song in a certain famous old school's songbook run as follows:
'How the buttons on his blue frock shone, tra-la-la!</content>
<content>How he carolled and he sang, like a bird!...'</content>
<content>Swithin did not exactly carol and sing like a bird, but he felt almost like endeavouring to hum a tune, as he stepped out of Hyde Park Mansions, and contemplated his horses drawn up before the door.</content>
<content>The afternoon was as balmy as a day in June, and to complete the simile of the old song, he had put on a blue frock-coat, dispensing with an overcoat, after sending Adolf down three times to make sure that there was not the least suspicion of east in the wind; and the frock-coat was buttoned so tightly around his personable form, that, if the buttons did not shine, they might pardonably have done so.</content>
<content>Majestic on the pavement he fitted on a pair of dog-skin gloves; with his large bell-shaped top hat, and his great stature and bulk he looked too primeval for a Forsyte.</content>
<content>His thick white hair, on which Adolf had bestowed a touch of pomatum, exhaled the fragrance of opoponax and cigars - the celebrated Swithin brand, for which he paid one hundred and forty shillings the hundred, and of which old Jolyon had unkindly said, he wouldn't  smoke them as a gift; they wanted the stomach of a horse!</content>
<content>"Adolf!"</content>
<content>"Sare!"</content>
<content>"The new plaid rug!"</content>
<content>He would never teach that fellow to look smart; and Mrs. Soames he felt sure, had an eye!</content>
<content>"The phaeton hood down; I am going - to - drive - a - lady!"</content>
<content>A pretty woman would want to show off her frock; and well - he was going to drive a lady!</content>
<content>It was like a new beginning to the good old days.</content>
<content>Ages since he had driven a woman!</content>
<content>The last time, if he remembered, it had been Juley; the poor old soul had been as nervous as a cat the whole time, and so put him out of patience that, as he dropped her in the Bayswater Road, he had said: "Well I'm d - -d if I ever drive you again!"</content>
<content>And he never had, not he!</content>
<content>Going up to his horses' heads, he examined their bits; not that he knew anything about bits - he didn't  pay his coachman sixty pounds a year to do his work for him, that had never been his principle.</content>
<content>Indeed, his reputation as a horsey man rested mainly on the fact that once, on Derby Day, he had been welshed by some thimble-riggers.</content>
<content>But someone at the Club, after seeing him drive his greys up to the door - he always drove grey horses, you got more style for the money, some thought - had called him 'Four-in-hand Forsyte.'</content>
<content>The name having reached his ears through that fellow Nicholas Treffry, old Jolyon's dead partner, the great driving man notorious for more carriage accidents than any man in the kingdom - Swithin had ever after conceived it right to act up to it.</content>
<content>The name had taken his fancy, not because he had ever driven four-in-hand, or was ever likely to, but because of something distinguished in the sound.</content>
<content>Four-in-hand Forsyte!</content>
<content>Not bad!</content>
<content>Born too soon, Swithin had missed his vocation.</content>
<content>Coming upon London twenty years later, he could not have failed to have become a stockbroker, but at the time when he was obliged to select, this great profession had not as yet became the chief glory of the upper-middle class.</content>
<content>He had literally been forced into land agency.</content>
<content>Once in the driving seat, with the reins handed to him, and blinking over his pale old cheeks in the full sunlight, he took a slow look round - Adolf was already up behind; the cockaded groom at the horses' heads stood ready to let go; everything was prepared for the signal, and Swithin gave it.</content>
<content>The equipage dashed forward, and before you could say Jack Robinson, with a rattle and flourish drew up at Soames' door.</content>
<content>Irene came out at once, and stepped in - he afterward described it at Timothy's - "as light as - er - Taglioni, no fuss about it, no wanting this or wanting that;" and above all, Swithin dwelt on this, staring at Mrs. Septimus in a way that disconcerted her a good deal, "no silly nervousness!"</content>
<content>To Aunt Hester he portrayed Irene's hat.</content>
<content>"Not one of your great flopping things, sprawling about, and catching the dust, that women are so fond of nowadays, but a neat little - " he made a circular motion of his hand, "white veil - capital taste."</content>
<content>"What was it made of?"</content>
<content>inquired Aunt Hester, who manifested a languid but permanent excitement at any mention of dress.</content>
<content>"Made of?"</content>
<content>returned Swithin; "now how should I know?"</content>
<content>He sank into silence so profound that Aunt Hester began to be afraid he had fallen into a trance.</content>
<content>She did not try to rouse him herself, it not being her custom.</content>
<content>'I wish somebody would come,' she thought; 'I don't  like the look of him!'</content>
<content>But suddenly Swithin returned to life.</content>
<content>"Made of" he wheezed out slowly, "what should it be made of?"</content>
<content>They had not gone four miles before Swithin received the impression that Irene liked driving with him.</content>
<content>Her face was so soft behind that white veil, and her dark eyes shone so in the spring light, and whenever he spoke she raised them to him and smiled.</content>
<content>On Saturday morning Soames had found her at her writing-table with a note written to Swithin, putting him off.</content>
<content>Why did she want to put him off?</content>
<content>he asked.</content>

<content>CHAPTER IV - JAMES GOES TO SEE FOR HIMSELF
Those ignorant of Forsyte 'Change would not, perhaps, foresee all the stir made by Irene's visit to the house.</content>
<content>After Swithin had related at Timothy's the full story of his memorable drive, the same, with the least suspicion of curiosity, the merest touch of malice, and a real desire to do good, was passed on to June.</content>
<content>"And what a dreadful thing to say, my dear!"</content>
<content>ended Aunt Juley; "that about not going home.</content>
<content>What did she mean?"</content>
<content>It was a strange recital for the girl.</content>
<content>She heard it flushing painfully, and, suddenly, with a curt handshake, took her departure.</content>
<content>"Almost rude!"</content>
<content>Mrs. Small said to Aunt Hester, when June was gone.</content>
<content>The proper construction was put on her reception of the news.</content>
<content>She was upset.</content>
<content>Something was therefore very wrong.</content>
<content>Odd!</content>
<content>She and Irene had been such friends!</content>
<content>It all tallied too well with whispers and hints that had been going about for some time past.</content>
<content>Recollections of Euphemia's account of the visit to the theatre - Mr. Bosinney always at Soames's?</content>
<content>Oh, indeed!</content>
<content>Yes, of course, he would be about the house!</content>
<content>Nothing open.</content>
<content>Only upon the greatest, the most important provocation was it necessary to say anything open on Forsyte 'Change.</content>
<content>This machine was too nicely adjusted; a hint, the merest trifling expression of regret or doubt, sufficed to set the family soul so sympathetic - vibrating.</content>
<content>No one desired that harm should come of these vibrations - far from it; they were set in motion with the best intentions, with the feeling, that each member of the family had a stake in the family soul.</content>
<content>And much kindness lay at the bottom of the gossip; it would frequently result in visits of condolence being made, in accordance with the customs of Society, thereby conferring a real benefit upon the sufferers, and affording consolation to the sound, who felt pleasantly that someone at all events was suffering from that from which they themselves were not suffering.</content>
<content>In fact, it was simply a desire to keep things well-aired, the desire which animates the Public Press, that brought James, for instance, into communication with Mrs. Septimus, Mrs. Septimus, with the little Nicholases, the little Nicholases with who-knows-whom, and so on.</content>
<content>That great class to which they had risen, and now belonged, demanded a certain candour, a still more certain reticence.</content>
<content>This combination guaranteed their membership.</content>
<content>Many of the younger Forsytes felt, very naturally, and would openly declare, that they did not want their affairs pried into; but so powerful was the invisible, magnetic current of family gossip, that for the life of them they could not help knowing all about everything.</content>
<content>It was felt to be hopeless.</content>
<content>One of them  young Roger  had made an heroic attempt to free the rising generation, by speaking of Timothy as an 'old cat.'</content>
<content>The effort had justly recoiled upon himself; the words, coming round in the most delicate way to Aunt Juley's ears, were repeated by her in a shocked voice to Mrs. Roger, whence they returned again to young Roger.</content>
<content>And, after all, it was only the wrong-doers who suffered; as, for instance, George, when he lost all that money playing billiards; or young Roger himself, when he was so dreadfully near to marrying the girl to whom, it was whispered, he was already married by the laws of Nature; or again Irene, who was thought, rather than said, to be in danger.</content>
<content>All this was not only pleasant but salutary.</content>
<content>And it made so many hours go lightly at Timothy's in the Bayswater Road; so many hours that must otherwise have been sterile and heavy to those three who lived there; and Timothy's was but one of hundreds of such homes in this City of London - the homes of neutral persons of the secure classes, who are out of the battle themselves, and must find their reason for existing, in the battles of others.</content>
<content>But for the sweetness of family gossip, it must indeed have been lonely there.</content>
<content>Rumours and tales, reports, surmises - were they not the children of the house, as dear and precious as the prattling babes the brother and sisters had missed in their own journey?</content>
<content>To talk about them was as near as they could get to the possession of all those children and grandchildren, after whom their soft hearts yearned.</content>
<content>For though it is doubtful whether Timothy's heart yearned, it is indubitable that at the arrival of each fresh Forsyte child he was quite upset.</content>
<content>Useless for young Roger to say, "Old cat!"</content>
<content>for Euphemia to hold up her hands and cry: "Oh!</content>
<content>those three!"</content>
<content>and break into her silent laugh with the squeak at the end.</content>
<content>Useless, and not too kind.</content>
<content>The situation which at this stage might seem, and especially to Forsyte eyes, strange - not to say 'impossible' - was, in view of certain facts, not so strange after all.</content>
<content>Some things had been lost sight of.</content>
<content>And first, in the security bred of many harmless marriages, it had been forgotten that Love is no hot-house flower, but a wild plant, born of a wet night, born of an hour of sunshine; sprung from wild seed, blown along the road by a wild wind.</content>
<content>A wild plant that, when it blooms by chance within the hedge of our gardens, we call a flower; and when it blooms outside we call a weed; but, flower or weed, whose scent and colour are always, wild!</content>
<content>And further - the facts and figures of their own lives being against the perception of this truth - it was not generally recognised by Forsytes that, where, this wild plant springs, men and women are but moths around the pale, flame-like blossom.</content>
<content>It was long since young Jolyon's escapade - there was danger of a tradition again arising that people in their position never cross the hedge to pluck that flower; that one could reckon on having love, like measles, once in due season, and getting over it comfortably for all time - as with measles, on a soothing mixture of butter and honey - in the arms of wedlock.</content>
<content>Of all those whom this strange rumour about Bosinney and Mrs. Soames reached, James was the most affected.</content>
<content>He had long forgotten how he had hovered, lanky and pale, in side whiskers of chestnut hue, round Emily, in the days of his own courtship.</content>
<content>He had long forgotten the small house in the purlieus of Mayfair, where he had spent the early days of his married life, or rather, he had long forgotten the early days, not the small house, - a Forsyte never forgot a house - he had afterwards sold it at a clear profit of four hundred pounds.</content>
<content>He had long forgotten those days, with their hopes and fears and doubts about the prudence of the match  for Emily, though pretty, had nothing, and he himself at that time was making a bare thousand a year , and that strange, irresistible attraction which had drawn him on, till he felt he must die if he could not marry the girl with the fair hair, looped so neatly back, the fair arms emerging from a skin-tight bodice, the fair form decorously shielded by a cage of really stupendous circumference.</content>
<content>James had passed through the fire, but he had passed also through the river of years which washes out the fire; he had experienced the saddest experience of all - forgetfulness of what it was like to be in love.</content>
<content>Forgotten!</content>
<content>Forgotten so long, that he had forgotten even that he had forgotten.</content>
<content>And now this rumour had come upon him, this rumour about his son's wife; very vague, a shadow dodging among the palpable, straightforward appearances of things, unreal, unintelligible as a ghost, but carrying with it, like a ghost, inexplicable terror.</content>
<content>He tried to bring it home to his mind, but it was no more use than trying to apply to himself one of those tragedies he read of daily in his evening paper.</content>
<content>He simply could not.</content>
<content>There could be nothing in it.</content>
<content>It was all their nonsense.</content>
<content>She didn't  get on with Soames as well as she might, but she was a good little thing - a good little thing!</content>
<content>Like the not inconsiderable majority of men, James relished a nice little bit of scandal, and would say, in a matter-of-fact tone, licking his lips, "Yes, yes - she and young Dyson; they tell me they're living at Monte Carlo!"</content>
<content>But the significance of an affair of this sort - of its past, its present, or its future - had never struck him.</content>
<content>What it meant, what torture and raptures had gone to its construction, what slow, overmastering fate had lurked within the facts, very naked, sometimes sordid, but generally spicy, presented to his gaze.</content>
<content>He was not in the habit of blaming, praising, drawing deductions, or generalizing at all about such things; he simply listened rather greedily, and repeated what he was told, finding considerable benefit from the practice, as from the consumption of a sherry and bitters before a meal.</content>
<content>Now, however, that such a thing - or rather the rumour, the breath of it - had come near him personally, he felt as in a fog, which filled his mouth full of a bad, thick flavour, and made it difficult to draw breath.</content>
<content>A scandal!</content>
<content>A possible scandal!</content>
<content>To repeat this word to himself thus was the only way in which he could focus or make it thinkable.</content>
<content>He had forgotten the sensations necessary for understanding the progress, fate, or meaning of any such business; he simply could no longer grasp the possibilities of people running any risk for the sake of passion.</content>
<content>Amongst all those persons of his acquaintance, who went into the City day after day and did their business there, whatever it was, and in their leisure moments bought shares, and houses, and ate dinners, and played games, as he was told, it would have seemed to him ridiculous to suppose that there were any who would run risks for the sake of anything so recondite, so figurative, as passion.</content>
<content>Passion!</content>
<content>He seemed, indeed, to have heard of it, and rules such as 'A young man and a young woman ought never to be trusted together' were fixed in his mind as the parallels of latitude are fixed on a map  for all Forsytes, when it comes to 'bed-rock' matters of fact, have quite a fine taste in realism ; but as to anything else - well, he could only appreciate it at all through the catch-word 'scandal.'</content>
<content>Ah!</content>
<content>but there was no truth in it - could not be.</content>
<content>He was not afraid; she was really a good little thing.</content>
<content>But there it was when you got a thing like that into your mind.</content>
<content>And James was of a nervous temperament - one of those men whom things will not leave alone, who suffer tortures from anticipation and indecision.</content>
<content>For fear of letting something slip that he might otherwise secure, he was physically unable to make up his mind until absolutely certain that, by not making it up, he would suffer loss.</content>
<content>In life, however, there were many occasions when the business of making up his mind did not even rest with himself, and this was one of them.</content>
<content>What could he do?</content>
<content>Talk it over with Soames?</content>
<content>That would only make matters worse.</content>
<content>And, after all, there was nothing in it, he felt sure.</content>
<content>It was all that house.</content>
<content>He had mistrusted the idea from the first.</content>
<content>What did Soames want to go into the country for?</content>
<content>And, if he must go spending a lot of money building himself a house, why not have a first-rate man, instead of this young Bosinney, whom nobody knew anything about?</content>
<content>He had told them how it would be.</content>
<content>And he had heard that the house was costing Soames a pretty penny beyond what he had reckoned on spending.</content>
<content>This fact, more than any other, brought home to James the real danger of the situation.</content>
<content>It was always like this with these 'artistic' chaps; a sensible man should have nothing to say to them.</content>
<content>He had warned Irene, too.</content>
<content>And see what had come of it!</content>
<content>And it suddenly sprang into James's mind that he ought to go and see for himself.</content>
<content>In the midst of that fog of uneasiness in which his mind was enveloped the notion that he could go and look at the house afforded him inexplicable satisfaction.</content>
<content>It may have been simply the decision to do something - more possibly the fact that he was going to look at a house - that gave him relief.</content>
<content>He felt that in staring at an edifice of bricks and mortar, of wood and stone, built by the suspected man himself, he would be looking into the heart of that rumour about Irene.</content>
<content>Without saying a word, therefore, to anyone, he took a hansom to the station and proceeded by train to Robin Hill; thence - there being no 'flies,' in accordance with the custom of the neighbourhood - he found himself obliged to walk.</content>
<content>He started slowly up the hill, his angular knees and high shoulders bent complainingly, his eyes fixed on his feet, yet, neat for all that, in his high hat and his frock-coat, on which was the speckless gloss imparted by perfect superintendence.</content>
<content>Emily saw to that; that is, she did not, of course, see to it - people of good position not seeing to each other's buttons, and Emily was of good position - but she saw that the butler saw to it.</content>
<content>He had to ask his way three times; on each occasion he repeated the directions given him, got the man to repeat them, then repeated them a second time, for he was naturally of a talkative disposition, and one could not be too careful in a new neighbourhood.</content>
<content>He kept assuring them that it was a new house he was looking for; it was only, however, when he was shown the roof through the trees that he could feel really satisfied that he had not been directed entirely wrong.</content>
<content>A heavy sky seemed to cover the world with the grey whiteness of a whitewashed ceiling.</content>
<content>There was no freshness or fragrance in the air.</content>
<content>On such a day even British workmen scarcely cared to do more then they were obliged, and moved about their business without the drone of talk which whiles away the pangs of labour.</content>
<content>Through spaces of the unfinished house, shirt-sleeved figures worked slowly, and sounds arose - spasmodic knockings, the scraping of metal, the sawing of wood, with the rumble of wheelbarrows along boards; now and again the foreman's dog, tethered by a string to an oaken beam, whimpered feebly, with a sound like the singing of a kettle.</content>
<content>The fresh-fitted window-panes, daubed each with a white patch in the centre, stared out at James like the eyes of a blind dog.</content>
<content>And the building chorus went on, strident and mirthless under the grey-white sky.</content>
<content>But the thrushes, hunting amongst the fresh-turned earth for worms, were silent quite.</content>
<content>James picked his way among the heaps of gravel - the drive was being laid - till he came opposite the porch.</content>
<content>Here he stopped and raised his eyes.</content>
<content>There was but little to see from this point of view, and that little he took in at once; but he stayed in this position many minutes, and who shall know of what he thought.</content>
<content>His china-blue eyes under white eyebrows that jutted out in little horns, never stirred; the long upper lip of his wide mouth, between the fine white whiskers, twitched once or twice; it was easy to see from that anxious rapt expression, whence Soames derived the handicapped look which sometimes came upon his face.</content>
<content>James might have been saying to himself: 'I don't  know - life's a tough job.'</content>
<content>In this position Bosinney surprised him.</content>
<content>James brought his eyes down from whatever bird's-nest they had been looking for in the sky to Bosinney's face, on which was a kind of humorous scorn.</content>
<content>"How do you do, Mr. Forsyte?</content>
<content>Come down to see for yourself?"</content>
<content>It was exactly what James, as we know, had come for, and he was made correspondingly uneasy.</content>
<content>He held out his hand, however, saying:
"How are you?"</content>
<content>without looking at Bosinney.</content>
<content>The latter made way for him with an ironical smile.</content>
<content>James scented something suspicious in this courtesy.</content>
<content>"I should like to walk round the outside first," he said, "and see what you've  been doing!"</content>
<content>A flagged terrace of rounded stones with a list of two or three inches to port had been laid round the south-east and south-west sides of the house, and ran with a bevelled edge into mould, which was in preparation for being turfed; along this terrace James led the way.</content>
<content>"Now what did this cost?"</content>
<content>he asked, when he saw the terrace extending round the corner.</content>
<content>"What should you think?"</content>
<content>inquired Bosinney.</content>
<content>"How should I know?"</content>
<content>replied James somewhat nonplussed; "two or three hundred, I dare say!"</content>
<content>"The exact sum!"</content>
<content>James gave him a sharp look, but the architect appeared unconscious, and he put the answer down to mishearing.</content>
<content>On arriving at the garden entrance, he stopped to look at the view.</content>
<content>"That ought to come down," he said, pointing to the oak-tree.</content>
<content>"You think so?</content>
<content>You think that with the tree there you don't  get enough view for your money."</content>
<content>Again James eyed him suspiciously - this young man had a peculiar way of putting things: "Well!"</content>
<content>he said, with a perplexed, nervous, emphasis, "I don't  see what you want with a tree."</content>
<content>"It shall come down to-morrow," said Bosinney.</content>
<content>James was alarmed.</content>
<content>"Oh," he said, "don't  go saying I said it was to come down!</content>
<content>I know nothing about it!"</content>
<content>"No?"</content>
<content>James went on in a fluster: "Why, what should I know about it?</content>
<content>It's nothing to do with me!</content>
<content>You do it on your own responsibility."</content>
<content>"You'll allow me to mention your name?"</content>
<content>James grew more and more alarmed: "I don't  know what you want mentioning my name for," he muttered; "you'd better leave the tree alone.</content>
<content>It's not your tree!"</content>
<content>He took out a silk handkerchief and wiped his brow.</content>
<content>They entered the house.</content>
<content>Like Swithin, James was impressed by the inner court-yard.</content>
<content>"You must have spent a douce of a lot of money here," he said, after staring at the columns and gallery for some time.</content>
<content>"Now, what did it cost to put up those columns?"</content>
<content>"I can't  tell you off-hand," thoughtfully answered Bosinney, "but I know it was a deuce of a lot!"</content>
<content>"I should think so," said James.</content>
<content>"I should...." He caught the architect's eye, and broke off.</content>
<content>And now, whenever he came to anything of which he desired to know the cost, he stifled that curiosity.</content>
<content>Bosinney appeared determined that he should see everything, and had not James been of too 'noticing' a nature, he would certainly have found himself going round the house a second time.</content>
<content>He seemed so anxious to be asked questions, too, that James felt he must be on his guard.</content>
<content>He began to suffer from his exertions, for, though wiry enough for a man of his long build, he was seventy-five years old.</content>
<content>He grew discouraged; he seemed no nearer to anything, had not obtained from his inspection any of the knowledge he had vaguely hoped for.</content>
<content>He had merely increased his dislike and mistrust of this young man, who had tired him out with his politeness, and in whose manner he now certainly detected mockery.</content>
<content>The fellow was sharper than he had thought, and better-looking than he had hoped.</content>
<content>He had a - a 'don't  care' appearance that James, to whom risk was the most intolerable thing in life, did not appreciate; a peculiar smile, too, coming when least expected; and very queer eyes.</content>
<content>He reminded James, as he said afterwards, of a hungry cat.</content>
<content>This was as near as he could get, in conversation with Emily, to a description of the peculiar exasperation, velvetiness, and mockery, of which Bosinney's manner had been composed.</content>
<content>At last, having seen all that was to be seen, he came out again at the door where he had gone in; and now, feeling that he was wasting time and strength and money, all for nothing, he took the courage of a Forsyte in both hands, and, looking sharply at Bosinney, said:
"I dare say you see a good deal of my daughter-in-law; now, what does she think of the house?</content>
<content>But she hasn't  seen it, I suppose?"</content>
<content>This he said, knowing all about Irene's visit not, of course, that there was anything in the visit, except that extraordinary remark she had made about 'not caring to get home' - and the story of how June had taken the news!</content>
<content>He had determined, by this way of putting the question, to give Bosinney a chance, as he said to himself.</content>
<content>The latter was long in answering, but kept his eyes with uncomfortable steadiness on James.</content>
<content>"She has seen the house, but I can't  tell you what she thinks of it."</content>
<content>Nervous and baffled, James was constitutionally prevented from letting the matter drop.</content>
<content>"Oh!"</content>
<content>he said, 'she has seen it?</content>
<content>Soames brought her down, I suppose?"</content>
<content>Bosinney smilingly replied: "Oh, no!"</content>
<content>"What, did she come down alone?"</content>
<content>"Oh, no!"</content>
<content>"Then - who brought her?"</content>
<content>"I really don't  know whether I ought to tell you who brought her."</content>
<content>To James, who knew that it was Swithin, this answer appeared incomprehensible.</content>
<content>"Why!"</content>
<content>he stammered, "you know that...." but he stopped, suddenly perceiving his danger.</content>
<content>"Well," he said, "if you don't  want to tell me I suppose you won't !</content>
<content>Nobody tells me anything."</content>
<content>Somewhat to his surprise Bosinney asked him a question.</content>
<content>"By the by," he said, "could you tell me if there are likely to be any more of you coming down?</content>
<content>I should like to be on the spot!"</content>
<content>"Any more?"</content>
<content>said James bewildered, "who should there be more?</content>
<content>I don't  know of any more.</content>
<content>Good-bye?"</content>
<content>Looking at the ground he held out his hand, crossed the palm of it with Bosinney's, and taking his umbrella just above the silk, walked away along the terrace.</content>
<content>Before he turned the corner he glanced back, and saw Bosinney following him slowly - 'slinking along the wall' as he put it to himself, 'like a great cat.'</content>
<content>He paid no attention when the young fellow raised his hat.</content>
<content>Outside the drive, and out of sight, he slackened his pace still more.</content>
<content>Very slowly, more bent than when he came, lean, hungry, and disheartened, he made his way back to the station.</content>
<content>The Buccaneer, watching him go so sadly home, felt sorry perhaps for his behaviour to the old man.</content>
<content>CHAPTER V - SOAMES AND BOSINNEY CORRESPOND
James said nothing to his son of this visit to the house; but, having occasion to go to Timothy's on morning on a matter connected with a drainage scheme which was being forced by the sanitary authorities on his brother, he mentioned it there.</content>
<content>It was not, he said, a bad house.</content>
<content>He could see that a good deal could be made of it.</content>
<content>The fellow was clever in his way, though what it was going to cost Soames before it was done with he didn't  know.</content>
<content>Euphemia Forsyte, who happened to be in the room - she had come round to borrow the Rev.</content>
<content>Mr. Scoles' last novel, 'Passion and Paregoric', which was having such a vogue - chimed in.</content>
<content>"I saw Irene yesterday at the Stores; she and Mr. Bosinney were having a nice little chat in the Groceries."</content>
<content>It was thus, simply, that she recorded a scene which had really made a deep and complicated impression on her.</content>
<content>She had been hurrying to the silk department of the Church and Commercial Stores - that Institution than which, with its admirable system, admitting only guaranteed persons on a basis of payment before delivery, no emporium can be more highly recommended to Forsytes - to match a piece of prunella silk for her mother, who was waiting in the carriage outside.</content>
<content>Passing through the Groceries her eye was unpleasantly attracted by the back view of a very beautiful figure.</content>
<content>It was so charmingly proportioned, so balanced, and so well clothed, that Euphemia's instinctive propriety was at once alarmed; such figures, she knew, by intuition rather than experience, were rarely connected with virtue - certainly never in her mind, for her own back was somewhat difficult to fit.</content>
<content>Her suspicions were fortunately confirmed.</content>
<content>A young man coming from the Drugs had snatched off his hat, and was accosting the lady with the unknown back.</content>
<content>It was then that she saw with whom she had to deal; the lady was undoubtedly Mrs. Soames, the young man Mr. Bosinney.</content>
<content>Concealing herself rapidly over the purchase of a box of Tunisian dates, for she was impatient of awkwardly meeting people with parcels in her hands, and at the busy time of the morning, she was quite unintentionally an interested observer of their little interview.</content>
<content>Mrs. Soames, usually somewhat pale, had a delightful colour in her cheeks; and Mr. Bosinney's manner was strange, though attractive  she thought him rather a distinguished-looking man, and George's name for him, 'The Buccaneer' - about which there was something romantic - quite charming .</content>
<content>He seemed to be pleading.</content>
<content>Indeed, they talked so earnestly - or, rather, he talked so earnestly, for Mrs. Soames did not say much - that they caused, inconsiderately, an eddy in the traffic.</content>
<content>One nice old General, going towards Cigars, was obliged to step quite out of the way, and chancing to look up and see Mrs. Soames' face, he actually took off his hat, the old fool!</content>
<content>So like a man!</content>
<content>But it was Mrs. Soames' eyes that worried Euphemia.</content>
<content>She never once looked at Mr. Bosinney until he moved on, and then she looked after him.</content>
<content>And, oh, that look!</content>
<content>On that look Euphemia had spent much anxious thought.</content>
<content>It is not too much to say that it had hurt her with its dark, lingering softness, for all the world as though the woman wanted to drag him back, and unsay something she had been saying.</content>
<content>Ah, well, she had had no time to go deeply into the matter just then, with that prunella silk on her hands; but she was 've ry intriguee' - very!</content>
<content>She had just nodded to Mrs. Soames, to show her that she had seen; and, as she confided, in talking it over afterwards, to her chum Francie  Roger's daughter , "Didn't  she look caught out just?..."</content>
<content>James, most averse at the first blush to accepting any news confirmatory of his own poignant suspicions, took her up at once.</content>
<content>"Oh" he said, "they'd be after wall-papers no doubt."</content>
<content>Euphemia smiled.</content>
<content>"In the Groceries?"</content>
<content>she said softly; and, taking 'Passion and Paregoric' from the table, added: "And so you'll lend me this, dear Auntie?</content>
<content>Good-bye!"</content>
<content>and went away.</content>
<content>James left almost immediately after; he was late as it was.</content>
<content>When he reached the office of Forsyte, Bustard and Forsyte, he found Soames, sitting in his revolving, chair, drawing up a defence.</content>
<content>The latter greeted his father with a curt good-morning, and, taking an envelope from his pocket, said:
"It may interest you to look through this."</content>
<content>James read as follows:
'309D, SLOANE STREET, May 15,
'DEAR FORSYTE,
'The construction of your house being now completed, my duties as architect have come to an end.</content>
<content>If I am to go on with the business of decoration, which at your request I undertook, I should like you to clearly understand that I must have a free hand.</content>
<content>'You never come down without suggesting something that goes counter to my scheme.</content>
<content>I have here three letters from you, each of which recommends an article I should never dream of putting in.</content>
<content>I had your father here yesterday afternoon, who made further valuable suggestions.</content>
<content>'Please make up your mind, therefore, whether you want me to decorate for you, or to retire which on the whole I should prefer to do.</content>
<content>'But understand that, if I decorate, I decorate alone, without interference of any sort.</content>
<content>If I do the thing, I will do it thoroughly, but I must have a free hand.</content>
<content>'Yours truly,
'PHILIP BOSINNEY.'</content>
<content>The exact and immediate cause of this letter cannot, of course, be told, though it is not improbable that Bosinney may have been moved by some sudden revolt against his position towards Soames - that eternal position of Art towards Property - which is so admirably summed up, on the back of the most indispensable of modern appliances, in a sentence comparable to the very finest in Tacitus:
THOS.</content>
<content>T. SORROW, Inventor.</content>
<content>BERT M. PADLAND, Proprietor.</content>
<content>"What are you going to say to him?"</content>
<content>James asked.</content>
<content>Soames did not even turn his head.</content>
<content>"I haven't  made up my mind," he said, and went on with his defence.</content>
<content>A client of his, having put some buildings on a piece of ground that did not belong to him, had been suddenly and most irritatingly warned to take them off again.</content>
<content>After carefully going into the facts, however, Soames had seen his way to advise that his client had what was known as a title by possession, and that, though undoubtedly the ground did not belong to him, he was entitled to keep it, and had better do so; and he was now following up this advice by taking steps to - as the sailors say - 'make it so.'</content>
<content>He had a distinct reputation for sound advice; people saying of him: "Go to young Forsyte - a long-headed fellow!"</content>
<content>and he prized this reputation highly.</content>
<content>His natural taciturnity was in his favour; nothing could be more calculated to give people, especially people with property  Soames had no other clients , the impression that he was a safe man.</content>
<content>And he was safe.</content>
<content>Tradition, habit, education, inherited aptitude, native caution, all joined to form a solid professional honesty, superior to temptation - from the very fact that it was built on an innate avoidance of risk.</content>
<content>How could he fall, when his soul abhorred circumstances which render a fall possible - a man cannot fall off the floor!</content>
<content>And those countless Forsytes, who, in the course of innumerable transactions concerned with property of all sorts  from wives to water rights , had occasion for the services of a safe man, found it both reposeful and profitable to confide in Soames.</content>
<content>That slight superciliousness of his, combined with an air of mousing amongst precedents, was in his favour too - a man would not be supercilious unless he knew!</content>
<content>He was really at the head of the business, for though James still came nearly every day to, see for himself, he did little now but sit in his chair, twist his legs, slightly confuse things already decided, and presently go away again, and the other partner, Bustard, was a poor thing, who did a great deal of work, but whose opinion was never taken.</content>
<content>So Soames went steadily on with his defence.</content>
<content>Yet it would be idle to say that his mind was at ease.</content>
<content>He was suffering from a sense of impending trouble, that had haunted him for some time past.</content>
<content>He tried to think it physical - a condition of his liver - but knew that it was not.</content>
<content>He looked at his watch.</content>
<content>In a quarter of an hour he was due at the General Meeting of the New Colliery Company - one of Uncle Jolyon's concerns; he should see Uncle Jolyon there, and say something to him about Bosinney - he had not made up his mind what, but something - in any case he should not answer this letter until he had seen Uncle Jolyon.</content>
<content>He got up and methodically put away the draft of his defence.</content>
<content>Going into a dark little cupboard, he turned up the light, washed his hands with a piece of brown Windsor soap, and dried them on a roller towel.</content>
<content>Then he brushed his hair, paying strict attention to the parting, turned down the light, took his hat, and saying he would be back at half-past two, stepped into the Poultry.</content>
<content>It was not far to the Offices of the New Colliery Company in Ironmonger Lane, where, and not at the Cannon Street Hotel, in accordance with the more ambitious practice of other companies, the General Meeting was always held.</content>
<content>Old Jolyon had from the first set his face against the Press.</content>
<content>What business - he said - had the Public with his concerns!</content>
<content>Soames arrived on the stroke of time, and took his seat alongside the Board, who, in a row, each Director behind his own ink-pot, faced their Shareholders.</content>
<content>In the centre of this row old Jolyon, conspicuous in his black, tightly-buttoned frock-coat and his white moustaches, was leaning back with finger tips crossed on a copy of the Directors' report and accounts.</content>
<content>On his right hand, always a little larger than life, sat the Secretary, 'Down-by-the-starn' Hemmings; an all-too-sad sadness beaming in his fine eyes; his iron-grey beard, in mourning like the rest of him, giving the feeling of an all-too-black tie behind it.</content>
<content>The occasion indeed was a melancholy one, only six weeks having elapsed since that telegram had come from Scorrier, the mining expert, on a private mission to the Mines, informing them that Pippin, their Superintendent, had committed suicide in endeavouring, after his extraordinary two years' silence, to write a letter to his Board.</content>
<content>That letter was on the table now; it would be read to the Shareholders, who would of course be put into possession of all the facts.</content>
<content>Hemmings had often said to Soames, standing with his coat-tails divided before the fireplace:
"What our Shareholders don't  know about our affairs isn't  worth knowing.</content>
<content>You may take that from me, Mr.</content>
<content>Soames."</content>
<content>On one occasion, old Jolyon being present, Soames recollected a little unpleasantness.</content>
<content>His uncle had looked up sharply and said: "Don't  talk nonsense, Hemmings!</content>
<content>You mean that what they do know isn't  worth knowing!"</content>
<content>Old Jolyon detested humbug.</content>
<content>Hemmings, angry-eyed, and wearing a smile like that of a trained poodle, had replied in an outburst of artificial applause: "Come, now, that's good, sir - that's very good.</content>
<content>Your uncle will have his joke!"</content>
<content>The next time he had seen Soames he had taken the opportunity of saying to him: "The chairman's getting very old!</content>
<content>- I can't  get him to understand things; and he's so wilful - but what can you expect, with a chin like his?"</content>
<content>Soames had nodded.</content>
<content>Everyone knew that Uncle Jolyon's chin was a caution.</content>
<content>He was looking worried to-day, in spite of his General Meeting look; he  Soames  should certainly speak to him about Bosinney.</content>
<content>Beyond old Jolyon on the left was little Mr. Booker, and he, too, wore his General Meeting look, as though searching for some particularly tender shareholder.</content>
<content>And next him was the deaf director, with a frown; and beyond the deaf director, again, was old Mr. Bleedham, very bland, and having an air of conscious virtue - as well he might, knowing that the brown-paper parcel he always brought to the Board-room was concealed behind his hat  one of that old-fashioned class, of flat-brimmed top-hats which go with very large bow ties, clean-shaven lips, fresh cheeks, and neat little, white whiskers .</content>
<content>Soames always attended the General Meeting; it was considered better that he should do so, in case 'anything should arise!'</content>
<content>He glanced round with his close, supercilious air at the walls of the room, where hung plans of the mine and harbour, together with a large photograph of a shaft leading to a working which had proved quite remarkably unprofitable.</content>
<content>This photograph - a witness to the eternal irony underlying commercial enterprise till retained its position on the - wall, an effigy of the directors' pet, but dead, lamb.</content>
<content>And now old Jolyon rose, to present the report and accounts.</content>
<content>Veiling under a Jove-like serenity that perpetual antagonism deep-seated in the bosom of a director towards his shareholders, he faced them calmly.</content>
<content>Soames faced them too.</content>
<content>He knew most of them by sight.</content>
<content>There was old Scrubsole, a tar man, who always came, as Hemmings would say, 'to make himself nasty,' a cantankerous-looking old fellow with a red face, a jowl, and an enormous low-crowned hat reposing on his knee.</content>
<content>And the Rev.</content>
<content>Mr. Boms, who always proposed a vote of thanks to the chairman, in which he invariably expressed the hope that the Board would not forget to elevate their employees, using the word with a double e, as being more vigorous and Anglo-Saxon  he had the strong Imperialistic tendencies of his cloth .</content>
<content>It was his salutary custom to buttonhole a director afterwards, and ask him whether he thought the coming year would be good or bad; and, according to the trend of the answer, to buy or sell three shares within the ensuing fortnight.</content>
<content>And there was that military man, Major O'Bally, who could not help speaking, if only to second the re-election of the auditor, and who sometimes caused serious consternation by taking toasts - proposals rather - out of the hands of persons who had been flattered with little slips of paper, entrusting the said proposals to their care.</content>
<content>These made up the lot, together with four or five strong, silent shareholders, with whom Soames could sympathize - men of business, who liked to keep an eye on their affairs for themselves, without being fussy - good, solid men, who came to the City every day and went back in the evening to good, solid wives.</content>
<content>Good, solid wives!</content>
<content>There was something in that thought which roused the nameless uneasiness in Soames again.</content>
<content>What should he say to his uncle?</content>
<content>What answer should he make to this letter?</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>"If any shareholder has any question to put, I shall be glad to answer it."</content>
<content>A soft thump.</content>
<content>Old Jolyon had let the report and accounts fall, and stood twisting his tortoise-shell glasses between thumb and forefinger.</content>
<content>The ghost of a smile appeared on Soames' face.</content>
<content>They had better hurry up with their questions!</content>
<content>He well knew his uncle's method  the ideal one  of at once saying: "I propose, then, that the report and accounts be adopted!"</content>
<content>Never let them get their wind - shareholders were notoriously wasteful of time!</content>
<content>A tall, white-bearded man, with a gaunt, dissatisfied face, arose:
"I believe I am in order, Mr. Chairman, in raising a question on this figure of L5000 in the accounts.</content>
<content>'To the widow and family"'  he looked sourly round , "'of our late superintendent,' who so - er - ill-advisedly  I say - ill-advisedly  committed suicide, at a time when his services were of the utmost value to this Company.</content>
<content>You have stated that the agreement which he has so unfortunately cut short with his own hand was for a period of five years, of which one only had expired - I - "
Old Jolyon made a gesture of impatience.</content>
<content>"I believe I am in order, Mr. Chairman - I ask whether this amount paid, or proposed to be paid, by the Board to the er - deceased - is for services which might have been rendered to the Company - had he not committed suicide?"</content>
<content>"It is in recognition of past services, which we all know - you as well as any of us - to have been of vital value."</content>
<content>"Then, sir, all I have to say is that the services being past, the amount is too much."</content>
<content>The shareholder sat down.</content>
<content>Old Jolyon waited a second and said: "I now propose that the report and - "
The shareholder rose again: "May I ask if the Board realizes that it is not their money which - I don't  hesitate to say that if it were their money...."
A second shareholder, with a round, dogged face, whom Soames recognised as the late superintendent's brother-in-law, got up and said warmly: "In my opinion, sir, the sum is not enough!"</content>
<content>The Rev.</content>
<content>Mr. Boms now rose to his feet.</content>
<content>"If I may venture to express myself," he said, "I should say that the fact of the - er - deceased having committed suicide should weigh very heavily - very heavily with our worthy chairman.</content>
<content>I have no doubt it has weighed with him, for - I say this for myself and I think for everyone present  hear, hear  - he enjoys our confidence in a high degree.</content>
<content>We all desire, I should hope, to be charitable.</content>
<content>But I feel sure"  he-looked severely at the late superintendent's brother-in-law  "that he will in some way, by some written expression, or better perhaps by reducing the amount, record our grave disapproval that so promising and valuable a life should have been thus impiously removed from a sphere where both its own interests and - if I may say so - our interests so imperatively demanded its continuance.</content>
<content>We should not - nay, we may not - countenance so grave a dereliction of all duty, both human and divine."</content>
<content>The reverend gentleman resumed his seat.</content>
<content>The late superintendent's brother-in-law again rose: "What I have said I stick to," he said; "the amount is not enough!"</content>
<content>The first shareholder struck in: "I challenge the legality of the payment.</content>
<content>In my opinion this payment is not legal.</content>
<content>The Company's solicitor is present; I believe I am in order in asking him the question."</content>
<content>All eyes were now turned upon Soames.</content>
<content>Something had arisen!</content>
<content>He stood up, close-lipped and cold; his nerves inwardly fluttered, his attention tweaked away at last from contemplation of that cloud looming on the horizon of his mind.</content>
<content>"The point," he said in a low, thin voice, "is by no means clear.</content>
<content>As there is no possibility of future consideration being received, it is doubtful whether the payment is strictly legal.</content>
<content>If it is desired, the opinion of the court could be taken."</content>
<content>The superintendent's brother-in-law frowned, and said in a meaning tone: "We have no doubt the opinion of the court could be taken.</content>
<content>May I ask the name of the gentleman who has given us that striking piece of information?</content>
<content>Mr. Soames Forsyte?</content>
<content>Indeed!"</content>
<content>He looked from Soames to old Jolyon in a pointed manner.</content>
<content>A flush coloured Soames' pale cheeks, but his superciliousness did not waver.</content>
<content>Old Jolyon fixed his eyes on the speaker.</content>
<content>"If," he said, "the late superintendents brother-in-law has nothing more to say, I propose that the report and accounts...."
At this moment, however, there rose one of those five silent, stolid shareholders, who had excited Soames' sympathy.</content>
<content>He said:
"I deprecate the proposal altogether.</content>
<content>We are expected to give charity to this man's wife and children, who, you tell us, were dependent on him.</content>
<content>They may have been; I do not care whether they were or not.</content>
<content>I object to the whole thing on principle.</content>
<content>It is high time a stand was made against this sentimental humanitarianism.</content>
<content>The country is eaten up with it.</content>
<content>I object to my money being paid to these people of whom I know nothing, who have done nothing to earn it.</content>
<content>I object in toto; it is not business.</content>
<content>I now move that the report and accounts be put back, and amended by striking out the grant altogether."</content>
<content>Old Jolyon had remained standing while the strong, silent man was speaking.</content>
<content>The speech awoke an echo in all hearts, voicing, as it did, the worship of strong men, the movement against generosity, which had at that time already commenced among the saner members of the community.</content>
<content>The words 'it is not business' had moved even the Board; privately everyone felt that indeed it was not.</content>
<content>But they knew also the chairman's domineering temper and tenacity.</content>
<content>He, too, at heart must feel that it was not business; but he was committed to his own proposition.</content>
<content>Would he go back upon it?</content>
<content>It was thought to be unlikely.</content>
<content>All waited with interest.</content>
<content>Old Jolyon held up his hand; dark-rimmed glasses depending between his finger and thumb quivered slightly with a suggestion of menace.</content>
<content>He addressed the strong, silent shareholder.</content>
<content>"Knowing, as you do, the efforts of our late superintendent upon the occasion of the explosion at the mines, do you seriously wish me to put that amendment, sir?"</content>
<content>"I do."</content>
<content>Old Jolyon put the amendment.</content>
<content>"Does anyone second this?"</content>
<content>he asked, looking calmly round.</content>
<content>And it was then that Soames, looking at his uncle, felt the power of will that was in that old man.</content>
<content>No one stirred.</content>
<content>Looking straight into the eyes of the strong, silent shareholder, old Jolyon said:
"I now move, 'That the report and accounts for the year 1886 be received and adopted.'</content>
<content>You second that?</content>
<content>Those in favour signify the same in the usual way.</content>
<content>Contrary - no.</content>
<content>Carried.</content>
<content>The next business, gentlemen...."
Soames smiled.</content>
<content>Certainly Uncle Jolyon had a way with him!</content>
<content>But now his attention relapsed upon Bosinney.</content>
<content>Odd how that fellow haunted his thoughts, even in business hours.</content>
<content>Irene's visit to the house - but there was nothing in that, except that she might have told him; but then, again, she never did tell him anything.</content>
<content>She was more silent, more touchy, every day.</content>
<content>He wished to God the house were finished, and they were in it, away from London.</content>
<content>Town did not suit her; her nerves were not strong enough.</content>
<content>That nonsense of the separate room had cropped up again!</content>
<content>The meeting was breaking up now.</content>
<content>Underneath the photograph of the lost shaft Hemmings was buttonholed by the Rev.</content>
<content>Mr. Boms.</content>
<content>Little Mr. Booker, his bristling eyebrows wreathed in angry smiles, was having a parting turn-up with old Scrubsole.</content>
<content>The two hated each other like poison.</content>
