<content>There was some matter of a tar-contract between them, little Mr. Booker having secured it from the Board for a nephew of his, over old Scrubsole's head.</content>
<content>Soames had heard that from Hemmings, who liked a gossip, more especially about his directors, except, indeed, old Jolyon, of whom he was afraid.</content>
<content>Soames awaited his opportunity.</content>
<content>The last shareholder was vanishing through the door, when he approached his uncle, who was putting on his hat.</content>
<content>"Can I speak to you for a minute, Uncle Jolyon?"</content>
<content>It is uncertain what Soames expected to get out of this interview.</content>
<content>Apart from that somewhat mysterious awe in which Forsytes in general held old Jolyon, due to his philosophic twist, or perhaps - as Hemmings would doubtless have said - to his chin, there was, and always had been, a subtle antagonism between the younger man and the old.</content>
<content>It had lurked under their dry manner of greeting, under their non-committal allusions to each other, and arose perhaps from old Jolyon's perception of the quiet tenacity  'obstinacy,' he rather naturally called it  of the young man, of a secret doubt whether he could get his own way with him.</content>
<content>Both these Forsytes, wide asunder as the poles in many respects, possessed in their different ways - to a greater degree than the rest of the family - that essential quality of tenacious and prudent insight into 'affairs,' which is the highwater mark of their great class.</content>
<content>Either of them, with a little luck and opportunity, was equal to a lofty career; either of them would have made a good financier, a great contractor, a statesman, though old Jolyon, in certain of his moods when under the influence of a cigar or of Nature - would have been capable of, not perhaps despising, but certainly of questioning, his own high position, while Soames, who never smoked cigars, would not.</content>
<content>Then, too, in old Jolyon's mind there was always the secret ache, that the son of James - of James, whom he had always thought such a poor thing, should be pursuing the paths of success, while his own son...!</content>
<content>And last, not least - for he was no more outside the radiation of family gossip than any other Forsyte - he had now heard the sinister, indefinite, but none the less disturbing rumour about Bosinney, and his pride was wounded to the quick.</content>
<content>Characteristically, his irritation turned not against Irene but against Soames.</content>
<content>The idea that his nephew's wife  why couldn't  the fellow take better care of her - Oh!</content>
<content>quaint injustice!</content>
<content>as though Soames could possibly take more care!</content>
<content>- should be drawing to herself June's lover, was intolerably humiliating.</content>
<content>And seeing the danger, he did not, like James, hide it away in sheer nervousness, but owned with the dispassion of his broader outlook, that it was not unlikely; there was something very attractive about Irene!</content>
<content>He had a presentiment on the subject of Soames' communication as they left the Board Room together, and went out into the noise and hurry of Cheapside.</content>
<content>They walked together a good minute without speaking, Soames with his mousing, mincing step, and old Jolyon upright and using his umbrella languidly as a walking-stick.</content>
<content>They turned presently into comparative quiet, for old Jolyon's way to a second Board led him in the direction of Moorage Street.</content>
<content>Then Soames, without lifting his eyes, began: "I've  had this letter from Bosinney.</content>
<content>You see what he says; I thought I'd let you know.</content>
<content>I've  spent a lot more than I intended on this house, and I want the position to be clear."</content>
<content>Old Jolyon ran his eyes unwillingly over the letter: "What he says is clear enough," he said.</content>
<content>"He talks about 'a free hand,'" replied Soames.</content>
<content>Old Jolyon looked at him.</content>
<content>The long-suppressed irritation and antagonism towards this young fellow, whose affairs were beginning to intrude upon his own, burst from him.</content>
<content>"Well, if you don't  trust him, why do you employ him?"</content>
<content>Soames stole a sideway look: "It's much too late to go into that," he said, "I only want it to be quite understood that if I give him a free hand, he doesn't  let me in.</content>
<content>I thought if you were to speak to him, it would carry more weight!"</content>
<content>"No," said old Jolyon abruptly; "I'll have nothing to do with it!"</content>
<content>The words of both uncle and nephew gave the impression of unspoken meanings, far more important, behind.</content>
<content>And the look they interchanged was like a revelation of this consciousness.</content>
<content>"Well," said Soames; "I thought, for June's sake, I'd tell you, that's all; I thought you'd better know I shan't  stand any nonsense!"</content>
<content>"What is that to me?"</content>
<content>old Jolyon took him up.</content>
<content>"Oh!</content>
<content>I don't  know," said Soames, and flurried by that sharp look he was unable to say more.</content>
<content>"Don't  say I didn't  tell you," he added sulkily, recovering his composure.</content>
<content>"Tell me!"</content>
<content>said old Jolyon; "I don't  know what you mean.</content>
<content>You come worrying me about a thing like this.</content>
<content>I don't  want to hear about your affairs; you must manage them yourself!"</content>
<content>"Very well," said Soames immovably, "I will!"</content>
<content>"Good-morning, then," said old Jolyon, and they parted.</content>
<content>Soames retraced his steps, and going into a celebrated eating-house, asked for a plate of smoked salmon and a glass of Chablis; he seldom ate much in the middle of the day, and generally ate standing, finding the position beneficial to his liver, which was very sound, but to which he desired to put down all his troubles.</content>
<content>When he had finished he went slowly back to his office, with bent head, taking no notice of the swarming thousands on the pavements, who in their turn took no notice of him.</content>
<content>The evening post carried the following reply to Bosinney:
'FORSYTE, BUSTARD AND FORSYTE,
'Commissioners for Oaths,
'92001, BRANCH LANE, POULTRY, E.C.,
'May 17, 1887.</content>
<content>'DEAR BOSINNEY,
'I have, received your letter, the terms of which not a little surprise me.</content>
<content>I was under the impression that you had, and have had all along, a "free hand"; for I do not recollect that any suggestions I have been so unfortunate as to make have met with your approval.</content>
<content>In giving you, in accordance with your request, this "free hand," I wish you to clearly understand that the total cost of the house as handed over to me completely decorated, inclusive of your fee  as arranged between us , must not exceed twelve thousand pounds - L12,000.</content>
<content>This gives you an ample margin, and, as you know, is far more than I originally contemplated.</content>
<content>'I am,
'Yours truly,
'SOAMES FORSYTE.'</content>
<content>On the following day he received a note from Bosinney:
'PHILIP BAYNES BOSINNEY,
'Architect,
'309D, SLOANE STREET, S.W.,
'May 18.</content>
<content>'DEAR FORSYTE,
'If you think that in such a delicate matter as decoration I can bind myself to the exact pound, I am afraid you are mistaken.</content>
<content>I can see that you are tired of the arrangement, and of me, and I had better, therefore, resign.</content>
<content>'Yours faithfully,
'PHILIP BAYNES BOSINNEY.'</content>
<content>Soames pondered long and painfully over his answer, and late at night in the dining-room, when Irene had gone to bed, he composed the following:
'62, MONTPELLIER SQUARE, S.W.,
'May 19, 1887.</content>
<content>'DEAR BOSINNEY,
'I think that in both our interests it would be extremely undesirable that matters should be so left at this stage.</content>
<content>I did not mean to say that if you should exceed the sum named in my letter to you by ten or twenty or even fifty pounds, there would be any difficulty between us.</content>
<content>This being so, I should like you to reconsider your answer.</content>
<content>You have a "free hand" in the terms of this correspondence, and I hope you will see your way to completing the decorations, in the matter of which I know it is difficult to be absolutely exact.</content>
<content>'Yours truly,
'SOAMES FORSYTE.'</content>
<content>Bosinney's answer, which came in the course of the next day, was:
'May 20.</content>
<content>'DEAR FORSYTE,
'Very well.</content>
<content>'PH.</content>
<content>BOSINNEY.'</content>
<content>CHAPTER VI - OLD JOLYON AT THE ZOO
Old Jolyon disposed of his second Meeting - an ordinary Board - summarily.</content>
<content>He was so dictatorial that his fellow directors were left in cabal over the increasing domineeringness of old Forsyte, which they were far from intending to stand much longer, they said.</content>
<content>He went out by Underground to Portland Road Station, whence he took a cab and drove to the Zoo.</content>
<content>He had an assignation there, one of those assignations that had lately been growing more frequent, to which his increasing uneasiness about June and the 'change in her,' as he expressed it, was driving him.</content>
<content>She buried herself away, and was growing thin; if he spoke to her he got no answer, or had his head snapped off, or she looked as if she would burst into tears.</content>
<content>She was as changed as she could be, all through this Bosinney.</content>
<content>As for telling him about anything, not a bit of it!</content>
<content>And he would sit for long spells brooding, his paper unread before him, a cigar extinct between his lips.</content>
<content>She had been such a companion to him ever since she was three years old!</content>
<content>And he loved her so!</content>
<content>Forces regardless of family or class or custom were beating down his guard; impending events over which he had no control threw their shadows on his head.</content>
<content>The irritation of one accustomed to have his way was roused against he knew not what.</content>
<content>Chafing at the slowness of his cab, he reached the Zoo door; but, with his sunny instinct for seizing the good of each moment, he forgot his vexation as he walked towards the tryst.</content>
<content>From the stone terrace above the bear-pit his son and his two grandchildren came hastening down when they saw old Jolyon coming, and led him away towards the lion-house.</content>
<content>They supported him on either side, holding one to each of his hands, - whilst Jolly, perverse like his father, carried his grandfather's umbrella in such a way as to catch people's legs with the crutch of the handle.</content>
<content>Young Jolyon followed.</content>
<content>It was as good as a play to see his father with the children, but such a play as brings smiles with tears behind.</content>
<content>An old man and two small children walking together can be seen at any hour of the day; but the sight of old Jolyon, with Jolly and Holly seemed to young Jolyon a special peep-show of the things that lie at the bottom of our hearts.</content>
<content>The complete surrender of that erect old figure to those little figures on either hand was too poignantly tender, and, being a man of an habitual reflex action, young Jolyon swore softly under his breath.</content>
<content>The show affected him in a way unbecoming to a Forsyte, who is nothing if not undemonstrative.</content>
<content>Thus they reached the lion-house.</content>
<content>There had been a morning fete at the Botanical Gardens, and a large number of Forsy...' - that is, of well-dressed people who kept carriages had brought them on to the Zoo, so as to have more, if possible, for their money, before going back to Rutland Gate or Bryanston Square.</content>
<content>"Let's go on to the Zoo," they had said to each other; "it'll be great fun!"</content>
<content>It was a shilling day; and there would not be all those horrid common people.</content>
<content>In front of the long line of cages they were collected in rows, watching the tawny, ravenous beasts behind the bars await their only pleasure of the four-and-twenty hours.</content>
<content>The hungrier the beast, the greater the fascination.</content>
<content>But whether because the spectators envied his appetite, or, more humanely, because it was so soon to be satisfied, young Jolyon could not tell.</content>
<content>Remarks kept falling on his ears: "That's a nasty-looking brute, that tiger!"</content>
<content>"Oh, what a love!</content>
<content>Look at his little mouth!"</content>
<content>"Yes, he's rather nice!</content>
<content>Don't  go too near, mother."</content>
<content>And frequently, with little pats, one or another would clap their hands to their pockets behind and look round, as though expecting young Jolyon or some disinterested-looking person to relieve them of the contents.</content>
<content>A well-fed man in a white waistcoat said slowly through his teeth: "It's all greed; they can't  be hungry.</content>
<content>Why, they take no exercise."</content>
<content>At these words a tiger snatched a piece of bleeding liver, and the fat man laughed.</content>
<content>His wife, in a Paris model frock and gold nose-nippers, reproved him: "How can you laugh, Harry?</content>
<content>Such a horrid sight!"</content>
<content>Young Jolyon frowned.</content>
<content>The circumstances of his life, though he had ceased to take a too personal view of them, had left him subject to an intermittent contempt; and the class to which he had belonged - the carriage class - especially excited his sarcasm.</content>
<content>To shut up a lion or tiger in confinement was surely a horrible barbarity.</content>
<content>But no cultivated person would admit this.</content>
<content>The idea of its being barbarous to confine wild animals had probably never even occurred to his father for instance; he belonged to the old school, who considered it at once humanizing and educational to confine baboons and panthers, holding the view, no doubt, that in course of time they might induce these creatures not so unreasonably to die of misery and heart-sickness against the bars of their cages, and put the society to the expense of getting others!</content>
<content>In his eyes, as in the eyes of all Forsytes, the pleasure of seeing these beautiful creatures in a state of captivity far outweighed the inconvenience of imprisonment to beasts whom God had so improvidently placed in a state of freedom!</content>
<content>It was for the animals good, removing them at once from the countless dangers of open air and exercise, and enabling them to exercise their functions in the guaranteed seclusion of a private compartment!</content>
<content>Indeed, it was doubtful what wild animals were made for but to be shut up in cages!</content>
<content>But as young Jolyon had in his constitution the elements of impartiality, he reflected that to stigmatize as barbarity that which was merely lack of imagination must be wrong; for none who held these views had been placed in a similar position to the animals they caged, and could not, therefore, be expected to enter into their sensations.</content>
<content>It was not until they were leaving the gardens - Jolly and Holly in a state of blissful delirium - that old Jolyon found an opportunity of speaking to his son on the matter next his heart.</content>
<content>"I don't  know what to make of it," he said; "if she's to go on as she's going on now, I can't  tell what's to come.</content>
<content>I wanted her to see the doctor, but she won't .</content>
<content>She's not a bit like me.</content>
<content>She's your mother all over.</content>
<content>Obstinate as a mule!</content>
<content>If she doesn't  want to do a thing, she won't , and there's an end of it!"</content>
<content>Young Jolyon smiled; his eyes had wandered to his father's chin.</content>
<content>'A pair of you,' he thought, but he said nothing.</content>
<content>"And then," went on old Jolyon, "there's this Bosinney.</content>
<content>I should like to punch the fellow's head, but I can't , I suppose, though - I don't  see why you shouldn't ," he added doubtfully.</content>
<content>"What has he done?</content>
<content>Far better that it should come to an end, if they don't  hit it off!"</content>
<content>Old Jolyon looked at his son.</content>
<content>Now they had actually come to discuss a subject connected with the relations between the sexes he felt distrustful.</content>
<content>Jo would be sure to hold some loose view or other.</content>
<content>"Well, I don't  know what you think," he said; "I dare say your sympathy's with him - shouldn't  be surprised; but I think he's behaving precious badly, and if he comes my way I shall tell him so."</content>
<content>He dropped the subject.</content>
<content>It was impossible to discuss with his son the true nature and meaning of Bosinney's defection.</content>
<content>Had not his son done the very same thing  worse, if possible  fifteen years ago?</content>
<content>There seemed no end to the consequences of that piece of folly.</content>
<content>Young Jolyon also was silent; he had quickly penetrated his father's thought, for, dethroned from the high seat of an obvious and uncomplicated view of things, he had become both perceptive and subtle.</content>
<content>The attitude he had adopted towards sexual matters fifteen years before, however, was too different from his father's.</content>
<content>There was no bridging the gulf.</content>
<content>He said coolly: "I suppose he's fallen in love with some other woman?"</content>
<content>Old Jolyon gave him a dubious look: "I can't  tell," he said; "they say so!"</content>
<content>"Then, it's probably true," remarked young Jolyon unexpectedly; "and I suppose they've  told you who she is?"</content>
<content>"Yes," said old Jolyon, "Soames's wife!"</content>
<content>Young Jolyon did not whistle: The circumstances of his own life had rendered him incapable of whistling on such a subject, but he looked at his father, while the ghost of a smile hovered over his face.</content>
<content>If old Jolyon saw, he took no notice.</content>
<content>"She and June were bosom friends!"</content>
<content>he muttered.</content>
<content>"Poor little June!"</content>
<content>said young Jolyon softly.</content>
<content>He thought of his daughter still as a babe of three.</content>
<content>Old Jolyon came to a sudden halt.</content>
<content>"I don't  believe a word of it," he said, "it's some old woman's tale.</content>
<content>Get me a cab, Jo, I'm tired to death!"</content>
<content>They stood at a corner to see if an empty cab would come along, while carriage after carriage drove past, bearing Forsytes of all descriptions from the Zoo.</content>
<content>The harness, the liveries, the gloss on the horses' coats, shone and glittered in the May sunlight, and each equipage, landau, sociable, barouche, Victoria, or brougham, seemed to roll out proudly from its wheels:
'I and my horses and my men you know,' Indeed the whole turn-out have cost a pot.</content>
<content>But we were worth it every penny.</content>
<content>Look At Master and at Missis now, the dawgs!</content>
<content>Ease with security - ah!</content>
<content>that's the ticket!</content>
<content>And such, as everyone knows, is fit accompaniment for a perambulating Forsyte.</content>
<content>Amongst these carriages was a barouche coming at a greater pace than the others, drawn by a pair of bright bay horses.</content>
<content>It swung on its high springs, and the four people who filled it seemed rocked as in a cradle.</content>
<content>This chariot attracted young Jolyon's attention; and suddenly, on the back seat, he recognised his Uncle James, unmistakable in spite of the increased whiteness of his whiskers; opposite, their backs defended by sunshades, Rachel Forsyte and her elder but married sister, Winifred Dartie, in irreproachable toilettes, had posed their heads haughtily, like two of the birds they had been seeing at the Zoo; while by James' side reclined Dartie, in a brand-new frock-coat buttoned tight and square, with a large expanse of carefully shot linen protruding below each wristband.</content>
<content>An extra, if subdued, sparkle, an added touch of the best gloss or varnish characterized this vehicle, and seemed to distinguish it from all the others, as though by some happy extravagance - like that which marks out the real 'work of art' from the ordinary 'picture' - it were designated as the typical car, the very throne of Forsytedom.</content>
<content>Old Jolyon did not see them pass; he was petting poor Holly who was tired, but those in the carriage had taken in the little group; the ladies' heads tilted suddenly, there was a spasmodic screening movement of parasols; James' face protruded naively, like the head of a long bird, his mouth slowly opening.</content>
<content>The shield-like rounds of the parasols grew smaller and smaller, and vanished.</content>
<content>Young Jolyon saw that he had been recognised, even by Winifred, who could not have been more than fifteen when he had forfeited the right to be considered a Forsyte.</content>
<content>There was not much change in them!</content>
<content>He remembered the exact look of their turn-out all that time ago: Horses, men, carriage - all different now, no doubt - but of the precise stamp of fifteen years before; the same neat display, the same nicely calculated arrogance ease with security!</content>
<content>The swing exact, the pose of the sunshades exact, exact the spirit of the whole thing.</content>
<content>And in the sunlight, defended by the haughty shields of parasols, carriage after carriage went by.</content>
<content>"Uncle James has just passed, with his female folk," said young Jolyon.</content>
<content>His father looked black.</content>
<content>"Did your uncle see us?</content>
<content>Yes?</content>
<content>Hmph!</content>
<content>What's he want, coming down into these parts?"</content>
<content>An empty cab drove up at this moment, and old Jolyon stopped it.</content>
<content>"I shall see you again before long, my boy!"</content>
<content>he said.</content>
<content>"Don't  you go paying any attention to what I've  been saying about young Bosinney - I don't  believe a word of it!"</content>
<content>Kissing the children, who tried to detain him, he stepped in and was borne away.</content>
<content>Young Jolyon, who had taken Holly up in his arms, stood motionless at the corner, looking after the cab.</content>
<content>CHAPTER VII - AFTERNOON AT TIMOTHY'S
If old Jolyon, as he got into his cab, had said: 'I won't  believe a word of it!'</content>
<content>he would more truthfully have expressed his sentiments.</content>
<content>The notion that James and his womankind had seen him in the company of his son had awakened in him not only the impatience he always felt when crossed, but that secret hostility natural between brothers, the roots of which - little nursery rivalries - sometimes toughen and deepen as life goes on, and, all hidden, support a plant capable of producing in season the bitterest fruits.</content>
<content>Hitherto there had been between these six brothers no more unfriendly feeling than that caused by the secret and natural doubt that the others might be richer than themselves; a feeling increased to the pitch of curiosity by the approach of death - that end of all handicaps - and the great 'closeness' of their man of business, who, with some sagacity, would profess to Nicholas ignorance of James' income, to James ignorance of old Jolyon's, to Jolyon ignorance of Roger's, to Roger ignorance of Swithin's, while to Swithin he would say most irritatingly that Nicholas must be a rich man.</content>
<content>Timothy alone was exempt, being in gilt-edged securities.</content>
<content>But now, between two of them at least, had arisen a very different sense of injury.</content>
<content>From the moment when James had the impertinence to pry into his affairs - as he put it - old Jolyon no longer chose to credit this story about Bosinney.</content>
<content>His grand-daughter slighted through a member of 'that fellow's' family!</content>
<content>He made up his mind that Bosinney was maligned.</content>
<content>There must be some other reason for his defection.</content>
<content>June had flown out at him, or something; she was as touchy as she could be!</content>
<content>He would, however, let Timothy have a bit of his mind, and see if he would go on dropping hints!</content>
<content>And he would not let the grass grow under his feet either, he would go there at once, and take very good care that he didn't  have to go again on the same errand.</content>
<content>He saw James' carriage blocking the pavement in front of 'The Bower.'</content>
<content>So they had got there before him - cackling about having seen him, he dared say!</content>
<content>And further on, Swithin's greys were turning their noses towards the noses of James' bays, as though in conclave over the family, while their coachmen were in conclave above.</content>
<content>Old Jolyon, depositing his hat on the chair in the narrow hall, where that hat of Bosinney's had so long ago been mistaken for a cat, passed his thin hand grimly over his face with its great drooping white moustaches, as though to remove all traces of expression, and made his way upstairs.</content>
<content>He found the front drawing-room full.</content>
<content>It was full enough at the best of times - without visitors - without any one in it - for Timothy and his sisters, following the tradition of their generation, considered that a room was not quite 'nice' unless it was 'properly' furnished.</content>
<content>It held, therefore, eleven chairs, a sofa, three tables, two cabinets, innumerable knicknacks, and part of a large grand piano.</content>
<content>And now, occupied by Mrs. Small, Aunt Hester, by Swithin, James, Rachel, Winifred, Euphemia, who had come in again to return 'Passion and Paregoric' which she had read at lunch, and her chum Frances, Roger's daughter  the musical Forsyte, the one who composed songs , there was only one chair left unoccupied, except, of course, the two that nobody ever sat on - and the only standing room was occupied by the cat, on whom old Jolyon promptly stepped.</content>
<content>In these days it was by no means unusual for Timothy to have so many visitors.</content>
<content>The family had always, one and all, had a real respect for Aunt Ann, and now that she was gone, they were coming far more frequently to The Bower, and staying longer.</content>
<content>Swithin had been the first to arrive, and seated torpid in a red satin chair with a gilt back, he gave every appearance of lasting the others out.</content>
<content>And symbolizing Bosinney's name 'the big one,' with his great stature and bulk, his thick white hair, his puffy immovable shaven face, he looked more primeval than ever in the highly upholstered room.</content>
<content>His conversation, as usual of late, had turned at once upon Irene, and he had lost no time in giving Aunts Juley and Hester his opinion with regard to this rumour he heard was going about.</content>
<content>No - as he said - she might want a bit of flirtation - a pretty woman must have her fling; but more than that he did not believe.</content>
<content>Nothing open; she had too much good sense, too much proper appreciation of what was due to her position, and to the family!</content>
<content>No sc - , he was going to say 'scandal' but the very idea was so preposterous that he waved his hand as though to say - 'but let that pass!'</content>
<content>Granted that Swithin took a bachelor's view of the situation - still what indeed was not due to that family in which so many had done so well for themselves, had attained a certain position?</content>
<content>If he had heard in dark, pessimistic moments the words 'yeomen' and 've ry small beer' used in connection with his origin, did he believe them?</content>
<content>No!</content>
<content>he cherished, hugging it pathetically to his bosom the secret theory that there was something distinguished somewhere in his ancestry.</content>
<content>"Must be," he once said to young Jolyon, before the latter went to the bad.</content>
<content>"Look at us, we've  got on!</content>
<content>There must be good blood in us somewhere."</content>
<content>He had been fond of young Jolyon: the boy had been in a good set at College, had known that old ruffian Sir Charles Fiste's sons - a pretty rascal one of them had turned out, too; and there was style about him - it was a thousand pities he had run off with that half-foreign governess!</content>
<content>If he must go off like that why couldn't  he have chosen someone who would have done them credit!</content>
<content>And what was he now?</content>
<content>- an underwriter at Lloyd's; they said he even painted pictures - pictures!</content>
<content>Damme!</content>
<content>he might have ended as Sir Jolyon Forsyte, Bart., with a seat in Parliament, and a place in the country!</content>
<content>It was Swithin who, following the impulse which sooner or later urges thereto some member of every great family, went to the Heralds' Office, where they assured him that he was undoubtedly of the same family as the well-known Forsites with an 'i,' whose arms were 'three dexter buckles on a sable ground gules,' hoping no doubt to get him to take them up.</content>
<content>Swithin, however, did not do this, but having ascertained that the crest was a 'pheasant proper,' and the motto 'For Forsite,' he had the pheasant proper placed upon his carriage and the buttons of his coachman, and both crest and motto on his writing-paper.</content>
<content>The arms he hugged to himself, partly because, not having paid for them, he thought it would look ostentatious to put them on his carriage, and he hated ostentation, and partly because he, like any practical man all over the country, had a secret dislike and contempt for things he could not understand he found it hard, as anyone might, to swallow 'three dexter buckles on a sable ground gules.'</content>
<content>He never forgot, however, their having told him that if he paid for them he would be entitled to use them, and it strengthened his conviction that he was a gentleman.</content>
<content>Imperceptibly the rest of the family absorbed the 'pheasant proper,' and some, more serious than others, adopted the motto; old Jolyon, however, refused to use the latter, saying that it was humbug meaning nothing, so far as he could see.</content>
<content>Among the older generation it was perhaps known at bottom from what great historical event they derived their crest; and if pressed on the subject, sooner than tell a lie - they did not like telling lies, having an impression that only Frenchmen and Russians told them - they would confess hurriedly that Swithin had got hold of it somehow.</content>
<content>Among the younger generation the matter was wrapped in a discretion proper.</content>
<content>They did not want to hurt the feelings of their elders, nor to feel ridiculous themselves; they simply used the crest....
"No," said Swithin, "he had had an opportunity of seeing for himself, and what he should say was, that there was nothing in her manner to that young Buccaneer or Bosinney or whatever his name was, different from her manner to himself; in fact, he should rather say...." But here the entrance of Frances and Euphemia put an unfortunate stop to the conversation, for this was not a subject which could be discussed before young people.</content>
<content>And though Swithin was somewhat upset at being stopped like this on the point of saying something important, he soon recovered his affability.</content>
<content>He was rather fond of Frances - Francie, as she was called in the family.</content>
<content>She was so smart, and they told him she made a pretty little pot of pin-money by her songs; he called it very clever of her.</content>
<content>He rather prided himself indeed on a liberal attitude towards women, not seeing any reason why they shouldn't  paint pictures, or write tunes, or books even, for the matter of that, especially if they could turn a useful penny by it; not at all - kept them out of mischief.</content>
<content>It was not as if they were men!</content>
<content>'Little Francie,' as she was usually called with good-natured contempt, was an important personage, if only as a standing illustration of the attitude of Forsytes towards the Arts.</content>
<content>She was not really 'little,' but rather tall, with dark hair for a Forsyte, which, together with a grey eye, gave her what was called 'a Celtic appearance.'</content>
<content>She wrote songs with titles like 'Breathing Sighs,' or 'Kiss me, Mother, ere I die,' with a refrain like an anthem:
'Kiss me, Mother, ere I die; Kiss me-kiss me, Mother, ah!</content>
<content>Kiss, ah!</content>
<content>kiss me e-ere I - Kiss me, Mother, ere I d-d-die!'</content>
<content>She wrote the words to them herself, and other poems.</content>
<content>In lighter moments she wrote waltzes, one of which, the 'Kensington Coil,' was almost national to Kensington, having a sweet dip in it.</content>
<content>It was very original.</content>
<content>Then there were her 'Songs for Little People,' at once educational and witty, especially 'Gran'ma's Porgie,' and that ditty, almost prophetically imbued with the coming Imperial spirit, entitled 'Black Him In His Little Eye.'</content>
<content>Any publisher would take these, and reviews like 'High Living,' and the 'Ladies' Genteel Guide' went into raptures over: 'Another of Miss Francie Forsyte's spirited ditties, sparkling and pathetic.</content>
<content>We ourselves were moved to tears and laughter.</content>
<content>Miss Forsyte should go far.'</content>
<content>With the true instinct of her breed, Francie had made a point of knowing the right people - people who would write about her, and talk about her, and people in Society, too - keeping a mental register of just where to exert her fascinations, and an eye on that steady scale of rising prices, which in her mind's eye represented the future.</content>
<content>In this way she caused herself to be universally respected.</content>
<content>Once, at a time when her emotions were whipped by an attachment - for the tenor of Roger's life, with its whole-hearted collection of house property, had induced in his only daughter a tendency towards passion - she turned to great and sincere work, choosing the sonata form, for the violin.</content>
<content>This was the only one of her productions that troubled the Forsytes.</content>
<content>They felt at once that it would not sell.</content>
<content>Roger, who liked having a clever daughter well enough, and often alluded to the amount of pocket-money she made for herself, was upset by this violin sonata.</content>
<content>"Rubbish like that!"</content>
<content>he called it.</content>
<content>Francie had borrowed young Flageoletti from Euphemia, to play it in the drawing-room at Prince's Gardens.</content>
<content>As a matter of fact Roger was right.</content>
<content>It was rubbish, but - annoying!</content>
<content>the sort of rubbish that wouldn't  sell.</content>
<content>As every Forsyte knows, rubbish that sells is not rubbish at all - far from it.</content>
<content>And yet, in spite of the sound common sense which fixed the worth of art at what it would fetch, some of the Forsytes - Aunt Hester, for instance, who had always been musical - could not help regretting that Francie's music was not 'classical'; the same with her poems.</content>
<content>But then, as Aunt Hester said, they didn't  see any poetry nowadays, all the poems were 'little light things.'</content>
<content>There was nobody who could write a poem like 'Paradise Lost,' or 'Childe Harold'; either of which made you feel that you really had read something.</content>
<content>Still, it was nice for Francie to have something to occupy her; while other girls were spending money shopping she was making it!</content>
<content>And both Aunt Hester and Aunt Juley were always ready to listen to the latest story of how Francie had got her price increased.</content>
<content>They listened now, together with Swithin, who sat pretending not to, for these young people talked so fast and mumbled so, he never could catch what they said.</content>
<content>"And I can't  think," said Mrs. Septimus, "how you do it.</content>
<content>I should never have the audacity!"</content>
<content>Francie smiled lightly.</content>
<content>"I'd much rather deal with a man than a woman.</content>
<content>Women are so sharp!"</content>
<content>"My dear," cried Mrs. Small, "I'm sure we're not."</content>
<content>Euphemia went off into her silent laugh, and, ending with the squeak, said, as though being strangled: "Oh, you'll kill me some day, auntie."</content>
<content>Swithin saw no necessity to laugh; he detested people laughing when he himself perceived no joke.</content>
<content>Indeed, he detested Euphemia altogether, to whom he always alluded as 'Nick's daughter, what's she called - the pale one?'</content>
<content>He had just missed being her god-father - indeed, would have been, had he not taken a firm stand against her outlandish name.</content>
<content>He hated becoming a godfather.</content>
<content>Swithin then said to Francie with dignity: "It's a fine day - er - for the time of year."</content>
<content>But Euphemia, who knew perfectly well that he had refused to be her godfather, turned to Aunt Hester, and began telling her how she had seen Irene - Mrs. Soames - at the Church and Commercial Stores.</content>
<content>"And Soames was with her?"</content>
<content>said Aunt Hester, to whom Mrs. Small had as yet had no opportunity of relating the incident.</content>
<content>"Soames with her?</content>
<content>Of course not!"</content>
<content>"But was she all alone in London?"</content>
<content>"Oh, no; there was Mr. Bosinney with her.</content>
<content>She was perfectly dressed."</content>
<content>But Swithin, hearing the name Irene, looked severely at Euphemia, who, it is true, never did look well in a dress, whatever she may have done on other occasions, and said:
"Dressed like a lady, I've  no doubt.</content>
<content>It's a pleasure to see her."</content>
<content>At this moment James and his daughters were announced.</content>
<content>Dartie, feeling badly in want of a drink, had pleaded an appointment with his dentist, and, being put down at the Marble Arch, had got into a hansom, and was already seated in the window of his club in Piccadilly.</content>
<content>His wife, he told his cronies, had wanted to take him to pay some calls.</content>
<content>It was not in his line - not exactly.</content>
<content>Haw!</content>
<content>Hailing the waiter, he sent him out to the hall to see what had won the 4.30 race.</content>
<content>He was dog-tired, he said, and that was a fact; had been drivin' about with his wife to 'shows' all the afternoon.</content>
<content>Had put his foot down at last.</content>
<content>A fellow must live his own life.</content>
<content>At this moment, glancing out of the bay window - for he loved this seat whence he could see everybody pass - his eye unfortunately, or perhaps fortunately, chanced to light on the figure of Soames, who was mousing across the road from the Green Park-side, with the evident intention of coming in, for he, too, belonged to 'The Iseeum.'</content>
<content>Dartie sprang to his feet; grasping his glass, he muttered something about 'that 4.30 race,' and swiftly withdrew to the card-room, where Soames never came.</content>
<content>Here, in complete isolation and a dim light, he lived his own life till half past seven, by which hour he knew Soames must certainly have left the club.</content>
<content>It would not do, as he kept repeating to himself whenever he felt the impulse to join the gossips in the bay-window getting too strong for him - it absolutely would not do, with finances as low as his, and the 'old man'  James  rusty ever since that business over the oil shares, which was no fault of his, to risk a row with Winifred.</content>
<content>If Soames were to see him in the club it would be sure to come round to her that he wasn't  at the dentist's at all.</content>
<content>He never knew a family where things 'came round' so.</content>
<content>Uneasily, amongst the green baize card-tables, a frown on his olive coloured face, his check trousers crossed, and patent-leather boots shining through the gloom, he sat biting his forefinger, and wondering where the deuce he was to get the money if Erotic failed to win the Lancashire Cup.</content>
<content>His thoughts turned gloomily to the Forsytes.</content>
<content>What a set they were!</content>
<content>There was no getting anything out of them - at least, it was a matter of extreme difficulty.</content>
<content>They were so d - -d particular about money matters; not a sportsman amongst the lot, unless it were George.</content>
<content>That fellow Soames, for instance, would have a ft if you tried to borrow a tenner from him, or, if he didn't  have a fit, he looked at you with his cursed supercilious smile, as if you were a lost soul because you were in want of money.</content>
<content>And that wife of his  Dartie's mouth watered involuntarily , he had tried to be on good terms with her, as one naturally would with any pretty sister-in-law, but he would be cursed if the  he mentally used a coarse word  - would have anything to say to him - she looked at him, indeed, as if he were dirt - and yet she could go far enough, he wouldn't  mind betting.</content>
<content>He knew women; they weren't  made with soft eyes and figures like that for nothing, as that fellow Soames would jolly soon find out, if there were anything in what he had heard about this Buccaneer Johnny.</content>
<content>Rising from his chair, Dartie took a turn across the room, ending in front of the looking-glass over the marble chimney-piece; and there he stood for a long time contemplating in the glass the reflection of his face.</content>
<content>It had that look, peculiar to some men, of having been steeped in linseed oil, with its waxed dark moustaches and the little distinguished commencements of side whiskers; and concernedly he felt the promise of a pimple on the side of his slightly curved and fattish nose.</content>
<content>In the meantime old Jolyon had found the remaining chair in Timothy's commodious drawing-room.</content>
<content>His advent had obviously put a stop to the conversation, decided awkwardness having set in.</content>
<content>Aunt Juley, with her well-known kindheartedness, hastened to set people at their ease again.</content>
<content>"Yes, Jolyon," she said, "we were just saying that you haven't  been here for a long time; but we mustn't  be surprised.</content>
<content>You're busy, of course?</content>
<content>James was just saying what a busy time of year...."
"Was he?"</content>
<content>said old Jolyon, looking hard at James.</content>
<content>"It wouldn't  be half so busy if everybody minded their own business."</content>
<content>James, brooding in a small chair from which his knees ran uphill, shifted his feet uneasily, and put one of them down on the cat, which had unwisely taken refuge from old Jolyon beside him.</content>
<content>"Here, you've  got a cat here," he said in an injured voice, withdrawing his foot nervously as he felt it squeezing into the soft, furry body.</content>
<content>"Several," said old Jolyon, looking at one face and another; "I trod on one just now."</content>
<content>A silence followed.</content>
<content>Then Mrs. Small, twisting her fingers and gazing round with 'pathetic calm', asked: "And how is dear June?"</content>
<content>A twinkle of humour shot through the sternness of old Jolyon's eyes.</content>
<content>Extraordinary old woman, Juley!</content>
<content>No one quite like her for saying the wrong thing!</content>
<content>"Bad!"</content>
<content>he said; "London don't  agree with her - too many people about, too much clatter and chatter by half."</content>
<content>He laid emphasis on the words, and again looked James in the face.</content>
<content>Nobody spoke.</content>
<content>A feeling of its being too dangerous to take a step in any direction, or hazard any remark, had fallen on them all.</content>
<content>Something of the sense of the impending, that comes over the spectator of a Greek tragedy, had entered that upholstered room, filled with those white-haired, frock-coated old men, and fashionably attired women, who were all of the same blood, between all of whom existed an unseizable resemblance.</content>
<content>Not that they were conscious of it - the visits of such fateful, bitter spirits are only felt.</content>
<content>Then Swithin rose.</content>
<content>He would not sit there, feeling like that - he was not to be put down by anyone!</content>
<content>And, manoeuvring round the room with added pomp, he shook hands with each separately.</content>
<content>"You tell Timothy from me," he said, "that he coddles himself too much!"</content>
<content>Then, turning to Francie, whom he considered 'smart,' he added: "You come with me for a drive one of these days."</content>
<content>But this conjured up the vision of that other eventful drive which had been so much talked about, and he stood quite still for a second, with glassy eyes, as though waiting to catch up with the significance of what he himself had said; then, suddenly recollecting that he didn't  care a damn, he turned to old Jolyon: "Well, good-bye, Jolyon!</content>
<content>You shouldn't  go about without an overcoat; you'll be getting sciatica or something!"</content>
<content>And, kicking the cat slightly with the pointed tip of his patent leather boot, he took his huge form away.</content>
<content>When he had gone everyone looked secretly at the others, to see how they had taken the mention of the word 'drive' - the word which had become famous, and acquired an overwhelming importance, as the only official - so to speak - news in connection with the vague and sinister rumour clinging to the family tongue.</content>
<content>Euphemia, yielding to an impulse, said with a short laugh: "I'm glad Uncle Swithin doesn't  ask me to go for drives."</content>
<content>Mrs. Small, to reassure her and smooth over any little awkwardness the subject might have, replied: "My dear, he likes to take somebody well dressed, who will do him a little credit.</content>
<content>I shall never forget the drive he took me.</content>
<content>It was an experience!"</content>
<content>And her chubby round old face was spread for a moment with a strange contentment; then broke into pouts, and tears came into her eyes.</content>
<content>She was thinking of that long ago driving tour she had once taken with Septimus Small.</content>
<content>James, who had relapsed into his nervous brooding in the little chair, suddenly roused himself: "He's a funny fellow, Swithin," he said, but in a half-hearted way.</content>
<content>Old Jolyon's silence, his stern eyes, held them all in a kind of paralysis.</content>
<content>He was disconcerted himself by the effect of his own words - an effect which seemed to deepen the importance of the very rumour he had come to scotch; but he was still angry.</content>
<content>He had not done with them yet - No, no - he would give them another rub or two.</content>
<content>He did not wish to rub his nieces, he had no quarrel with them - a young and presentable female always appealed to old Jolyon's clemency - but that fellow James, and, in a less degree perhaps, those others, deserved all they would get.</content>
<content>And he, too, asked for Timothy.</content>
<content>As though feeling that some danger threatened her younger brother, Aunt Juley suddenly offered him tea: "There it is," she said, "all cold and nasty, waiting for you in the back drawing room, but Smither shall make you some fresh."</content>
<content>Old Jolyon rose: "Thank you," he said, looking straight at James, "but I've  no time for tea, and - scandal, and the rest of it!</content>
<content>It's time I was at home.</content>
<content>Good-bye, Julia; good-bye, Hester; good-bye, Winifred."</content>
<content>Without more ceremonious adieux, he marched out.</content>
<content>Once again in his cab, his anger evaporated, for so it ever was with his wrath - when he had rapped out, it was gone.</content>
<content>Sadness came over his spirit.</content>
<content>He had stopped their mouths, maybe, but at what a cost!</content>
<content>At the cost of certain knowledge that the rumour he had been resolved not to believe was true.</content>
<content>June was abandoned, and for the wife of that fellow's son!</content>
<content>He felt it was true, and hardened himself to treat it as if it were not; but the pain he hid beneath this resolution began slowly, surely, to vent itself in a blind resentment against James and his son.</content>
<content>The six women and one man left behind in the little drawing-room began talking as easily as might be after such an occurrence, for though each one of them knew for a fact that he or she never talked scandal, each one of them also knew that the other six did; all were therefore angry and at a loss.</content>
<content>James only was silent, disturbed, to the bottom of his soul.</content>
<content>Presently Francie said: "Do you know, I think Uncle Jolyon is terribly changed this last year.</content>
<content>What do you think, Aunt Hester?"</content>
<content>Aunt Hester made a little movement of recoil: "Oh, ask your Aunt Julia!"</content>
<content>she said; "I know nothing about it."</content>
<content>No one else was afraid of assenting, and James muttered gloomily at the floor: "He's not half the man he was."</content>
<content>"I've  noticed it a long time," went on Francie; "he's aged tremendously."</content>
<content>Aunt Juley shook her head; her face seemed suddenly to have become one immense pout.</content>
<content>"Poor dear Jolyon," she said, 'somebody ought to see to it for him!"</content>
<content>There was again silence; then, as though in terror of being left solitarily behind, all five visitors rose simultaneously, and took their departure.</content>
<content>Mrs. Small, Aunt Hester, and their cat were left once more alone, the sound of a door closing in the distance announced the approach of Timothy.</content>
<content>That evening, when Aunt Hester had just got off to sleep in the back bedroom that used to be Aunt Juley's before Aunt Juley took Aunt Ann's, her door was opened, and Mrs. Small, in a pink night-cap, a candle in her hand, entered: "Hester!"</content>
<content>she said.</content>
<content>"Hester!"</content>
<content>Aunt Hester faintly rustled the sheet.</content>
<content>"Hester," repeated Aunt Juley, to make quite sure that she had awakened her, "I am quite troubled about poor dear Jolyon.</content>
<content>What," Aunt Juley dwelt on the word, "do you think ought to be done?"</content>
<content>Aunt Hester again rustled the sheet, her voice was heard faintly pleading: "Done?</content>
<content>How should I know?"</content>
<content>Aunt Juley turned away satisfied, and closing the door with extra gentleness so as not to disturb dear Hester, let it slip through her fingers and fall to with a 'crack.'</content>
<content>Back in her own room, she stood at the window gazing at the moon over the trees in the Park, through a chink in the muslin curtains, close drawn lest anyone should see.</content>
<content>And there, with her face all round and pouting in its pink cap, and her eyes wet, she thought of 'dear Jolyon,' so old and so lonely, and how she could be of some use to him; and how he would come to love her, as she had never been loved since - since poor Septimus went away.</content>
<content>CHAPTER VIII - DANCE AT ROGER'S
Roger's house in Prince's Gardens was brilliantly alight.</content>
<content>Large numbers of wax candles had been collected and placed in cut-glass chandeliers, and the parquet floor of the long, double drawing-room reflected these constellations.</content>
<content>An appearance of real spaciousness had been secured by moving out all the furniture on to the upper landings, and enclosing the room with those strange appendages of civilization known as 'rout' seats.</content>
<content>In a remote corner, embowered in palms, was a cottage piano, with a copy of the 'Kensington Coil' open on the music-stand.</content>
<content>Roger had objected to a band.</content>
<content>He didn't  see in the least what they wanted with a band; he wouldn't  go to the expense, and there was an end of it.</content>
<content>Francie  her mother, whom Roger had long since reduced to chronic dyspepsia, went to bed on such occasions , had been obliged to content herself with supplementing the piano by a young man who played the cornet, and she so arranged with palms that anyone who did not look into the heart of things might imagine there were several musicians secreted there.</content>
<content>She made up her mind to tell them to play loud - there was a lot of music in a cornet, if the man would only put his soul into it.</content>
<content>In the more cultivated American tongue, she was 'through' at last - through that tortuous labyrinth of make-shifts, which must be traversed before fashionable display can be combined with the sound economy of a Forsyte.</content>
<content>Thin but brilliant, in her maize-coloured frock with much tulle about the shoulders, she went from place to place, fitting on her gloves, and casting her eye over it all.</content>
<content>To the hired butler  for Roger only kept maids  she spoke about the wine.</content>
<content>Did he quite understand that Mr. Forsyte wished a dozen bottles of the champagne from Whiteley's to be put out?</content>
<content>But if that were finished  she did not suppose it would be, most of the ladies would drink water, no doubt , but if it were, there was the champagne cup, and he must do the best he could with that.</content>
<content>She hated having to say this sort of thing to a butler, it was so infra dig.</content>
<content>; but what could you do with father?</content>
<content>Roger, indeed, after making himself consistently disagreeable about the dance, would come down presently, with his fresh colour and bumpy forehead, as though he had been its promoter; and he would smile, and probably take the prettiest woman in to supper; and at two o'clock, just as they were getting into the swing, he would go up secretly to the musicians and tell them to play 'God Save the Queen,' and go away.</content>
<content>Francie devoutly hoped he might soon get tired, and slip off to bed.</content>
<content>The three or four devoted girl friends who were staying in the house for this dance had partaken with her, in a small, abandoned room upstairs, of tea and cold chicken-legs, hurriedly served; the men had been sent out to dine at Eustace's Club, it being felt that they must be fed up.</content>
<content>Punctually on the stroke of nine arrived Mrs. Small alone.</content>
<content>She made elaborate apologies for the absence of Timothy, omitting all mention of Aunt Hester, who, at the last minute, had said she could not be bothered.</content>
<content>Francie received her effusively, and placed her on a rout seat, where she left her, pouting and solitary in lavender-coloured satin - the first time she had worn colour since Aunt Ann's death.</content>
<content>The devoted maiden friends came now from their rooms, each by magic arrangement in a differently coloured frock, but all with the same liberal allowance of tulle on the shoulders and at the bosom - for they were, by some fatality, lean to a girl.</content>
<content>They were all taken up to Mrs. Small.</content>
<content>None stayed with her more than a few seconds, but clustering together talked and twisted their programmes, looking secretly at the door for the first appearance of a man.</content>
<content>Then arrived in a group a number of Nicholases, always punctual - the fashion up Ladbroke Grove way; and close behind them Eustace and his men, gloomy and smelling rather of smoke.</content>
<content>Three or four of Francie's lovers now appeared, one after the other; she had made each promise to come early.</content>
<content>They were all clean-shaven and sprightly, with that peculiar kind of young-man sprightliness which had recently invaded Kensington; they did not seem to mind each other's presence in the least, and wore their ties bunching out at the ends, white waistcoats, and socks with clocks.</content>
<content>All had handkerchiefs concealed in their cuffs.</content>
<content>They moved buoyantly, each armoured in professional gaiety, as though he had come to do great deeds.</content>
<content>Their faces when they danced, far from wearing the traditional solemn look of the dancing Englishman, were irresponsible, charming, suave; they bounded, twirling their partners at great pace, without pedantic attention to the rhythm of the music.</content>
<content>At other dancers they looked with a kind of airy scorn - they, the light brigade, the heroes of a hundred Kensington 'hops' - from whom alone could the right manner and smile and step be hoped.</content>
<content>After this the stream came fast; chaperones silting up along the wall facing the entrance, the volatile element swelling the eddy in the larger room.</content>
<content>Men were scarce, and wallflowers wore their peculiar, pathetic expression, a patient, sourish smile which seemed to say: "Oh, no!</content>
<content>don't  mistake me, I know you are not coming up to me.</content>
<content>I can hardly expect that!"</content>
<content>And Francie would plead with one of her lovers, or with some callow youth: "Now, to please me, do let me introduce you to Miss Pink; such a nice girl, really!"</content>
<content>and she would bring him up, and say: "Miss Pink - Mr. Gathercole.</content>
<content>Can you spare him a dance?"</content>
<content>Then Miss Pink, smiling her forced smile, colouring a little, answered: "Oh!</content>
<content>I think so!"</content>
<content>and screening her empty card, wrote on it the name of Gathercole, spelling it passionately in the district that he proposed, about the second extra.</content>
<content>But when the youth had murmured that it was hot, and passed, she relapsed into her attitude of hopeless expectation, into her patient, sourish smile.</content>
<content>Mothers, slowly fanning their faces, watched their daughters, and in their eyes could be read all the story of those daughters' fortunes.</content>
<content>As for themselves, to sit hour after hour, dead tired, silent, or talking spasmodically - what did it matter, so long as the girls were having a good time!</content>
<content>But to see them neglected and passed by!</content>
<content>Ah!</content>
<content>they smiled, but their eyes stabbed like the eyes of an offended swan; they longed to pluck young Gathercole by the slack of his dandified breeches, and drag him to their daughters - the jackanapes!</content>
<content>And all the cruelties and hardness of life, its pathos and unequal chances, its conceit, self-forgetfulness, and patience, were presented on the battle-field of this Kensington ball-room.</content>
<content>Here and there, too, lovers - not lovers like Francie's, a peculiar breed, but simply lovers - trembling, blushing, silent, sought each other by flying glances, sought to meet and touch in the mazes of the dance, and now and again dancing together, struck some beholder by the light in their eyes.</content>
<content>Not a second before ten o'clock came the Jameses - Emily, Rachel, Winifred  Dartie had been left behind, having on a former occasion drunk too much of Roger's champagne , and Cicely, the youngest, making her debut; behind them, following in a hansom from the paternal mansion where they had dined, Soames and Irene.</content>
<content>All these ladies had shoulder-straps and no tulle - thus showing at once, by a bolder exposure of flesh, that they came from the more fashionable side of the Park.</content>
<content>Soames, sidling back from the contact of the dancers, took up a position against the wall.</content>
<content>Guarding himself with his pale smile, he stood watching.</content>
<content>Waltz after waltz began and ended, couple after couple brushed by with smiling lips, laughter, and snatches of talk; or with set lips, and eyes searching the throng; or again, with silent, parted lips, and eyes on each other.</content>
<content>And the scent of festivity, the odour of flowers, and hair, of essences that women love, rose suffocatingly in the heat of the summer night.</content>
<content>Silent, with something of scorn in his smile, Soames seemed to notice nothing; but now and again his eyes, finding that which they sought, would fix themselves on a point in the shifting throng, and the smile die off his lips.</content>
<content>He danced with no one.</content>
<content>Some fellows danced with their wives; his sense of 'form' had never permitted him to dance with Irene since their marriage, and the God of the Forsytes alone can tell whether this was a relief to him or not.</content>
<content>She passed, dancing with other men, her dress, iris-coloured, floating away from her feet.</content>
<content>She danced well; he was tired of hearing women say with an acid smile: "How beautifully your wife dances, Mr. Forsyte - it's quite a pleasure to watch her!"</content>
<content>Tired of answering them with his sidelong glance: "You think so?"</content>
<content>A young couple close by flirted a fan by turns, making an unpleasant draught.</content>
<content>Francie and one of her lovers stood near.</content>
<content>They were talking of love.</content>
<content>He heard Roger's voice behind, giving an order about supper to a servant.</content>
<content>Everything was very second-class!</content>
<content>He wished that he had not come!</content>
<content>He had asked Irene whether she wanted him; she had answered with that maddening smile of hers "Oh, no!"</content>
<content>Why had he come?</content>
<content>For the last quarter of an hour he had not even seen her.</content>
<content>Here was George advancing with his Quilpish face; it was too late to get out of his way.</content>
<content>"Have you seen 'The Buccaneer'?"</content>
<content>said this licensed wag; "he's on the warpath - hair cut and everything!"</content>
<content>Soames said he had not, and crossing the room, half-empty in an interval of the dance, he went out on the balcony, and looked down into the street.</content>
<content>A carriage had driven up with late arrivals, and round the door hung some of those patient watchers of the London streets who spring up to the call of light or music; their faces, pale and upturned above their black and rusty figures, had an air of stolid watching that annoyed Soames.</content>
<content>Why were they allowed to hang about; why didn't  the bobby move them on?</content>
<content>But the policeman took no notice of them; his feet were planted apart on the strip of crimson carpet stretched across the pavement; his face, under the helmet, wore the same stolid, watching look as theirs.</content>
<content>Across the road, through the railings, Soames could see the branches of trees shining, faintly stirring in the breeze, by the gleam of the street lamps; beyond, again, the upper lights of the houses on the other side, so many eyes looking down on the quiet blackness of the garden; and over all, the sky, that wonderful London sky, dusted with the innumerable reflection of countless lamps; a dome woven over between its stars with the refraction of human needs and human fancies - immense mirror of pomp and misery that night after night stretches its kindly mocking over miles of houses and gardens, mansions and squalor, over Forsytes, policemen, and patient watchers in the streets.</content>
<content>Soames turned away, and, hidden in the recess, gazed into the lighted room.</content>
<content>It was cooler out there.</content>
<content>He saw the new arrivals, June and her grandfather, enter.</content>
<content>What had made them so late?</content>
<content>They stood by the doorway.</content>
<content>They looked fagged.</content>
<content>Fancy Uncle Jolyon turning out at this time of night!</content>
<content>Why hadn't  June come to Irene, as she usually did, and it occurred to him suddenly that he had seen nothing of June for a long time now.</content>
<content>Watching her face with idle malice, he saw it change, grow so pale that he thought she would drop, then flame out crimson.</content>
<content>Turning to see at what she was looking, he saw his wife on Bosinney's arm, coming from the conservatory at the end of the room.</content>
<content>Her eyes were raised to his, as though answering some question he had asked, and he was gazing at her intently.</content>
<content>Soames looked again at June.</content>
<content>Her hand rested on old Jolyon's arm; she seemed to be making a request.</content>
<content>He saw a surprised look on his uncle's face; they turned and passed through the door out of his sight.</content>
<content>The music began again - a waltz - and, still as a statue in the recess of the window, his face unmoved, but no smile on his lips, Soames waited.</content>
<content>Presently, within a yard of the dark balcony, his wife and Bosinney passed.</content>
<content>He caught the perfume of the gardenias that she wore, saw the rise and fall of her bosom, the languor in her eyes, her parted lips, and a look on her face that he did not know.</content>
<content>To the slow, swinging measure they danced by, and it seemed to him that they clung to each other; he saw her raise her eyes, soft and dark, to Bosinney's, and drop them again.</content>
<content>Very white, he turned back to the balcony, and leaning on it, gazed down on the Square; the figures were still there looking up at the light with dull persistency, the policeman's face, too, upturned, and staring, but he saw nothing of them.</content>
<content>Below, a carriage drew up, two figures got in, and drove away....
That evening June and old Jolyon sat down to dinner at the usual hour.</content>
<content>The girl was in her customary high-necked frock, old Jolyon had not dressed.</content>
<content>At breakfast she had spoken of the dance at Uncle Roger's, she wanted to go; she had been stupid enough, she said, not to think of asking anyone to take her.</content>
<content>It was too late now.</content>
<content>Old Jolyon lifted his keen eyes.</content>
<content>June was used to go to dances with Irene as a matter of course!</content>
<content>and deliberately fixing his gaze on her, he asked: "Why don't  you get Irene?"</content>
<content>No!</content>
<content>June did not want to ask Irene; she would only go if - if her grandfather wouldn't  mind just for once for a little time!</content>
<content>At her look, so eager and so worn, old Jolyon had grumblingly consented.</content>
<content>He did not know what she wanted, he said, with going to a dance like this, a poor affair, he would wager; and she no more fit for it than a cat!</content>
<content>What she wanted was sea air, and after his general meeting of the Globular Gold Concessions he was ready to take her.</content>
<content>She didn't  want to go away?</content>
<content>Ah!</content>
<content>she would knock herself up!</content>
<content>Stealing a mournful look at her, he went on with his breakfast.</content>
<content>June went out early, and wandered restlessly about in the heat.</content>
<content>Her little light figure that lately had moved so languidly about its business, was all on fire.</content>
<content>She bought herself some flowers.</content>
<content>She wanted - she meant to look her best.</content>
<content>He would be there!</content>
<content>She knew well enough that he had a card.</content>
<content>She would show him that she did not care.</content>
<content>But deep down in her heart she resolved that evening to win him back.</content>
<content>She came in flushed, and talked brightly all lunch; old Jolyon was there, and he was deceived.</content>
<content>In the afternoon she was overtaken by a desperate fit of sobbing.</content>
<content>She strangled the noise against the pillows of her bed, but when at last it ceased she saw in the glass a swollen face with reddened eyes, and violet circles round them.</content>
<content>She stayed in the darkened room till dinner time.</content>
<content>All through that silent meal the struggle went on within her.</content>
<content>She looked so shadowy and exhausted that old Jolyon told 'Sankey' to countermand the carriage, he would not have her going out.... She was to go to bed!</content>
<content>She made no resistance.</content>
<content>She went up to her room, and sat in the dark.</content>
<content>At ten o'clock she rang for her maid.</content>
<content>"Bring some hot water, and go down and tell Mr. Forsyte that I feel perfectly rested.</content>
<content>Say that if he's too tired I can go to the dance by myself."</content>
<content>The maid looked askance, and June turned on her imperiously.</content>
<content>"Go," she said, "bring the hot water at once!"</content>
<content>Her ball-dress still lay on the sofa, and with a sort of fierce care she arrayed herself, took the flowers in her hand, and went down, her small face carried high under its burden of hair.</content>
<content>She could hear old Jolyon in his room as she passed.</content>
<content>Bewildered and vexed, he was dressing.</content>
<content>It was past ten, they would not get there till eleven; the girl was mad.</content>
<content>But he dared not cross her - the expression of her face at dinner haunted him.</content>
<content>With great ebony brushes he smoothed his hair till it shone like silver under the light; then he, too, came out on the gloomy staircase.</content>
<content>June met him below, and, without a word, they went to the carriage.</content>
<content>When, after that drive which seemed to last for ever, she entered Roger's drawing-room, she disguised under a mask of resolution a very torment of nervousness and emotion.</content>
<content>The feeling of shame at what might be called 'running after him' was smothered by the dread that he might not be there, that she might not see him after all, and by that dogged resolve - somehow, she did not know how - to win him back.</content>
<content>The sight of the ballroom, with its gleaming floor, gave her a feeling of joy, of triumph, for she loved dancing, and when dancing she floated, so light was she, like a strenuous, eager little spirit.</content>
<content>He would surely ask her to dance, and if he danced with her it would all be as it was before.</content>
<content>She looked about her eagerly.</content>
<content>The sight of Bosinney coming with Irene from the conservatory, with that strange look of utter absorption on his face, struck her too suddenly.</content>
<content>They had not seen - no one should see - her distress, not even her grandfather.</content>
<content>She put her hand on Jolyon's arm, and said very low:
"I must go home, Gran; I feel ill."
He hurried her away, grumbling to himself that he had known how it would be.</content>
<content>To her he said nothing; only when they were once more in the carriage, which by some fortunate chance had lingered near the door, he asked her: "What is it, my darling?"</content>
<content>Feeling her whole slender body shaken by sobs, he was terribly alarmed.</content>
<content>She must have Blank to-morrow.</content>
<content>He would insist upon it.</content>
<content>He could not have her like this....</content>
<content>There, there!</content>
<content>June mastered her sobs, and squeezing his hand feverishly, she lay back in her corner, her face muffled in a shawl.</content>
<content>He could only see her eyes, fixed and staring in the dark, but he did not cease to stroke her hand with his thin fingers.</content>
<content>CHAPTER IX - EVENING AT RICHMOND
Other eyes besides the eyes of June and of Soames had seen 'those two'  as Euphemia had already begun to call them  coming from the conservatory; other eyes had noticed the look on Bosinney's face.</content>
<content>There are moments when Nature reveals the passion hidden beneath the careless calm of her ordinary moods - violent spring flashing white on almond-blossom through the purple clouds; a snowy, moonlit peak, with its single star, soaring up to the passionate blue; or against the flames of sunset, an old yew-tree standing dark guardian of some fiery secret.</content>
<content>There are moments, too, when in a picture-gallery, a work, noted by the casual spectator as '......Titian - remarkably fine,' breaks through the defences of some Forsyte better lunched perhaps than his fellows, and holds him spellbound in a kind of ecstasy.</content>
<content>There are things, he feels - there are things here which - well, which are things.</content>
<content>Something unreasoning, unreasonable, is upon him; when he tries to define it with the precision of a practical man, it eludes him, slips away, as the glow of the wine he has drunk is slipping away, leaving him cross, and conscious of his liver.</content>
<content>He feels that he has been extravagant, prodigal of something; virtue has gone out of him.</content>
<content>He did not desire this glimpse of what lay under the three stars of his catalogue.</content>
<content>God forbid that he should know anything about the forces of Nature!</content>
<content>God forbid that he should admit for a moment that there are such things!</content>
<content>Once admit that, and where was he?</content>
<content>One paid a shilling for entrance, and another for the programme.</content>
<content>The look which June had seen, which other Forsytes had seen, was like the sudden flashing of a candle through a hole in some imaginary canvas, behind which it was being moved - the sudden flaming-out of a vague, erratic glow, shadowy and enticing.</content>
<content>It brought home to onlookers the consciousness that dangerous forces were at work.</content>
<content>For a moment they noticed it with pleasure, with interest, then felt they must not notice it at all.</content>
<content>It supplied, however, the reason of June's coming so late and disappearing again without dancing, without even shaking hands with her lover.</content>
<content>She was ill, it was said, and no wonder.</content>
<content>But here they looked at each other guiltily.</content>
<content>They had no desire to spread scandal, no desire to be ill-natured.</content>
<content>Who would have?</content>
<content>And to outsiders no word was breathed, unwritten law keeping them silent.</content>
<content>Then came the news that June had gone to the seaside with old Jolyon.</content>
<content>He had carried her off to Broadstairs, for which place there was just then a feeling, Yarmouth having lost caste, in spite of Nicholas, and no Forsyte going to the sea without intending to have an air for his money such as would render him bilious in a week.</content>
<content>That fatally aristocratic tendency of the first Forsyte to drink Madeira had left his descendants undoubtedly accessible.</content>
<content>So June went to the sea.</content>
<content>The family awaited developments; there was nothing else to do.</content>
<content>But how far - how far had 'those two' gone?</content>
<content>How far were they going to go?</content>
<content>Could they really be going at all?</content>
<content>Nothing could surely come of it, for neither of them had any money.</content>
<content>At the most a flirtation, ending, as all such attachments should, at the proper time.</content>
<content>Soames' sister, Winifred Dartie, who had imbibed with the breezes of Mayfair - she lived in Green Street - more fashionable principles in regard to matrimonial behaviour than were current, for instance, in Ladbroke Grove, laughed at the idea of there being anything in it.</content>
<content>The 'little thing' - Irene was taller than herself, and it was real testimony to the solid worth of a Forsyte that she should always thus be a 'little thing' - the little thing was bored.</content>
<content>Why shouldn't  she amuse herself?</content>
<content>Soames was rather tiring; and as to Mr. Bosinney - only that buffoon George would have called him the Buccaneer - she maintained that he was very chic.</content>
<content>This dictum - that Bosinney was chic - caused quit a sensation.</content>
<content>It failed to convince.</content>
<content>That he was 'good-looking in a way' they were prepared to admit, but that anyone could call a man with his pronounced cheekbones, curious eyes, and soft felt hats chic was only another instance of Winifred's extravagant way of running after something new.</content>
<content>It was that famous summer when extravagance was fashionable, when the very earth was extravagant, chestnut-trees spread with blossom, and flowers drenched in perfume, as they had never been before; when roses blew in every garden; and for the swarming stars the nights had hardly space; when every day and all day long the sun, in full armour, swung his brazen shield above the Park, and people did strange things, lunching and dining in the open air.</content>
<content>Unprecedented was the tale of cabs and carriages that streamed across the bridges of the shining river, bearing the upper-middle class in thousands to the green glories of Bushey, Richmond, Kew, and Hampton Court.</content>
<content>Almost every family with any pretensions to be of the carriage-class paid one visit that year to the horse-chestnuts at Bushey, or took one drive amongst the Spanish chestnuts of Richmond Park.</content>
<content>Bowling smoothly, if dustily, along, in a cloud of their own creation, they would stare fashionably at the antlered heads which the great slow deer raised out of a forest of bracken that promised to autumn lovers such cover as was never seen before.</content>
<content>And now and again, as the amorous perfume of chestnut flowers and of fern was drifted too near, one would say to the other: "My dear!</content>
<content>What a peculiar scent!"</content>
<content>And the lime-flowers that year were of rare prime, near honey-coloured.</content>
<content>At the corners of London squares they gave out, as the sun went down, a perfume sweeter than the honey bees had taken - a perfume that stirred a yearning unnamable in the hearts of Forsytes and their peers, taking the cool after dinner in the precincts of those gardens to which they alone had keys.</content>
