<content>Brutality is not so deplorably diluted by humaneness as it used to be, yet a sentimental segment of the population may still be relieved to learn that he did none of these things.</content>
<content>For active brutality is not popular with Forsytes; they are too circumspect, and, on the whole, too softhearted.</content>
<content>And in Soames there was some common pride, not sufficient to make him do a really generous action, but enough to prevent his indulging in an extremely mean one, except, perhaps, in very hot blood.</content>
<content>Above all this a true Forsyte refused to feel himself ridiculous.</content>
<content>Short of actually beating his wife, he perceived nothing to be done; he therefore accepted the situation without another word.</content>
<content>Throughout the summer and autumn he continued to go to the office, to sort his pictures, and ask his friends to dinner.</content>
<content>He did not leave town; Irene refused to go away.</content>
<content>The house at Robin Hill, finished though it was, remained empty and ownerless.</content>
<content>Soames had brought a suit against the Buccaneer, in which he claimed from him the sum of three hundred and fifty pounds.</content>
<content>A firm of solicitors, Messrs. Freak and Able, had put in a defence on Bosinney's behalf.</content>
<content>Admitting the facts, they raised a point on the correspondence which, divested of legal phraseology, amounted to this: To speak of 'a free hand in the terms of this correspondence' is an Irish bull.</content>
<content>By a chance, fortuitous but not improbable in the close borough of legal circles, a good deal of information came to Soames' ear anent this line of policy, the working partner in his firm, Bustard, happening to sit next at dinner at Walmisley's, the Taxing Master, to young Chankery, of the Common Law Bar.</content>
<content>The necessity for talking what is known as 'shop,' which comes on all lawyers with the removal of the ladies, caused Chankery, a young and promising advocate, to propound an impersonal conundrum to his neighbour, whose name he did not know, for, seated as he permanently was in the background, Bustard had practically no name.</content>
<content>He had, said Chankery, a case coming on with a 've ry nice point.'</content>
<content>He then explained, preserving every professional discretion, the riddle in Soames' case.</content>
<content>Everyone, he said, to whom he had spoken, thought it a nice point.</content>
<content>The issue was small unfortunately, 'though d - - d serious for his client he believed' - Walmisley's champagne was bad but plentiful.</content>
<content>A Judge would make short work of it, he was afraid.</content>
<content>He intended to make a big effort - the point was a nice one.</content>
<content>What did his neighbour say?</content>
<content>Bustard, a model of secrecy, said nothing.</content>
<content>He related the incident to Soames however with some malice, for this quiet man was capable of human feeling, ending with his own opinion that the point was 'a very nice one.'</content>
<content>In accordance with his resolve, our Forsyte had put his interests into the hands of Jobling and Boulter.</content>
<content>From the moment of doing so he regretted that he had not acted for himself.</content>
<content>On receiving a copy of Bosinney's defence he went over to their offices.</content>
<content>Boulter, who had the matter in hand, Jobling having died some years before, told him that in his opinion it was rather a nice point; he would like counsel's opinion on it.</content>
<content>Soames told him to go to a good man, and they went to Waterbuck, Q.C., marking him ten and one, who kept the papers six weeks and then wrote as follows:
'In my opinion the true interpretation of this correspondence depends very much on the intention of the parties, and will turn upon the evidence given at the trial.</content>
<content>I am of opinion that an attempt should be made to secure from the architect an admission that he understood he was not to spend at the outside more than twelve thousand and fifty pounds.</content>
<content>With regard to the expression, "a free hand in the terms of this correspondence," to which my attention is directed, the point is a nice one; but I am of opinion that upon the whole the ruling in "Boileau v. The Blasted Cement Co., Ltd.," will apply.'</content>
<content>Upon this opinion they acted, administering interrogatories, but to their annoyance Messrs. Freak and Able answered these in so masterly a fashion that nothing whatever was admitted and that without prejudice.</content>
<content>It was on October 1 that Soames read Waterbuck's opinion, in the dining-room before dinner.</content>
<content>It made him nervous; not so much because of the case of 'Boileau v. The Blasted Cement Co., Ltd.,' as that the point had lately begun to seem to him, too, a nice one; there was about it just that pleasant flavour of subtlety so attractive to the best legal appetites.</content>
<content>To have his own impression confirmed by Waterbuck, Q.C., would have disturbed any man.</content>
<content>He sat thinking it over, and staring at the empty grate, for though autumn had come, the weather kept as gloriously fine that jubilee year as if it were still high August.</content>
<content>It was not pleasant to be disturbed; he desired too passionately to set his foot on Bosinney's neck.</content>
<content>Though he had not seen the architect since the last afternoon at Robin Hill, he was never free from the sense of his presence - never free from the memory of his worn face with its high cheek bones and enthusiastic eyes.</content>
<content>It would not be too much to say that he had never got rid of the feeling of that night when he heard the peacock's cry at dawn - the feeling that Bosinney haunted the house.</content>
<content>And every man's shape that he saw in the dark evenings walking past, seemed that of him whom George had so appropriately named the Buccaneer.</content>
<content>Irene still met him, he was certain; where, or how, he neither knew, nor asked; deterred by a vague and secret dread of too much knowledge.</content>
<content>It all seemed subterranean nowadays.</content>
<content>Sometimes when he questioned his wife as to where she had been, which he still made a point of doing, as every Forsyte should, she looked very strange.</content>
<content>Her self-possession was wonderful, but there were moments when, behind the mask of her face, inscrutable as it had always been to him, lurked an expression he had never been used to see there.</content>
<content>She had taken to lunching out too; when he asked Bilson if her mistress had been in to lunch, as often as not she would answer: "No, sir."</content>
<content>He strongly disapproved of her gadding about by herself, and told her so.</content>
<content>But she took no notice.</content>
<content>There was something that angered, amazed, yet almost amused him about the calm way in which she disregarded his wishes.</content>
<content>It was really as if she were hugging to herself the thought of a triumph over him.</content>
<content>He rose from the perusal of Waterbuck, Q.C.</content>
<content>'s opinion, and, going upstairs, entered her room, for she did not lock her doors till bed-time - she had the decency, he found, to save the feelings of the servants.</content>
<content>She was brushing her hair, and turned to him with strange fierceness.</content>
<content>"What do you want?"</content>
<content>she said.</content>
<content>"Please leave my room!"</content>
<content>He answered: "I want to know how long this state of things between us is to last?</content>
<content>I have put up with it long enough."</content>
<content>"Will you please leave my room?"</content>
<content>"Will you treat me as your husband?"</content>
<content>"No."</content>
<content>"Then, I shall take steps to make you."</content>
<content>"Do!"</content>
<content>He stared, amazed at the calmness of her answer.</content>
<content>Her lips were compressed in a thin line; her hair lay in fluffy masses on her bare shoulders, in all its strange golden contrast to her dark eyes - those eyes alive with the emotions of fear, hate, contempt, and odd, haunting triumph.</content>
<content>"Now, please, will you leave my room?"</content>
<content>He turned round, and went sulkily out.</content>
<content>He knew very well that he had no intention of taking steps, and he saw that she knew too - knew that he was afraid to.</content>
<content>It was a habit with him to tell her the doings of his day: how such and such clients had called; how he had arranged a mortgage for Parkes; how that long-standing suit of Fryer v. Forsyte was getting on, which, arising in the preternaturally careful disposition of his property by his great uncle Nicholas, who had tied it up so that no one could get at it at all, seemed likely to remain a source of income for several solicitors till the Day of Judgment.</content>
<content>And how he had called in at Jobson's, and seen a Boucher sold, which he had just missed buying of Talleyrand and Sons in Pall Mall.</content>
<content>He had an admiration for Boucher, Watteau, and all that school.</content>
<content>It was a habit with him to tell her all these matters, and he continued to do it even now, talking for long spells at dinner, as though by the volubility of words he could conceal from himself the ache in his heart.</content>
<content>Often, if they were alone, he made an attempt to kiss her when she said good-night.</content>
<content>He may have had some vague notion that some night she would let him; or perhaps only the feeling that a husband ought to kiss his wife.</content>
<content>Even if she hated him, he at all events ought not to put himself in the wrong by neglecting this ancient rite.</content>
<content>And why did she hate him?</content>
<content>Even now he could not altogether believe it.</content>
<content>It was strange to be hated!</content>
<content>- the emotion was too extreme; yet he hated Bosinney, that Buccaneer, that prowling vagabond, that night-wanderer.</content>
<content>For in his thoughts Soames always saw him lying in wait - wandering.</content>
<content>Ah, but he must be in very low water!</content>
<content>Young Burkitt, the architect, had seen him coming out of a third-rate restaurant, looking terribly down in the mouth!</content>
<content>During all the hours he lay awake, thinking over the situation, which seemed to have no end - unless she should suddenly come to her senses - never once did the thought of separating from his wife seriously enter his head....
And the Forsytes!</content>
<content>What part did they play in this stage of Soames' subterranean tragedy?</content>
<content>Truth to say, little or none, for they were at the sea.</content>
<content>From hotels, hydropathics, or lodging-houses, they were bathing daily; laying in a stock of ozone to last them through the winter.</content>
<content>Each section, in the vineyard of its own choosing, grew and culled and pressed and bottled the grapes of a pet sea-air.</content>
<content>The end of September began to witness their several returns.</content>
<content>In rude health and small omnibuses, with considerable colour in their cheeks, they arrived daily from the various termini.</content>
<content>The following morning saw them back at their vocations.</content>
<content>On the next Sunday Timothy's was thronged from lunch till dinner.</content>
<content>Amongst other gossip, too numerous and interesting to relate, Mrs. Septimus Small mentioned that Soames and Irene had not been away.</content>
<content>It remained for a comparative outsider to supply the next evidence of interest.</content>
<content>It chanced that one afternoon late in September, Mrs. MacAnder, Winifred Dartie's greatest friend, taking a constitutional, with young Augustus Flippard, on her bicycle in Richmond Park, passed Irene and Bosinney walking from the bracken towards the Sheen Gate.</content>
<content>Perhaps the poor little woman was thirsty, for she had ridden long on a hard, dry road, and, as all London knows, to ride a bicycle and talk to young Flippard will try the toughest constitution; or perhaps the sight of the cool bracken grove, whence 'those two' were coming down, excited her envy.</content>
<content>The cool bracken grove on the top of the hill, with the oak boughs for roof, where the pigeons were raising an endless wedding hymn, and the autumn, humming, whispered to the ears of lovers in the fern, while the deer stole by.</content>
<content>The bracken grove of irretrievable delights, of golden minutes in the long marriage of heaven and earth!</content>
<content>The bracken grove, sacred to stags, to strange tree-stump fauns leaping around the silver whiteness of a birch-tree nymph at summer dusk.</content>
<content>This lady knew all the Forsytes, and having been at June's 'at home,' was not at a loss to see with whom she had to deal.</content>
<content>Her own marriage, poor thing, had not been successful, but having had the good sense and ability to force her husband into pronounced error, she herself had passed through the necessary divorce proceedings without incurring censure.</content>
<content>She was therefore a judge of all that sort of thing, and lived in one of those large buildings, where in small sets of apartments, are gathered incredible quantities of Forsytes, whose chief recreation out of business hours is the discussion of each other's affairs.</content>
<content>Poor little woman, perhaps she was thirsty, certainly she was bored, for Flippard was a wit.</content>
<content>To see 'those two' in so unlikely a spot was quite a merciful 'pick-me-up.'</content>
<content>At the MacAnder, like all London, Time pauses.</content>
<content>This small but remarkable woman merits attention; her all-seeing eye and shrewd tongue were inscrutably the means of furthering the ends of Providence.</content>
<content>With an air of being in at the death, she had an almost distressing power of taking care of herself.</content>
<content>She had done more, perhaps, in her way than any woman about town to destroy the sense of chivalry which still clogs the wheel of civilization.</content>
<content>So smart she was, and spoken of endearingly as 'the little MacAnder!'</content>
<content>Dressing tightly and well, she belonged to a Woman's Club, but was by no means the neurotic and dismal type of member who was always thinking of her rights.</content>
<content>She took her rights unconsciously, they came natural to her, and she knew exactly how to make the most of them without exciting anything but admiration amongst that great class to whom she was affiliated, not precisely perhaps by manner, but by birth, breeding, and the true, the secret gauge, a sense of property.</content>
<content>The daughter of a Bedfordshire solicitor, by the daughter of a clergyman, she had never, through all the painful experience of being married to a very mild painter with a cranky love of Nature, who had deserted her for an actress, lost touch with the requirements, beliefs, and inner feeling of Society; and, on attaining her liberty, she placed herself without effort in the very van of Forsyteism.</content>
<content>Always in good spirits, and 'full of information,' she was universally welcomed.</content>
<content>She excited neither surprise nor disapprobation when encountered on the Rhine or at Zermatt, either alone, or travelling with a lady and two gentlemen; it was felt that she was perfectly capable of taking care of herself; and the hearts of all Forsytes warmed to that wonderful instinct, which enabled her to enjoy everything without giving anything away.</content>
<content>It was generally felt that to such women as Mrs. MacAnder should we look for the perpetuation and increase of our best type of woman.</content>
<content>She had never had any children.</content>
<content>If there was one thing more than another that she could not stand it was one of those soft women with what men called 'charm' about them, and for Mrs. Soames she always had an especial dislike.</content>
<content>Obscurely, no doubt, she felt that if charm were once admitted as the criterion, smartness and capability must go to the wall; and she hated - with a hatred the deeper that at times this so-called charm seemed to disturb all calculations - the subtle seductiveness which she could not altogether overlook in Irene.</content>
<content>She said, however, that she could see nothing in the woman - there was no 'go' about her - she would never be able to stand up for herself - anyone could take advantage of her, that was plain - she could not see in fact what men found to admire!</content>
<content>She was not really ill-natured, but, in maintaining her position after the trying circumstances of her married life, she had found it so necessary to be 'full of information,' that the idea of holding her tongue about 'those two' in the Park never occurred to her.</content>
<content>And it so happened that she was dining that very evening at Timothy's, where she went sometimes to 'cheer the old things up,' as she was wont to put it.</content>
<content>The same people were always asked to meet her: Winifred Dartie and her husband; Francie, because she belonged to the artistic circles, for Mrs. MacAnder was known to contribute articles on dress to 'The Ladies Kingdom Come'; and for her to flirt with, provided they could be obtained, two of the Hayman boys, who, though they never said anything, were believed to be fast and thoroughly intimate with all that was latest in smart Society.</content>
<content>At twenty-five minutes past seven she turned out the electric light in her little hall, and wrapped in her opera cloak with the chinchilla collar, came out into the corridor, pausing a moment to make sure she had her latch-key.</content>
<content>These little self-contained flats were convenient; to be sure, she had no light and no air, but she could shut it up whenever she liked and go away.</content>
<content>There was no bother with servants, and she never felt tied as she used to when poor, dear Fred was always about, in his mooney way.</content>
<content>She retained no rancour against poor, dear Fred, he was such a fool; but the thought of that actress drew from her, even now, a little, bitter, derisive smile.</content>
<content>Firmly snapping the door to, she crossed the corridor, with its gloomy, yellow-ochre walls, and its infinite vista of brown, numbered doors.</content>
<content>The lift was going down; and wrapped to the ears in the high cloak, with every one of her auburn hairs in its place, she waited motionless for it to stop at her floor.</content>
<content>The iron gates clanked open; she entered.</content>
<content>There were already three occupants, a man in a great white waistcoat, with a large, smooth face like a baby's, and two old ladies in black, with mittened hands.</content>
<content>Mrs. MacAnder smiled at them; she knew everybody; and all these three, who had been admirably silent before, began to talk at once.</content>
<content>This was Mrs. MacAnder's successful secret.</content>
<content>She provoked conversation.</content>
<content>Throughout a descent of five stories the conversation continued, the lift boy standing with his back turned, his cynical face protruding through the bars.</content>
<content>At the bottom they separated, the man in the white waistcoat sentimentally to the billiard room, the old ladies to dine and say to each other: "A dear little woman!"</content>
<content>"Such a rattle!"</content>
<content>and Mrs. MacAnder to her cab.</content>
<content>When Mrs. MacAnder dined at Timothy's, the conversation  although Timothy himself could never be induced to be present  took that wider, man-of-the-world tone current among Forsytes at large, and this, no doubt, was what put her at a premium there.</content>
<content>Mrs. Small and Aunt Hester found it an exhilarating change.</content>
<content>"If only," they said, "Timothy would meet her!"</content>
<content>It was felt that she would do him good.</content>
<content>She could tell you, for instance, the latest story of Sir Charles Fiste's son at Monte Carlo; who was the real heroine of Tynemouth Eddy's fashionable novel that everyone was holding up their hands over, and what they were doing in Paris about wearing bloomers.</content>
<content>She was so sensible, too, knowing all about that vexed question, whether to send young Nicholas' eldest into the navy as his mother wished, or make him an accountant as his father thought would be safer.</content>
<content>She strongly deprecated the navy.</content>
<content>If you were not exceptionally brilliant or exceptionally well connected, they passed you over so disgracefully, and what was it after all to look forward to, even if you became an admiral - a pittance!</content>
<content>An accountant had many more chances, but let him be put with a good firm, where there was no risk at starting!</content>
<content>Sometimes she would give them a tip on the Stock Exchange; not that Mrs. Small or Aunt Hester ever took it.</content>
<content>They had indeed no money to invest; but it seemed to bring them into such exciting touch with the realities of life.</content>
<content>It was an event.</content>
<content>They would ask Timothy, they said.</content>
<content>But they never did, knowing in advance that it would upset him.</content>
<content>Surreptitiously, however, for weeks after they would look in that paper, which they took with respect on account of its really fashionable proclivities, to see whether 'Bright's Rubies' or 'The Woollen Mackintosh Company' were up or down.</content>
<content>Sometimes they could not find the name of the company at all; and they would wait until James or Roger or even Swithin came in, and ask them in voices trembling with curiosity how that 'Bolivia Lime and Speltrate' was doing - they could not find it in the paper.</content>
<content>And Roger would answer: "What do you want to know for?</content>
<content>Some trash!</content>
<content>You'll go burning your fingers - investing your money in lime, and things you know nothing about!</content>
<content>Who told you?"</content>
<content>and ascertaining what they had been told, he would go away, and, making inquiries in the City, would perhaps invest some of his own money in the concern.</content>
<content>It was about the middle of dinner, just in fact as the saddle of mutton had been brought in by Smither, that Mrs. MacAnder, looking airily round, said: "Oh!</content>
<content>and whom do you think I passed to-day in Richmond Park?</content>
<content>You'll never guess - Mrs. Soames and - Mr. Bosinney.</content>
<content>They must have been down to look at the house!"</content>
<content>Winifred Dartie coughed, and no one said a word.</content>
<content>It was the piece of evidence they had all unconsciously been waiting for.</content>
<content>To do Mrs. MacAnder justice, she had been to Switzerland and the Italian lakes with a party of three, and had not heard of Soames' rupture with his architect.</content>
<content>She could not tell, therefore, the profound impression her words would make.</content>
<content>Upright and a little flushed, she moved her small, shrewd eyes from face to face, trying to gauge the effect of her words.</content>
<content>On either side of her a Hayman boy, his lean, taciturn, hungry face turned towards his plate, ate his mutton steadily.</content>
<content>These two, Giles and Jesse, were so alike and so inseparable that they were known as the Dromios.</content>
<content>They never talked, and seemed always completely occupied in doing nothing.</content>
<content>It was popularly supposed that they were cramming for an important examination.</content>
<content>They walked without hats for long hours in the Gardens attached to their house, books in their hands, a fox-terrier at their heels, never saying a word, and smoking all the time.</content>
<content>Every morning, about fifty yards apart, they trotted down Campden Hill on two lean hacks, with legs as long as their own, and every morning about an hour later, still fifty yards apart, they cantered up again.</content>
<content>Every evening, wherever they had dined, they might be observed about half-past ten, leaning over the balustrade of the Alhambra promenade.</content>
<content>They were never seen otherwise than together; in this way passing their lives, apparently perfectly content.</content>
<content>Inspired by some dumb stirring within them of the feelings of gentlemen, they turned at this painful moment to Mrs. MacAnder, and said in precisely the same voice: "Have you seen the...?"</content>
<content>Such was her surprise at being thus addressed that she put down her fork; and Smither, who was passing, promptly removed her plate.</content>
<content>Mrs. MacAnder, however, with presence of mind, said instantly: "I must have a little more of that nice mutton."</content>
<content>But afterwards in the drawing - room she sat down by Mrs. Small, determined to get to the bottom of the matter.</content>
<content>And she began:
"What a charming woman, Mrs. Soames; such a sympathetic temperament!</content>
<content>Soames is a really lucky man!"</content>
<content>Her anxiety for information had not made sufficient allowance for that inner Forsyte skin which refuses to share its troubles with outsiders.</content>
<content>Mrs. Septimus Small, drawing herself up with a creak and rustle of her whole person, said, shivering in her dignity:
"My dear, it is a subject we do not talk about!"</content>
<content>CHAPTER II - NIGHT IN THE PARK
Although with her infallible instinct Mrs. Small had said the very thing to make her guest 'more intriguee than ever,' it is difficult to see how else she could truthfully have spoken.</content>
<content>It was not a subject which the Forsytes could talk about even among themselves - to use the word Soames had invented to characterize to himself the situation, it was 'subterranean.'</content>
<content>Yet, within a week of Mrs. MacAnder's encounter in Richmond Park, to all of them - save Timothy, from whom it was carefully kept - to James on his domestic beat from the Poultry to Park Lane, to George the wild one, on his daily adventure from the bow window at the Haversnake to the billiard room at the 'Red Pottle,' was it known that 'those two' had gone to extremes.</content>
<content>George  it was he who invented many of those striking expressions still current in fashionable circles  voiced the sentiment more accurately than any one when he said to his brother Eustace that 'the Buccaneer' was 'going it'; he expected Soames was about 'fed up.'</content>
<content>It was felt that he must be, and yet, what could be done?</content>
<content>He ought perhaps to take steps; but to take steps would be deplorable.</content>
<content>Without an open scandal which they could not see their way to recommending, it was difficult to see what steps could be taken.</content>
<content>In this impasse, the only thing was to say nothing to Soames, and nothing to each other; in fact, to pass it over.</content>
<content>By displaying towards Irene a dignified coldness, some impression might be made upon her; but she was seldom now to be seen, and there seemed a slight difficulty in seeking her out on purpose to show her coldness.</content>
<content>Sometimes in the privacy of his bedroom James would reveal to Emily the real suffering that his son's misfortune caused him.</content>
<content>"I can't  tell," he would say; "it worries me out of my life.</content>
<content>There'll be a scandal, and that'll do him no good.</content>
<content>I shan't  say anything to him.</content>
<content>There might be nothing in it.</content>
<content>What do you think?</content>
<content>She's very artistic, they tell me.</content>
<content>What?</content>
<content>Oh, you're a 'regular Juley!</content>
<content>Well, I don't  know; I expect the worst.</content>
<content>This is what comes of having no children.</content>
<content>I knew how it would be from the first.</content>
<content>They never told me they didn't  mean to have any children - nobody tells me anything!"</content>
<content>On his knees by the side of the bed, his eyes open and fixed with worry, he would breathe into the counterpane.</content>
<content>Clad in his nightshirt, his neck poked forward, his back rounded, he resembled some long white bird.</content>
<content>"Our Father-," he repeated, turning over and over again the thought of this possible scandal.</content>
<content>Like old Jolyon, he, too, at the bottom of his heart set the blame of the tragedy down to family interference.</content>
<content>What business had that lot - he began to think of the Stanhope Gate branch, including young Jolyon and his daughter, as 'that lot' - to introduce a person like this Bosinney into the family?</content>
<content>He had heard George's soubriquet, 'The Buccaneer,' but he could make nothing of that - the young man was an architect.</content>
<content>He began to feel that his brother Jolyon, to whom he had always looked up and on whose opinion he had relied, was not quite what he had expected.</content>
<content>Not having his eldest brother's force of character, he was more sad than angry.</content>
<content>His great comfort was to go to Winifred's, and take the little Darties in his carriage over to Kensington Gardens, and there, by the Round Pond, he could often be seen walking with his eyes fixed anxiously on little Publius Dartie's sailing-boat, which he had himself freighted with a penny, as though convinced that it would never again come to shore; while little Publius - who, James delighted to say, was not a bit like his father skipping along under his lee, would try to get him to bet another that it never would, having found that it always did.</content>
<content>And James would make the bet; he always paid - sometimes as many as three or four pennies in the afternoon, for the game seemed never to pall on little Publius - and always in paying he said: "Now, that's for your money-box.</content>
<content>Why, you're getting quite a rich man!"</content>
<content>The thought of his little grandson's growing wealth was a real pleasure to him.</content>
<content>But little Publius knew a sweet-shop, and a trick worth two of that.</content>
<content>And they would walk home across the Park, James' figure, with high shoulders and absorbed and worried face, exercising its tall, lean protectorship, pathetically unregarded, over the robust child-figures of Imogen and little Publius.</content>
<content>But those Gardens and that Park were not sacred to James.</content>
<content>Forsytes and tramps, children and lovers, rested and wandered day after day, night after night, seeking one and all some freedom from labour, from the reek and turmoil of the streets.</content>
<content>The leaves browned slowly, lingering with the sun and summer-like warmth of the nights.</content>
<content>On Saturday, October 5, the sky that had been blue all day deepened after sunset to the bloom of purple grapes.</content>
<content>There was no moon, and a clear dark, like some velvety garment, was wrapped around the trees, whose thinned branches, resembling plumes, stirred not in the still, warm air.</content>
<content>All London had poured into the Park, draining the cup of summer to its dregs.</content>
<content>Couple after couple, from every gate, they streamed along the paths and over the burnt grass, and one after another, silently out of the lighted spaces, stole into the shelter of the feathery trees, where, blotted against some trunk, or under the shadow of shrubs, they were lost to all but themselves in the heart of the soft darkness.</content>
<content>To fresh-comers along the paths, these forerunners formed but part of that passionate dusk, whence only a strange murmur, like the confused beating of hearts, came forth.</content>
<content>But when that murmur reached each couple in the lamp-light their voices wavered, and ceased; their arms enlaced, their eyes began seeking, searching, probing the blackness.</content>
<content>Suddenly, as though drawn by invisible hands, they, too, stepped over the railing, and, silent as shadows, were gone from the light.</content>
<content>The stillness, enclosed in the far, inexorable roar of the town, was alive with the myriad passions, hopes, and loves of multitudes of struggling human atoms; for in spite of the disapproval of that great body of Forsytes, the Municipal Council - to whom Love had long been considered, next to the Sewage Question, the gravest danger to the community - a process was going on that night in the Park, and in a hundred other parks, without which the thousand factories, churches, shops, taxes, and drains, of which they were custodians, were as arteries without blood, a man without a heart.</content>
<content>The instincts of self-forgetfulness, of passion, and of love, hiding under the trees, away from the trustees of their remorseless enemy, the 'sense of property,' were holding a stealthy revel, and Soames, returning from Bayswater for he had been alone to dine at Timothy's walking home along the water, with his mind upon that coming lawsuit, had the blood driven from his heart by a low laugh and the sound of kisses.</content>
<content>He thought of writing to the Times the next morning, to draw the attention of the Editor to the condition of our parks.</content>
<content>He did not, however, for he had a horror of seeing his name in print.</content>
<content>But starved as he was, the whispered sounds in the stillness, the half-seen forms in the dark, acted on him like some morbid stimulant.</content>
<content>He left the path along the water and stole under the trees, along the deep shadow of little plantations, where the boughs of chestnut trees hung their great leaves low, and there was blacker refuge, shaping his course in circles which had for their object a stealthy inspection of chairs side by side, against tree-trunks, of enlaced lovers, who stirred at his approach.</content>
<content>Now he stood still on the rise overlooking the Serpentine, where, in full lamp-light, black against the silver water, sat a couple who never moved, the woman's face buried on the man's neck - a single form, like a carved emblem of passion, silent and unashamed.</content>
<content>And, stung by the sight, Soames hurried on deeper into the shadow of the trees.</content>
<content>In this search, who knows what he thought and what he sought?</content>
<content>Bread for hunger - light in darkness?</content>
<content>Who knows what he expected to find - impersonal knowledge of the human heart - the end of his private subterranean tragedy - for, again, who knew, but that each dark couple, unnamed, unnameable, might not be he and she?</content>
<content>But it could not be such knowledge as this that he was seeking - the wife of Soames Forsyte sitting in the Park like a common wench!</content>
<content>Such thoughts were inconceivable; and from tree to tree, with his noiseless step, he passed.</content>
<content>Once he was sworn at; once the whisper, "If only it could always be like this!"</content>
<content>sent the blood flying again from his heart, and he waited there, patient and dogged, for the two to move.</content>
<content>But it was only a poor thin slip of a shop-girl in her draggled blouse who passed him, clinging to her lover's arm.</content>
<content>A hundred other lovers too whispered that hope in the stillness of the trees, a hundred other lovers clung to each other.</content>
<content>But shaking himself with sudden disgust, Soames returned to the path, and left that seeking for he knew not what.</content>
<content>CHAPTER III - MEETING AT THE BOTANICAL
Young Jolyon, whose circumstances were not those of a Forsyte, found at times a difficulty in sparing the money needful for those country jaunts and researches into Nature, without having prosecuted which no watercolour artist ever puts brush to paper.</content>
<content>He was frequently, in fact, obliged to take his colour-box into the Botanical Gardens, and there, on his stool, in the shade of a monkey-puzzler or in the lee of some India-rubber plant, he would spend long hours sketching.</content>
<content>An Art critic who had recently been looking at his work had delivered himself as follows:
"In a way your drawings are very good; tone and colour, in some of them certainly quite a feeling for Nature.</content>
<content>But, you see, they're so scattered; you'll never get the public to look at them.</content>
<content>Now, if you'd taken a definite subject, such as 'London by Night,' or 'The Crystal Palace in the Spring,' and made a regular series, the public would have known at once what they were looking at.</content>
<content>I can't  lay too much stress upon that.</content>
<content>All the men who are making great names in Art, like Crum Stone or Bleeder, are making them by avoiding the unexpected; by specializing and putting their works all in the same pigeon-hole, so that the public know pat once where to go.</content>
<content>And this stands to reason, for if a man's a collector he doesn't  want people to smell at the canvas to find out whom his pictures are by; he wants them to be able to say at once, 'A capital Forsyte!'</content>
<content>It is all the more important for you to be careful to choose a subject that they can lay hold of on the spot, since there's no very marked originality in your style."</content>
<content>Young Jolyon, standing by the little piano, where a bowl of dried rose leaves, the only produce of the garden, was deposited on a bit of faded damask, listened with his dim smile.</content>
<content>Turning to his wife, who was looking at the speaker with an angry expression on her thin face, he said:
"You see, dear?"</content>
<content>"I do not," she answered in her staccato voice, that still had a little foreign accent; "your style has originality."</content>
<content>The critic looked at her, smiled' deferentially, and said no more.</content>
<content>Like everyone else, he knew their history.</content>
<content>The words bore good fruit with young Jolyon; they were contrary to all that he believed in, to all that he theoretically held good in his Art, but some strange, deep instinct moved him against his will to turn them to profit.</content>
<content>He discovered therefore one morning that an idea had come to him for making a series of watercolour drawings of London.</content>
<content>How the idea had arisen he could not tell; and it was not till the following year, when he had completed and sold them at a very fair price, that in one of his impersonal moods, he found himself able to recollect the Art critic, and to discover in his own achievement another proof that he was a Forsyte.</content>
<content>He decided to commence with the Botanical Gardens, where he had already made so many studies, and chose the little artificial pond, sprinkled now with an autumn shower of red and yellow leaves, for though the gardeners longed to sweep them off, they could not reach them with their brooms.</content>
<content>The rest of the gardens they swept bare enough, removing every morning Nature's rain of leaves; piling them in heaps, whence from slow fires rose the sweet, acrid smoke that, like the cuckoo's note for spring, the scent of lime trees for the summer, is the true emblem of the fall.</content>
<content>The gardeners' tidy souls could not abide the gold and green and russet pattern on the grass.</content>
<content>The gravel paths must lie unstained, ordered, methodical, without knowledge of the realities of life, nor of that slow and beautiful decay which flings crowns underfoot to star the earth with fallen glories, whence, as the cycle rolls, will leap again wild spring.</content>
<content>Thus each leaf that fell was marked from the moment when it fluttered a good-bye and dropped, slow turning, from its twig.</content>
<content>But on that little pond the leaves floated in peace, and praised Heaven with their hues, the sunlight haunting over them.</content>
<content>And so young Jolyon found them.</content>
<content>Coming there one morning in the middle of October, he was disconcerted to find a bench about twenty paces from his stand occupied, for he had a proper horror of anyone seeing him at work.</content>
<content>A lady in a velvet jacket was sitting there, with her eyes fixed on the ground.</content>
<content>A flowering laurel, however, stood between, and, taking shelter behind this, young Jolyon prepared his easel.</content>
<content>His preparations were leisurely; he caught, as every true artist should, at anything that might delay for a moment the effort of his work, and he found himself looking furtively at this unknown dame.</content>
<content>Like his father before him, he had an eye for a face.</content>
<content>This face was charming!</content>
<content>He saw a rounded chin nestling in a cream ruffle, a delicate face with large dark eyes and soft lips.</content>
<content>A black 'picture' hat concealed the hair; her figure was lightly poised against the back of the bench, her knees were crossed; the tip of a patent-leather shoe emerged beneath her skirt.</content>
<content>There was something, indeed, inexpressibly dainty about the person of this lady, but young Jolyon's attention was chiefly riveted by the look on her face, which reminded him of his wife.</content>
<content>It was as though its owner had come into contact with forces too strong for her.</content>
<content>It troubled him, arousing vague feelings of attraction and chivalry.</content>
<content>Who was she?</content>
<content>And what doing there, alone?</content>
<content>Two young gentlemen of that peculiar breed, at once forward and shy, found in the Regent's Park, came by on their way to lawn tennis, and he noted with disapproval their furtive stares of admiration.</content>
<content>A loitering gardener halted to do something unnecessary to a clump of pampas grass; he, too, wanted an excuse for peeping.</content>
<content>A gentleman, old, and, by his hat, a professor of horticulture, passed three times to scrutinize her long and stealthily, a queer expression about his lips.</content>
<content>With all these men young Jolyon felt the same vague irritation.</content>
<content>She looked at none of them, yet was he certain that every man who passed would look at her like that.</content>
<content>Her face was not the face of a sorceress, who in every look holds out to men the offer of pleasure; it had none of the 'devil's beauty' so highly prized among the first Forsytes of the land; neither was it of that type, no less adorable, associated with the box of chocolate; it was not of the spiritually passionate, or passionately spiritual order, peculiar to house-decoration and modern poetry; nor did it seem to promise to the playwright material for the production of the interesting and neurasthenic figure, who commits suicide in the last act.</content>
<content>In shape and colouring, in its soft persuasive passivity, its sensuous purity, this woman's face reminded him of Titian's 'Heavenly Love,' a reproduction of which hung over the sideboard in his dining-room.</content>
<content>And her attraction seemed to be in this soft passivity, in the feeling she gave that to pressure she must yield.</content>
<content>For what or whom was she waiting, in the silence, with the trees dropping here and there a leaf, and the thrushes strutting close on grass, touched with the sparkle of the autumn rime?</content>
<content>Then her charming face grew eager, and, glancing round, with almost a lover's jealousy, young Jolyon saw Bosinney striding across the grass.</content>
<content>Curiously he watched the meeting, the look in their eyes, the long clasp of their hands.</content>
<content>They sat down close together, linked for all their outward discretion.</content>
<content>He heard the rapid murmur of their talk; but what they said he could not catch.</content>
<content>He had rowed in the galley himself!</content>
<content>He knew the long hours of waiting and the lean minutes of a half-public meeting; the tortures of suspense that haunt the unhallowed lover.</content>
<content>It required, however, but a glance at their two faces to see that this was none of those affairs of a season that distract men and women about town; none of those sudden appetites that wake up ravening, and are surfeited and asleep again in six weeks.</content>
<content>This was the real thing!</content>
<content>This was what had happened to himself!</content>
<content>Out of this anything might come!</content>
<content>Bosinney was pleading, and she so quiet, so soft, yet immovable in her passivity, sat looking over the grass.</content>
<content>Was he the man to carry her off, that tender, passive being, who would never stir a step for herself?</content>
<content>Who had given him all herself, and would die for him, but perhaps would never run away with him!</content>
<content>It seemed to young Jolyon that he could hear her saying: "But, darling, it would ruin you!"</content>
<content>For he himself had experienced to the full the gnawing fear at the bottom of each woman's heart that she is a drag on the man she loves.</content>
<content>And he peeped at them no more; but their soft, rapid talk came to his ears, with the stuttering song of some bird who seemed trying to remember the notes of spring: Joy - tragedy?</content>
<content>Which - which?</content>
<content>And gradually their talk ceased; long silence followed.</content>
<content>'And where does Soames come in?'</content>
<content>young Jolyon thought.</content>
<content>'People think she is concerned about the sin of deceiving her husband!</content>
<content>Little they know of women!</content>
<content>She's eating, after starvation - taking her revenge!</content>
<content>And Heaven help her - for he'll take his.'</content>
<content>He heard the swish of silk, and, spying round the laurel, saw them walking away, their hands stealthily joined....
At the end of July old Jolyon had taken his grand-daughter to the mountains; and on that visit  the last they ever paid  June recovered to a great extent her health and spirits.</content>
<content>In the hotels, filled with British Forsytes - for old Jolyon could not bear a 'set of Germans,' as he called all foreigners - she was looked upon with respect - the only grand-daughter of that fine-looking, and evidently wealthy, old Mr. Forsyte.</content>
<content>She did not mix freely with people - to mix freely with people was not June's habit - but she formed some friendships, and notably one in the Rhone Valley, with a French girl who was dying of consumption.</content>
<content>Determining at once that her friend should not die, she forgot, in the institution of a campaign against Death, much of her own trouble.</content>
<content>Old Jolyon watched the new intimacy with relief and disapproval; for this additional proof that her life was to be passed amongst 'lame ducks' worried him.</content>
<content>Would she never make a friendship or take an interest in something that would be of real benefit to her?</content>
<content>'Taking up with a parcel of foreigners,' he called it.</content>
<content>He often, however, brought home grapes or roses, and presented them to 'Mam'zelle' with an ingratiating twinkle.</content>
<content>Towards the end of September, in spite of June's disapproval, Mademoiselle Vigor breathed her last in the little hotel at St. Luc, to which they had moved her; and June took her defeat so deeply to heart that old Jolyon carried her away to Paris.</content>
<content>Here, in contemplation of the 'Venus de Milo' and the 'Madeleine,' she shook off her depression, and when, towards the middle of October, they returned to town, her grandfather believed that he had effected a cure.</content>
<content>No sooner, however, had they established themselves in Stanhope Gate than he perceived to his dismay a return of her old absorbed and brooding manner.</content>
<content>She would sit, staring in front of her, her chin on her hand, like a little Norse spirit, grim and intent, while all around in the electric light, then just installed, shone the great, drawing-room brocaded up to the frieze, full of furniture from Baple and Pullbred's.</content>
<content>And in the huge gilt mirror were reflected those Dresden china groups of young men in tight knee breeches, at the feet of full-bosomed ladies nursing on their laps pet lambs, which old Jolyon had bought when he was a bachelor and thought so highly of in these days of degenerate taste.</content>
<content>He was a man of most open mind, who, more than any Forsyte of them all, had moved with the times, but he could never forget that he had bought these groups at Jobson's, and given a lot of money for them.</content>
<content>He often said to June, with a sort of disillusioned contempt:
"You don't  care about them!</content>
<content>They're not the gimcrack things you and your friends like, but they cost me seventy pounds!"</content>
<content>He was not a man who allowed his taste to be warped when he knew for solid reasons that it was sound.</content>
<content>One of the first things that June did on getting home was to go round to Timothy's.</content>
<content>She persuaded herself that it was her duty to call there, and cheer him with an account of all her travels; but in reality she went because she knew of no other place where, by some random speech, or roundabout question, she could glean news of Bosinney.</content>
<content>They received her most cordially: And how was her dear grandfather?</content>
<content>He had not been to see them since May.</content>
<content>Her Uncle Timothy was very poorly, he had had a lot of trouble with the chimney-sweep in his bedroom; the stupid man had let the soot down the chimney!</content>
<content>It had quite upset her uncle.</content>
<content>June sat there a long time, dreading, yet passionately hoping, that they would speak of Bosinney.</content>
<content>But paralyzed by unaccountable discretion, Mrs. Septimus Small let fall no word, neither did she question June about him.</content>
<content>In desperation the girl asked at last whether Soames and Irene were in town - she had not yet been to see anyone.</content>
<content>It was Aunt Hester who replied: Oh, yes, they were in town, they had not been away at all.</content>
<content>There was some little difficulty about the house, she believed.</content>
<content>June had heard, no doubt!</content>
<content>She had better ask her Aunt Juley!</content>
<content>June turned to Mrs. Small, who sat upright in her chair, her hands clasped, her face covered with innumerable pouts.</content>
<content>In answer to the girl's look she maintained a strange silence, and when she spoke it was to ask June whether she had worn night-socks up in those high hotels where it must be so cold of a night.</content>
<content>June answered that she had not, she hated the stuffy things; and rose to leave.</content>
<content>Mrs. Small's infallibly chosen silence was far more ominous to her than anything that could have been said.</content>
<content>Before half an hour was over she had dragged the truth from Mrs. Baynes in Lowndes Square, that Soames was bringing an action against Bosinney over the decoration of the house.</content>
<content>Instead of disturbing her, the news had a strangely calming effect; as though she saw in the prospect of this struggle new hope for herself.</content>
<content>She learnt that the case was expected to come on in about a month, and there seemed little or no prospect of Bosinney's success.</content>
<content>"And whatever he'll do I can't  think," said Mrs. Baynes; "it's very dreadful for him, you know - he's got no money - he's very hard up.</content>
<content>And we can't  help him, I'm sure.</content>
<content>I'm told the money-lenders won't  lend if you have no security, and he has none - none at all."</content>
<content>Her embonpoint had increased of late; she was in the full swing of autumn organization, her writing-table literally strewn with the menus of charity functions.</content>
<content>She looked meaningly at June, with her round eyes of parrot-grey.</content>
<content>The sudden flush that rose on the girl's intent young face - she must have seen spring up before her a great hope - the sudden sweetness of her smile, often came back to Lady Baynes in after years  Baynes was knighted when he built that public Museum of Art which has given so much employment to officials, and so little pleasure to those working classes for whom it was designed .</content>
<content>The memory of that change, vivid and touching, like the breaking open of a flower, or the first sun after long winter, the memory, too, of all that came after, often intruded itself, unaccountably, inopportunely on Lady Baynes, when her mind was set upon the most important things.</content>
<content>This was the very afternoon of the day that young Jolyon witnessed the meeting in the Botanical Gardens, and on this day, too, old Jolyon paid a visit to his solicitors, Forsyte, Bustard, and Forsyte, in the Poultry.</content>
<content>Soames was not in, he had gone down to Somerset House; Bustard was buried up to the hilt in papers and that inaccessible apartment, where he was judiciously placed, in order that he might do as much work as possible; but James was in the front office, biting a finger, and lugubriously turning over the pleadings in Forsyte v. Bosinney.</content>
<content>This sound lawyer had only a sort of luxurious dread of the 'nice point,' enough to set up a pleasurable feeling of fuss; for his good practical sense told him that if he himself were on the Bench he would not pay much attention to it.</content>
<content>But he was afraid that this Bosinney would go bankrupt and Soames would have to find the money after all, and costs into the bargain.</content>
<content>And behind this tangible dread there was always that intangible trouble, lurking in the background, intricate, dim, scandalous, like a bad dream, and of which this action was but an outward and visible sign.</content>
<content>He raised his head as old Jolyon came in, and muttered: "How are you, Jolyon?</content>
<content>Haven't  seen you for an age.</content>
<content>You've  been to Switzerland, they tell me.</content>
<content>This young Bosinney, he's got himself into a mess.</content>
<content>I knew how it would be!"</content>
<content>He held out the papers, regarding his elder brother with nervous gloom.</content>
<content>Old Jolyon read them in silence, and while he read them James looked at the floor, biting his fingers the while.</content>
<content>Old Jolyon pitched them down at last, and they fell with a thump amongst a mass of affidavits in 're Buncombe, deceased,' one of the many branches of that parent and profitable tree, 'Fryer v.</content>
<content>Forsyte.'</content>
<content>"I don't  know what Soames is about," he said, "to make a fuss over a few hundred pounds.</content>
<content>I thought he was a man of property."</content>
<content>James' long upper lip twitched angrily; he could not bear his son to be attacked in such a spot.</content>
<content>"It's not the money," he began, but meeting his brother's glance, direct, shrewd, judicial, he stopped.</content>
<content>There was a silence.</content>
<content>"I've  come in for my Will," said old Jolyon at last, tugging at his moustache.</content>
<content>James' curiosity was roused at once.</content>
<content>Perhaps nothing in this life was more stimulating to him than a Will; it was the supreme deal with property, the final inventory of a man's belongings, the last word on what he was worth.</content>
<content>He sounded the bell.</content>
<content>"Bring in Mr. Jolyon's Will," he said to an anxious, dark-haired clerk.</content>
<content>"You going to make some alterations?"</content>
<content>And through his mind there flashed the thought: 'Now, am I worth as much as he?'</content>
<content>Old Jolyon put the Will in his breast pocket, and James twisted his long legs regretfully.</content>
<content>"You've  made some nice purchases lately, they tell me," he said.</content>
<content>"I don't  know where you get your information from," answered old Jolyon sharply.</content>
<content>"When's this action coming on?</content>
<content>Next month?</content>
<content>I can't  tell what you've  got in your minds.</content>
<content>You must manage your own affairs; but if you take my advice, you'll settle it out of Court.</content>
<content>Good-bye!"</content>
<content>With a cold handshake he was gone.</content>
<content>James, his fixed grey-blue eye corkscrewing round some secret anxious image, began again to bite his finger.</content>
<content>Old Jolyon took his Will to the offices of the New Colliery Company, and sat down in the empty Board Room to read it through.</content>
<content>He answered 'Down-by-the-starn' Hemmings so tartly when the latter, seeing his Chairman seated there, entered with the new Superintendent's first report, that the Secretary withdrew with regretful dignity; and sending for the transfer clerk, blew him up till the poor youth knew not where to look.</content>
<content>It was not - by George - as he  Down-by-the-starn  would have him know, for a whippersnapper of a young fellow like him, to come down to that office, and think that he was God Almighty.</content>
<content>He  Down-by-the-starn  had been head of that office for more years than a boy like him could count, and if he thought that when he had finished all his work, he could sit there doing nothing, he did not know him, Hemmings  Down-by-the-starn , and so forth.</content>
<content>On the other side of the green baize door old Jolyon sat at the long, mahogany-and-leather board table, his thick, loose-jointed, tortoiseshell eye-glasses perched on the bridge of his nose, his gold pencil moving down the clauses of his Will.</content>
<content>It was a simple affair, for there were none of those vexatious little legacies and donations to charities, which fritter away a man's possessions, and damage the majestic effect of that little paragraph in the morning papers accorded to Forsytes who die with a hundred thousand pounds.</content>
<content>A simple affair.</content>
<content>Just a bequest to his son of twenty thousand, and 'as to the residue of my property of whatsoever kind whether realty or personalty, or partaking of the nature of either - upon trust to pay the proceeds rents annual produce dividends or interest thereof and thereon to my said grand-daughter June Forsyte or her assigns during her life to be for her sole use and benefit and without, etc... and from and after her death or decease upon trust to convey assign transfer or make over the said last-mentioned lands hereditaments premises trust moneys stocks funds investments and securities or such as shall then stand for and represent the same unto such person or persons whether one or more for such intents purposes and uses and generally in such manner way and form in all respects as the said June Forsyte notwithstanding coverture shall by her last Will and Testament or any writing or writings in the nature of a Will testament or testamentary disposition to be by her duly made signed and published direct appoint or make over give and dispose of the same And in default etc....</content>
<content>Provided always...' and so on, in seven folios of brief and simple phraseology.</content>
<content>The Will had been drawn by James in his palmy days.</content>
<content>He had foreseen almost every contingency.</content>
<content>Old Jolyon sat a long time reading this Will; at last he took half a sheet of paper from the rack, and made a prolonged pencil note; then buttoning up the Will, he caused a cab to be called and drove to the offices of Paramor and Herring, in Lincoln's Inn Fields.</content>
<content>Jack Herring was dead, but his nephew was still in the firm, and old Jolyon was closeted with him for half an hour.</content>
<content>He had kept the hansom, and on coming out, gave the driver the address - 3, Wistaria Avenue.</content>
<content>He felt a strange, slow satisfaction, as though he had scored a victory over James and the man of property.</content>
<content>They should not poke their noses into his affairs any more; he had just cancelled their trusteeships of his Will; he would take the whole of his business out of their hands, and put it into the hands of young Herring, and he would move the business of his Companies too.</content>
<content>If that young Soames were such a man of property, he would never miss a thousand a year or so; and under his great white moustache old Jolyon grimly smiled.</content>
<content>He felt that what he was doing was in the nature of retributive justice, richly deserved.</content>
<content>Slowly, surely, with the secret inner process that works the destruction of an old tree, the poison of the wounds to his happiness, his will, his pride, had corroded the comely edifice of his philosophy.</content>
<content>Life had worn him down on one side, till, like that family of which he was the head, he had lost balance.</content>
<content>To him, borne northwards towards his son's house, the thought of the new disposition of property, which he had just set in motion, appeared vaguely in the light of a stroke of punishment, levelled at that family and that Society, of which James and his son seemed to him the representatives.</content>
<content>He had made a restitution to young Jolyon, and restitution to young Jolyon satisfied his secret craving for revenge-revenge against Time, sorrow, and interference, against all that incalculable sum of disapproval that had been bestowed by the world for fifteen years on his only son.</content>
<content>It presented itself as the one possible way of asserting once more the domination of his will; of forcing James, and Soames, and the family, and all those hidden masses of Forsytes - a great stream rolling against the single dam of his obstinacy - to recognise once and for all that he would be master.</content>
<content>It was sweet to think that at last he was going to make the boy a richer man by far than that son of James, that 'man of property.'</content>
<content>And it was sweet to give to Jo, for he loved his son.</content>
<content>Neither young Jolyon nor his wife were in  young Jolyon indeed was not back from the Botanical , but the little maid told him that she expected the master at any moment:
"He's always at 'ome to tea, sir, to play with the children."</content>
<content>Old Jolyon said he would wait; and sat down patiently enough in the faded, shabby drawing room, where, now that the summer chintzes were removed, the old chairs and sofas revealed all their threadbare deficiencies.</content>
<content>He longed to send for the children; to have them there beside him, their supple bodies against his knees; to hear Jolly's: "Hallo, Gran!"</content>
<content>and see his rush; and feel Holly's soft little hand stealing up against his cheek.</content>
<content>But he would not.</content>
<content>There was solemnity in what he had come to do, and until it was over he would not play.</content>
<content>He amused himself by thinking how with two strokes of his pen he was going to restore the look of caste so conspicuously absent from everything in that little house; how he could fill these rooms, or others in some larger mansion, with triumphs of art from Baple and Pullbred's; how he could send little Jolly to Harrow and Oxford  he no longer had faith in Eton and Cambridge, for his son had been there ; how he could procure little Holly the best musical instruction, the child had a remarkable aptitude.</content>
<content>As these visions crowded before him, causing emotion to swell his heart, he rose, and stood at the window, looking down into the little walled strip of garden, where the pear-tree, bare of leaves before its time, stood with gaunt branches in the slow-gathering mist of the autumn afternoon.</content>
<content>The dog Balthasar, his tail curled tightly over a piebald, furry back, was walking at the farther end, sniffing at the plants, and at intervals placing his leg for support against the wall.</content>
<content>And old Jolyon mused.</content>
<content>What pleasure was there left but to give?</content>
<content>It was pleasant to give, when you could find one who would be thankful for what you gave - one of your own flesh and blood!</content>
<content>There was no such satisfaction to be had out of giving to those who did not belong to you, to those who had no claim on you!</content>
<content>Such giving as that was a betrayal of the individualistic convictions and actions of his life, of all his enterprise, his labour, and his moderation, of the great and proud fact that, like tens of thousands of Forsytes before him, tens of thousands in the present, tens of thousands in the future, he had always made his own, and held his own, in the world.</content>
<content>And, while he stood there looking down on the smut-covered foliage of the laurels, the black-stained grass-plot, the progress of the dog Balthasar, all the suffering of the fifteen years during which he had been baulked of legitimate enjoyment mingled its gall with the sweetness of the approaching moment.</content>
<content>Young Jolyon came at last, pleased with his work, and fresh from long hours in the open air.</content>
<content>On hearing that his father was in the drawing room, he inquired hurriedly whether Mrs. Forsyte was at home, and being informed that she was not, heaved a sigh of relief.</content>
<content>Then putting his painting materials carefully in the little coat-closet out of sight, he went in.</content>
<content>With characteristic decision old Jolyon came at once to the point.</content>
<content>"I've  been altering my arrangements, Jo," he said.</content>
<content>"You can cut your coat a bit longer in the future - I'm settling a thousand a year on you at once.</content>
<content>June will have fifty thousand at my death; and you the rest.</content>
<content>That dog of yours is spoiling the garden.</content>
<content>I shouldn't  keep a dog, if I were you!"</content>
<content>The dog Balthasar, seated in the centre of the lawn, was examining his tail.</content>
<content>Young Jolyon looked at the animal, but saw him dimly, for his eyes were misty.</content>
<content>"Yours won't  come short of a hundred thousand, my boy," said old Jolyon; "I thought you'd better know.</content>
<content>I haven't  much longer to live at my age.</content>
<content>I shan't  allude to it again.</content>
<content>How's your wife?</content>
<content>And - give her my love."</content>
<content>Young Jolyon put his hand on his father's shoulder, and, as neither spoke, the episode closed.</content>
<content>Having seen his father into a hansom, young Jolyon came back to the drawing-room and stood, where old Jolyon had stood, looking down on the little garden.</content>
<content>He tried to realize all that this meant to him, and, Forsyte that he was, vistas of property were opened out in his brain; the years of half rations through which he had passed had not sapped his natural instincts.</content>
<content>In extremely practical form, he thought of travel, of his wife's costume, the children's education, a pony for Jolly, a thousand things; but in the midst of all he thought, too, of Bosinney and his mistress, and the broken song of the thrush.</content>
<content>Joy - tragedy!</content>
<content>Which?</content>
<content>Which?</content>
<content>The old past - the poignant, suffering, passionate, wonderful past, that no money could buy, that nothing could restore in all its burning sweetness - had come back before him.</content>
<content>When his wife came in he went straight up to her and took her in his arms; and for a long time he stood without speaking, his eyes closed, pressing her to him, while she looked at him with a wondering, adoring, doubting look in her eyes.</content>
<content>CHAPTER IV - VOYAGE INTO THE INFERNO
The morning after a certain night on which Soames at last asserted his rights and acted like a man, he breakfasted alone.</content>
<content>He breakfasted by gaslight, the fog of late November wrapping the town as in some monstrous blanket till the trees of the Square even were barely visible from the dining-room window.</content>
<content>He ate steadily, but at times a sensation as though he could not swallow attacked him.</content>
<content>Had he been right to yield to his overmastering hunger of the night before, and break down the resistance which he had suffered now too long from this woman who was his lawful and solemnly constituted helpmate?</content>
<content>He was strangely haunted by the recollection of her face, from before which, to soothe her, he had tried to pull her hands - of her terrible smothered sobbing, the like of which he had never heard, and still seemed to hear; and he was still haunted by the odd, intolerable feeling of remorse and shame he had felt, as he stood looking at her by the flame of the single candle, before silently slinking away.</content>
<content>And somehow, now that he had acted like this, he was surprised at himself.</content>
<content>Two nights before, at Winifred Dartie's, he had taken Mrs. MacAnder into dinner.</content>
<content>She had said to him, looking in his face with her sharp, greenish eyes: "And so your wife is a great friend of that Mr.</content>
<content>Bosinney's?"</content>
<content>Not deigning to ask what she meant, he had brooded over her words.</content>
<content>They had roused in him a fierce jealousy, which, with the peculiar perversion of this instinct, had turned to fiercer desire.</content>
<content>Without the incentive of Mrs. MacAnder's words he might never have done what he had done.</content>
<content>Without their incentive and the accident of finding his wife's door for once unlocked, which had enabled him to steal upon her asleep.</content>
<content>Slumber had removed his doubts, but the morning brought them again.</content>
<content>One thought comforted him: No one would know - it was not the sort of thing that she would speak about.</content>
<content>And, indeed, when the vehicle of his daily business life, which needed so imperatively the grease of clear and practical thought, started rolling once more with the reading of his letters, those nightmare-like doubts began to assume less extravagant importance at the back of his mind.</content>
<content>The incident was really not of great moment; women made a fuss about it in books; but in the cool judgment of right-thinking men, of men of the world, of such as he recollected often received praise in the Divorce Court, he had but done his best to sustain the sanctity of marriage, to prevent her from abandoning her duty, possibly, if she were still seeing Bosinney, from....
No, he did not regret it.</content>
<content>Now that the first step towards reconciliation had been taken, the rest would be comparatively - comparatively....</content>
<content>He, rose and walked to the window.</content>
<content>His nerve had been shaken.</content>
<content>The sound of smothered sobbing was in his ears again.</content>
<content>He could not get rid of it.</content>
<content>He put on his fur coat, and went out into the fog; having to go into the City, he took the underground railway from Sloane Square station.</content>
<content>In his corner of the first-class compartment filled with City men the smothered sobbing still haunted him, so he opened the Times with the rich crackle that drowns all lesser sounds, and, barricaded behind it, set himself steadily to con the news.</content>
<content>He read that a Recorder had charged a grand jury on the previous day with a more than usually long list of offences.</content>
<content>He read of three murders, five manslaughters, seven arsons, and as many as eleven rapes - a surprisingly high number - in addition to many less conspicuous crimes, to be tried during a coming Sessions; and from one piece of news he went on to another, keeping the paper well before his face.</content>
<content>And still, inseparable from his reading, was the memory of Irene's tear-stained face, and the sounds from her broken heart.</content>
<content>The day was a busy one, including, in addition to the ordinary affairs of his practice, a visit to his brokers, Messrs. Grin and Grinning, to give them instructions to sell his shares in the New Colliery Co., Ltd., whose business he suspected, rather than knew, was stagnating  this enterprise afterwards slowly declined, and was ultimately sold for a song to an American syndicate ; and a long conference at Waterbuck, Q.C.</content>
<content>'s chambers, attended by Boulter, by Fiske, the junior counsel, and Waterbuck, Q.C., himself.</content>
<content>The case of Forsyte v. Bosinney was expected to be reached on the morrow, before Mr. Justice Bentham.</content>
<content>Mr. Justice Bentham, a man of common-sense rather than too great legal knowledge, was considered to be about the best man they could have to try the action.</content>
<content>He was a 'strong' Judge.</content>
<content>Waterbuck, Q.C., in pleasing conjunction with an almost rude neglect of Boulter and Fiske paid to Soames a good deal of attention, by instinct or the sounder evidence of rumour, feeling him to be a man of property.</content>
<content>He held with remarkable consistency to the opinion he had already expressed in writing, that the issue would depend to a great extent on the evidence given at the trial, and in a few well directed remarks he advised Soames not to be too careful in giving that evidence.</content>
<content>"A little bluffness, Mr. Forsyte," he said, "a little bluffness," and after he had spoken he laughed firmly, closed his lips tight, and scratched his head just below where he had pushed his wig back, for all the world like the gentleman-farmer for whom he loved to be taken.</content>
<content>He was considered perhaps the leading man in breach of promise cases.</content>
<content>Soames used the underground again in going home.</content>
<content>The fog was worse than ever at Sloane Square station.</content>
<content>Through the still, thick blur, men groped in and out; women, very few, grasped their reticules to their bosoms and handkerchiefs to their mouths; crowned with the weird excrescence of the driver, haloed by a vague glow of lamp-light that seemed to drown in vapour before it reached the pavement, cabs loomed dim-shaped ever and again, and discharged citizens, bolting like rabbits to their burrows.</content>
<content>And these shadowy figures, wrapped each in his own little shroud of fog, took no notice of each other.</content>
<content>In the great warren, each rabbit for himself, especially those clothed in the more expensive fur, who, afraid of carriages on foggy days, are driven underground.</content>
<content>One figure, however, not far from Soames, waited at the station door.</content>
<content>Some buccaneer or lover, of whom each Forsyte thought: 'Poor devil!</content>
<content>looks as if he were having a bad time!'</content>
<content>Their kind hearts beat a stroke faster for that poor, waiting, anxious lover in the fog; but they hurried by, well knowing that they had neither time nor money to spare for any suffering but their own.</content>
<content>Only a policeman, patrolling slowly and at intervals, took an interest in that waiting figure, the brim of whose slouch hat half hid a face reddened by the cold, all thin, and haggard, over which a hand stole now and again to smooth away anxiety, or renew the resolution that kept him waiting there.</content>
<content>But the waiting lover  if lover he were  was used to policemen's scrutiny, or too absorbed in his anxiety, for he never flinched.</content>
<content>A hardened case, accustomed to long trysts, to anxiety, and fog, and cold, if only his mistress came at last.</content>
<content>Foolish lover!</content>
<content>Fogs last until the spring; there is also snow and rain, no comfort anywhere; gnawing fear if you bring her out, gnawing fear if you bid her stay at home!</content>
<content>"Serve him right; he should arrange his affairs better!"</content>
<content>So any respectable Forsyte.</content>
<content>Yet, if that sounder citizen could have listened at the waiting lover's heart, out there in the fog and the cold, he would have said again: "Yes, poor devil he's having a bad time!"</content>
<content>Soames got into his cab, and, with the glass down, crept along Sloane Street, and so along the Brompton Road, and home.</content>
<content>He reached his house at five.</content>
<content>His wife was not in.</content>
<content>She had gone out a quarter of an hour before.</content>
<content>Out at such a time of night, into this terrible fog!</content>
<content>What was the meaning of that?</content>
<content>He sat by the dining-room fire, with the door open, disturbed to the soul, trying to read the evening paper.</content>
<content>A book was no good - in daily papers alone was any narcotic to such worry as his.</content>
<content>From the customary events recorded in the journal he drew some comfort.</content>
<content>'Suicide of an actress' - 'Grave indisposition of a Statesman'  that chronic sufferer  - 'Divorce of an army officer' - 'Fire in a colliery' - he read them all.</content>
<content>They helped him a little - prescribed by the greatest of all doctors, our natural taste.</content>
<content>It was nearly seven when he heard her come in.</content>
<content>The incident of the night before had long lost its importance under stress of anxiety at her strange sortie into the fog.</content>
<content>But now that Irene was home, the memory of her broken-hearted sobbing came back to him, and he felt nervous at the thought of facing her.</content>
<content>She was already on the stairs; her grey fur coat hung to her knees, its high collar almost hid her face, she wore a thick veil.</content>
<content>She neither turned to look at him nor spoke.</content>
<content>No ghost or stranger could have passed more silently.</content>
<content>Bilson came to lay dinner, and told him that Mrs. Forsyte was not coming down; she was having the soup in her room.</content>
<content>For once Soames did not 'change'; it was, perhaps, the first time in his life that he had sat down to dinner with soiled cuffs, and, not even noticing them, he brooded long over his wine.</content>
<content>He sent Bilson to light a fire in his picture-room, and presently went up there himself.</content>
<content>Turning on the gas, he heaved a deep sigh, as though amongst these treasures, the backs of which confronted him in stacks, around the little room, he had found at length his peace of mind.</content>
<content>He went straight up to the greatest treasure of them all, an undoubted Turner, and, carrying it to the easel, turned its face to the light.</content>
<content>There had been a movement in Turners, but he had not been able to make up his mind to part with it.</content>
