<content>He stood for a long time, his pale, clean-shaven face poked forward above his stand-up collar, looking at the picture as though he were adding it up; a wistful expression came into his eyes; he found, perhaps, that it came to too little.</content>
<content>He took it down from the easel to put it back against the wall; but, in crossing the room, stopped, for he seemed to hear sobbing.</content>
<content>It was nothing - only the sort of thing that had been bothering him in the morning.</content>
<content>And soon after, putting the high guard before the blazing fire, he stole downstairs.</content>
<content>Fresh for the morrow!</content>
<content>was his thought.</content>
<content>It was long before he went to sleep....</content>
<content>It is now to George Forsyte that the mind must turn for light on the events of that fog-engulfed afternoon.</content>
<content>The wittiest and most sportsmanlike of the Forsytes had passed the day reading a novel in the paternal mansion at Princes' Gardens.</content>
<content>Since a recent crisis in his financial affairs he had been kept on parole by Roger, and compelled to reside 'at home.'</content>
<content>Towards five o'clock he went out, and took train at South Kensington Station  for everyone to-day went Underground .</content>
<content>His intention was to dine, and pass the evening playing billiards at the Red Pottle - that unique hostel, neither club, hotel, nor good gilt restaurant.</content>
<content>He got out at Charing Cross, choosing it in preference to his more usual St. James's Park, that he might reach Jermyn Street by better lighted ways.</content>
<content>On the platform his eyes - for in combination with a composed and fashionable appearance, George had sharp eyes, and was always on the look-out for fillips to his sardonic humour - his eyes were attracted by a man, who, leaping from a first-class compartment, staggered rather than walked towards the exit.</content>
<content>'So ho, my bird!'</content>
<content>said George to himself; 'why, it's "the Buccaneer!"'</content>
<content>and he put his big figure on the trail.</content>
<content>Nothing afforded him greater amusement than a drunken man.</content>
<content>Bosinney, who wore a slouch hat, stopped in front of him, spun around, and rushed back towards the carriage he had just left.</content>
<content>He was too late.</content>
<content>A porter caught him by the coat; the train was already moving on.</content>
<content>George's practised glance caught sight of the face of a lady clad in a grey fur coat at the carriage window.</content>
<content>It was Mrs. Soames - and George felt that this was interesting!</content>
<content>And now he followed Bosinney more closely than ever - up the stairs, past the ticket collector into the street.</content>
<content>In that progress, however, his feelings underwent a change; no longer merely curious and amused, he felt sorry for the poor fellow he was shadowing.</content>
<content>'The Buccaneer' was not drunk, but seemed to be acting under the stress of violent emotion; he was talking to himself, and all that George could catch were the words "Oh, God!"</content>
<content>Nor did he appear to know what he was doing, or where going; but stared, hesitated, moved like a man out of his mind; and from being merely a joker in search of amusement, George felt that he must see the poor chap through.</content>
<content>He had 'taken the knock' - 'taken the knock!'</content>
<content>And he wondered what on earth Mrs. Soames had been saying, what on earth she had been telling him in the railway carriage.</content>
<content>She had looked bad enough herself!</content>
<content>It made George sorry to think of her travelling on with her trouble all alone.</content>
<content>He followed close behind Bosinney's elbow - tall, burly figure, saying nothing, dodging warily - and shadowed him out into the fog.</content>
<content>There was something here beyond a jest!</content>
<content>He kept his head admirably, in spite of some excitement, for in addition to compassion, the instincts of the chase were roused within him.</content>
<content>Bosinney walked right out into the thoroughfare - a vast muffled blackness, where a man could not see six paces before him; where, all around, voices or whistles mocked the sense of direction; and sudden shapes came rolling slow upon them; and now and then a light showed like a dim island in an infinite dark sea.</content>
<content>And fast into this perilous gulf of night walked Bosinney, and fast after him walked George.</content>
<content>If the fellow meant to put his 'twopenny' under a 'bus, he would stop it if he could!</content>
<content>Across the street and back the hunted creature strode, not groping as other men were groping in that gloom, but driven forward as though the faithful George behind wielded a knout; and this chase after a haunted man began to have for George the strangest fascination.</content>
<content>But it was now that the affair developed in a way which ever afterwards caused it to remain green in his mind.</content>
<content>Brought to a stand-still in the fog, he heard words which threw a sudden light on these proceedings.</content>
<content>What Mrs. Soames had said to Bosinney in the train was now no longer dark.</content>
<content>George understood from those mutterings that Soames had exercised his rights over an estranged and unwilling wife in the greatest - the supreme act of property.</content>
<content>His fancy wandered in the fields of this situation; it impressed him; he guessed something of the anguish, the sexual confusion and horror in Bosinney's heart.</content>
<content>And he thought: 'Yes, it's a bit thick!</content>
<content>I don't  wonder the poor fellow is half-cracked!'</content>
<content>He had run his quarry to earth on a bench under one of the lions in Trafalgar Square, a monster sphynx astray like themselves in that gulf of darkness.</content>
<content>Here, rigid and silent, sat Bosinney, and George, in whose patience was a touch of strange brotherliness, took his stand behind.</content>
<content>He was not lacking in a certain delicacy - a sense of form - that did not permit him to intrude upon this tragedy, and he waited, quiet as the lion above, his fur collar hitched above his ears concealing the fleshy redness of his cheeks, concealing all but his eyes with their sardonic, compassionate stare.</content>
<content>And men kept passing back from business on the way to their clubs - men whose figures shrouded in cocoons of fog came into view like spectres, and like spectres vanished.</content>
<content>Then even in his compassion George's Quilpish humour broke forth in a sudden longing to pluck these spectres by the sleeve, and say:
"Hi, you Johnnies!</content>
<content>You don't  often see a show like this!</content>
<content>Here's a poor devil whose mistress has just been telling him a pretty little story of her husband; walk up, walk up!</content>
<content>He's taken the knock, you see."</content>
<content>In fancy he saw them gaping round the tortured lover; and grinned as he thought of some respectable, newly-married spectre enabled by the state of his own affections to catch an inkling of what was going on within Bosinney; he fancied he could see his mouth getting wider and wider, and the fog going down and down.</content>
<content>For in George was all that contempt of the of the married middle-class - peculiar to the wild and sportsmanlike spirits in its ranks.</content>
<content>But he began to be bored.</content>
<content>Waiting was not what he had bargained for.</content>
<content>'After all,' he thought, 'the poor chap will get over it; not the first time such a thing has happened in this little city!'</content>
<content>But now his quarry again began muttering words of violent hate and anger.</content>
<content>And following a sudden impulse George touched him on the shoulder.</content>
<content>Bosinney spun round.</content>
<content>"Who are you?</content>
<content>What do you want?"</content>
<content>George could have stood it well enough in the light of the gas lamps, in the light of that everyday world of which he was so hardy a connoisseur; but in this fog, where all was gloomy and unreal, where nothing had that matter-of-fact value associated by Forsytes with earth, he was a victim to strange qualms, and as he tried to stare back into the eyes of this maniac, he thought:
'If I see a bobby, I'll hand him over; he's not fit to be at large.'</content>
<content>But waiting for no answer, Bosinney strode off into the fog, and George followed, keeping perhaps a little further off, yet more than ever set on tracking him down.</content>
<content>'He can't  go on long like this,' he thought.</content>
<content>'It's God's own miracle he's not been run over already.'</content>
<content>He brooded no more on policemen, a sportsman's sacred fire alive again within him.</content>
<content>Into a denser gloom than ever Bosinney held on at a furious pace; but his pursuer perceived more method in his madness - he was clearly making his way westwards.</content>
<content>'He's really going for Soames!'</content>
<content>thought George.</content>
<content>The idea was attractive.</content>
<content>It would be a sporting end to such a chase.</content>
<content>He had always disliked his cousin.</content>
<content>The shaft of a passing cab brushed against his shoulder and made him leap aside.</content>
<content>He did not intend to be killed for the Buccaneer, or anyone.</content>
<content>Yet, with hereditary tenacity, he stuck to the trail through vapour that blotted out everything but the shadow of the hunted man and the dim moon of the nearest lamp.</content>
<content>Then suddenly, with the instinct of a town-stroller, George knew himself to be in Piccadilly.</content>
<content>Here he could find his way blindfold; and freed from the strain of geographical uncertainty, his mind returned to Bosinney's trouble.</content>
<content>Down the long avenue of his man-about-town experience, bursting, as it were, through a smirch of doubtful amours, there stalked to him a memory of his youth.</content>
<content>A memory, poignant still, that brought the scent of hay, the gleam of moonlight, a summer magic, into the reek and blackness of this London fog - the memory of a night when in the darkest shadow of a lawn he had overheard from a woman's lips that he was not her sole possessor.</content>
<content>And for a moment George walked no longer in black Piccadilly, but lay again, with hell in his heart, and his face to the sweet-smelling, dewy grass, in the long shadow of poplars that hid the moon.</content>
<content>A longing seized him to throw his arm round the Buccaneer, and say, "Come, old boy.</content>
<content>Time cures all.</content>
<content>Let's go and drink it off!"</content>
<content>But a voice yelled at him, and he started back.</content>
<content>A cab rolled out of blackness, and into blackness disappeared.</content>
<content>And suddenly George perceived that he had lost Bosinney.</content>
<content>He ran forward and back, felt his heart clutched by a sickening fear, the dark fear which lives in the wings of the fog.</content>
<content>Perspiration started out on his brow.</content>
<content>He stood quite still, listening with all his might.</content>
<content>"And then," as he confided to Dartie the same evening in the course of a game of billiards at the Red Pottle, "I lost him."</content>
<content>Dartie twirled complacently at his dark moustache.</content>
<content>He had just put together a neat break of twenty-three, - failing at a 'Jenny.'</content>
<content>"And who was she?"</content>
<content>he asked.</content>
<content>George looked slowly at the 'man of the world's' fattish, sallow face, and a little grim smile lurked about the curves of his cheeks and his heavy-lidded eyes.</content>
<content>'No, no, my fine fellow,' he thought, 'I'm not going to tell you.'</content>
<content>For though he mixed with Dartie a good deal, he thought him a bit of a cad.</content>
<content>"Oh, some little love-lady or other," he said, and chalked his cue.</content>
<content>"A love-lady!"</content>
<content>exclaimed Dartie - he used a more figurative expression.</content>
<content>"I made sure it was our friend Soa...."
"Did you?"</content>
<content>said George curtly.</content>
<content>"Then damme you've  made an error."</content>
<content>He missed his shot.</content>
<content>He was careful not to allude to the subject again till, towards eleven o'clock, having, in his poetic phraseology, 'looked upon the drink when it was yellow,' he drew aside the blind, and gazed out into the street.</content>
<content>The murky blackness of the fog was but faintly broken by the lamps of the 'Red Pottle,' and no shape of mortal man or thing was in sight.</content>
<content>"I can't  help thinking of that poor Buccaneer," he said.</content>
<content>"He may be wandering out there now in that fog.</content>
<content>If he's not a corpse," he added with strange dejection.</content>
<content>"Corpse!"</content>
<content>said Dartie, in whom the recollection of his defeat at Richmond flared up.</content>
<content>"He's all right.</content>
<content>Ten to one if he wasn't  tight!"</content>
<content>George turned on him, looking really formidable, with a sort of savage gloom on his big face.</content>
<content>"Dry up!"</content>
<content>he said.</content>
<content>"Don't  I tell you he's 'taken the knock!"'</content>
<content>CHAPTER V - THE TRIAL
In the morning of his case, which was second in the list, Soames was again obliged to start without seeing Irene, and it was just as well, for he had not as yet made up his mind what attitude to adopt towards her.</content>
<content>He had been requested to be in court by half-past ten, to provide against the event of the first action  a breach of promise  collapsing, which however it did not, both sides showing a courage that afforded Waterbuck, Q.C., an opportunity for improving his already great reputation in this class of case.</content>
<content>He was opposed by Ram, the other celebrated breach of promise man.</content>
<content>It was a battle of giants.</content>
<content>The court delivered judgment just before the luncheon interval.</content>
<content>The jury left the box for good, and Soames went out to get something to eat.</content>
<content>He met James standing at the little luncheon-bar, like a pelican in the wilderness of the galleries, bent over a sandwich with a glass of sherry before him.</content>
<content>The spacious emptiness of the great central hall, over which father and son brooded as they stood together, was marred now and then for a fleeting moment by barristers in wig and gown hurriedly bolting across, by an occasional old lady or rusty-coated man, looking up in a frightened way, and by two persons, bolder than their generation, seated in an embrasure arguing.</content>
<content>The sound of their voices arose, together with a scent as of neglected wells, which, mingling with the odour of the galleries, combined to form the savour, like nothing but the emanation of a refined cheese, so indissolubly connected with the administration of British Justice.</content>
<content>It was not long before James addressed his son.</content>
<content>"When's your case coming on?</content>
<content>I suppose it'll be on directly.</content>
<content>I shouldn't  wonder if this Bosinney'd say anything; I should think he'd have to.</content>
<content>He'll go bankrupt if it goes against him."</content>
<content>He took a large bite at his sandwich and a mouthful of sherry.</content>
<content>"Your mother," he said, "wants you and Irene to come and dine to-night."</content>
<content>A chill smile played round Soames' lips; he looked back at his father.</content>
<content>Anyone who had seen the look, cold and furtive, thus interchanged, might have been pardoned for not appreciating the real understanding between them.</content>
<content>James finished his sherry at a draught.</content>
<content>"How much?"</content>
<content>he asked.</content>
<content>On returning to the court Soames took at once his rightful seat on the front bench beside his solicitor.</content>
<content>He ascertained where his father was seated with a glance so sidelong as to commit nobody.</content>
<content>James, sitting back with his hands clasped over the handle of his umbrella, was brooding on the end of the bench immediately behind counsel, whence he could get away at once when the case was over.</content>
<content>He considered Bosinney's conduct in every way outrageous, but he did not wish to run up against him, feeling that the meeting would be awkward.</content>
<content>Next to the Divorce Court, this court was, perhaps, the favourite emporium of justice, libel, breach of promise, and other commercial actions being frequently decided there.</content>
<content>Quite a sprinkling of persons unconnected with the law occupied the back benches, and the hat of a woman or two could be seen in the gallery.</content>
<content>The two rows of seats immediately in front of James were gradually filled by barristers in wigs, who sat down to make pencil notes, chat, and attend to their teeth; but his interest was soon diverted from these lesser lights of justice by the entrance of Waterbuck, Q.C., with the wings of his silk gown rustling, and his red, capable face supported by two short, brown whiskers.</content>
<content>The famous Q.C.</content>
<content>looked, as James freely admitted, the very picture of a man who could heckle a witness.</content>
<content>For all his experience, it so happened that he had never seen Waterbuck, Q.C., before, and, like many Forsytes in the lower branch of the profession, he had an extreme admiration for a good cross-examiner.</content>
<content>The long, lugubrious folds in his cheeks relaxed somewhat after seeing him, especially as he now perceived that Soames alone was represented by silk.</content>
<content>Waterbuck, Q.C., had barely screwed round on his elbow to chat with his Junior before Mr. Justice Bentham himself appeared - a thin, rather hen-like man, with a little stoop, clean-shaven under his snowy wig.</content>
<content>Like all the rest of the court, Waterbuck rose, and remained on his feet until the judge was seated.</content>
<content>James rose but slightly; he was already comfortable, and had no opinion of Bentham, having sat next but one to him at dinner twice at the Bumley Tomms'.</content>
<content>Bumley Tomm was rather a poor thing, though he had been so successful.</content>
<content>James himself had given him his first brief.</content>
<content>He was excited, too, for he had just found out that Bosinney was not in court.</content>
<content>'Now, what's he mean by that?'</content>
<content>he kept on thinking.</content>
<content>The case having been called on, Waterbuck, Q.C., pushing back his papers, hitched his gown on his shoulder, and, with a semi-circular look around him, like a man who is going to bat, arose and addressed the Court.</content>
<content>The facts, he said, were not in dispute, and all that his Lordship would be asked was to interpret the correspondence which had taken place between his client and the defendant, an architect, with reference to the decoration of a house.</content>
<content>He would, however, submit that this correspondence could only mean one very plain thing.</content>
<content>After briefly reciting the history of the house at Robin Hill, which he described as a mansion, and the actual facts of expenditure, he went on as follows:
"My client, Mr. Soames Forsyte, is a gentleman, a man of property, who would be the last to dispute any legitimate claim that might be made against him, but he has met with such treatment from his architect in the matter of this house, over which he has, as your lordship has heard, already spent some twelve - some twelve thousand pounds, a sum considerably in advance of the amount he had originally contemplated, that as a matter of principle - and this I cannot too strongly emphasize - as a matter of principle, and in the interests of others, he has felt himself compelled to bring this action.</content>
<content>The point put forward in defence by the architect I will suggest to your lordship is not worthy of a moment's serious consideration."</content>
<content>He then read the correspondence.</content>
<content>His client, "a man of recognised position," was prepared to go into the box, and to swear that he never did authorize, that it was never in his mind to authorize, the expenditure of any money beyond the extreme limit of twelve thousand and fifty pounds, which he had clearly fixed; and not further to waste the time of the court, he would at once call Mr. Forsyte.</content>
<content>Soames then went into the box.</content>
<content>His whole appearance was striking in its composure.</content>
<content>His face, just supercilious enough, pale and clean-shaven, with a little line between the eyes, and compressed lips; his dress in unostentatious order, one hand neatly gloved, the other bare.</content>
<content>He answered the questions put to him in a somewhat low, but distinct voice.</content>
<content>His evidence under cross-examination savoured of taciturnity.</content>
<content>Had he not used the expression, "a free hand"?</content>
<content>No.</content>
<content>"Come, come!"</content>
<content>The expression he had used was 'a free hand in the terms of this correspondence.'</content>
<content>"Would you tell the Court that that was English?"</content>
<content>"Yes!"</content>
<content>"What do you say it means?"</content>
<content>"What it says!"</content>
<content>"Are you prepared to deny that it is a contradiction in terms?"</content>
<content>"Yes."</content>
<content>"You are not an Irishman?"</content>
<content>"No."</content>
<content>"Are you a well-educated man?"</content>
<content>"Yes."</content>
<content>"And yet you persist in that statement?"</content>
<content>"Yes."</content>
<content>Throughout this and much more cross-examination, which turned again and again around the 'nice point,' James sat with his hand behind his ear, his eyes fixed upon his son.</content>
<content>He was proud of him!</content>
<content>He could not but feel that in similar circumstances he himself would have been tempted to enlarge his replies, but his instinct told him that this taciturnity was the very thing.</content>
<content>He sighed with relief, however, when Soames, slowly turning, and without any change of expression, descended from the box.</content>
<content>When it came to the turn of Bosinney's Counsel to address the Judge, James redoubled his attention, and he searched the Court again and again to see if Bosinney were not somewhere concealed.</content>
<content>Young Chankery began nervously; he was placed by Bosinney's absence in an awkward position.</content>
<content>He therefore did his best to turn that absence to account.</content>
<content>He could not but fear - he said - that his client had met with an accident.</content>
<content>He had fully expected him there to give evidence; they had sent round that morning both to Mr. Bosinney's office and to his rooms  though he knew they were one and the same, he thought it was as well not to say so , but it was not known where he was, and this he considered to be ominous, knowing how anxious Mr. Bosinney had been to give his evidence.</content>
<content>He had not, however, been instructed to apply for an adjournment, and in default of such instruction he conceived it his duty to go on.</content>
<content>The plea on which he somewhat confidently relied, and which his client, had he not unfortunately been prevented in some way from attending, would have supported by his evidence, was that such an expression as a 'free hand' could not be limited, fettered, and rendered unmeaning, by any verbiage which might follow it.</content>
<content>He would go further and say that the correspondence showed that whatever he might have said in his evidence, Mr. Forsyte had in fact never contemplated repudiating liability on any of the work ordered or executed by his architect.</content>
<content>The defendant had certainly never contemplated such a contingency, or, as was demonstrated by his letters, he would never have proceeded with the work - a work of extreme delicacy, carried out with great care and efficiency, to meet and satisfy the fastidious taste of a connoisseur, a rich man, a man of property.</content>
<content>He felt strongly on this point, and feeling strongly he used, perhaps, rather strong words when he said that this action was of a most unjustifiable, unexpected, indeed - unprecedented character.</content>
<content>If his Lordship had had the opportunity that he himself had made it his duty to take, to go over this very fine house and see the great delicacy and beauty of the decorations executed by his client - an artist in his most honourable profession - he felt convinced that not for one moment would his Lordship tolerate this, he would use no stronger word than daring attempt to evade legitimate responsibility.</content>
<content>Taking the text of Soames' letters, he lightly touched on 'Boileau v. The Blasted Cement Company, Limited.'</content>
<content>"It is doubtful," he said, "what that authority has decided; in any case I would submit that it is just as much in my favour as in my friend's."</content>
<content>He then argued the 'nice point' closely.</content>
<content>With all due deference he submitted that Mr. Forsyte's expression nullified itself.</content>
<content>His client not being a rich man, the matter was a serious one for him; he was a very talented architect, whose professional reputation was undoubtedly somewhat at stake.</content>
<content>He concluded with a perhaps too personal appeal to the Judge, as a lover of the arts, to show himself the protector of artists, from what was occasionally - he said occasionally - the too iron hand of capital.</content>
<content>"What," he said, "will be the position of the artistic professions, if men of property like this Mr. Forsyte refuse, and are allowed to refuse, to carry out the obligations of the commissions which they have given."</content>
<content>He would now call his client, in case he should at the last moment have found himself able to be present.</content>
<content>The name Philip Baynes Bosinney was called three times by the Ushers, and the sound of the calling echoed with strange melancholy throughout the Court and Galleries.</content>
<content>The crying of this name, to which no answer was returned, had upon James a curious effect: it was like calling for your lost dog about the streets.</content>
<content>And the creepy feeling that it gave him, of a man missing, grated on his sense of comfort and security-on his cosiness.</content>
<content>Though he could not have said why, it made him feel uneasy.</content>
<content>He looked now at the clock - a quarter to three!</content>
<content>It would be all over in a quarter of an hour.</content>
<content>Where could the young fellow be?</content>
<content>It was only when Mr. Justice Bentham delivered judgment that he got over the turn he had received.</content>
<content>Behind the wooden erection, by which he was fenced from more ordinary mortals, the learned Judge leaned forward.</content>
<content>The electric light, just turned on above his head, fell on his face, and mellowed it to an orange hue beneath the snowy crown of his wig; the amplitude of his robes grew before the eye; his whole figure, facing the comparative dusk of the Court, radiated like some majestic and sacred body.</content>
<content>He cleared his throat, took a sip of water, broke the nib of a quill against the desk, and, folding his bony hands before him, began.</content>
<content>To James he suddenly loomed much larger than he had ever thought Bentham would loom.</content>
<content>It was the majesty of the law; and a person endowed with a nature far less matter-of-fact than that of James might have been excused for failing to pierce this halo, and disinter therefrom the somewhat ordinary Forsyte, who walked and talked in every-day life under the name of Sir Walter Bentham.</content>
<content>He delivered judgment in the following words:
"The facts in this case are not in dispute.</content>
<content>On May 15 last the defendant wrote to the plaintiff, requesting to be allowed to withdraw from his professional position in regard to the decoration of the plaintiff's house, unless he were given 'a free hand.'</content>
<content>The plaintiff, on May 17, wrote back as follows: 'In giving you, in accordance with your request, this free hand, I wish you to clearly understand that the total cost of the house as handed over to me completely decorated, inclusive of your fee  as arranged between us  must not exceed twelve thousand pounds.'</content>
<content>To this letter the defendant replied on May 18: 'If you think that in such a delicate matter as decoration I can bind myself to the exact pound, I am afraid you are mistaken.'</content>
<content>On May 19 the plaintiff wrote as follows: 'I did not mean to say that if you should exceed the sum named in my letter to you by ten or twenty or even fifty pounds there would be any difficulty between us.</content>
<content>You have a free hand in the terms of this correspondence, and I hope you will see your way to completing the decorations.'</content>
<content>On May 20 the defendant replied thus shortly: 'Very well.'</content>
<content>"In completing these decorations, the defendant incurred liabilities and expenses which brought the total cost of this house up to the sum of twelve thousand four hundred pounds, all of which expenditure has been defrayed by the plaintiff.</content>
<content>This action has been brought by the plaintiff to recover from the defendant the sum of three hundred and fifty pounds expended by him in excess of a sum of twelve thousand and fifty pounds, alleged by the plaintiff to have been fixed by this correspondence as the maximum sum that the defendant had authority to expend.</content>
<content>"The question for me to decide is whether or no the defendant is liable to refund to the plaintiff this sum.</content>
<content>In my judgment he is so liable.</content>
<content>"What in effect the plaintiff has said is this 'I give you a free hand to complete these decorations, provided that you keep within a total cost to me of twelve thousand pounds.</content>
<content>If you exceed that sum by as much as fifty pounds, I will not hold you responsible; beyond that point you are no agent of mine, and I shall repudiate liability.'</content>
<content>It is not quite clear to me whether, had the plaintiff in fact repudiated liability under his agent's contracts, he would, under all the circumstances, have been successful in so doing; but he has not adopted this course.</content>
<content>He has accepted liability, and fallen back upon his rights against the defendant under the terms of the latter's engagement.</content>
<content>"In my judgment the plaintiff is entitled to recover this sum from the defendant.</content>
<content>"It has been sought, on behalf of the defendant, to show that no limit of expenditure was fixed or intended to be fixed by this correspondence.</content>
<content>If this were so, I can find no reason for the plaintiff's importation into the correspondence of the figures of twelve thousand pounds and subsequently of fifty pounds.</content>
<content>The defendant's contention would render these figures meaningless.</content>
<content>It is manifest to me that by his letter of May 20 he assented to a very clear proposition, by the terms of which he must be held to be bound.</content>
<content>"For these reasons there will be judgment for the plaintiff for the amount claimed with costs."</content>
<content>James sighed, and stooping, picked up his umbrella which had fallen with a rattle at the words 'importation into this correspondence.'</content>
<content>Untangling his legs, he rapidly left the Court; without waiting for his son, he snapped up a hansom cab  it was a clear, grey afternoon  and drove straight to Timothy's where he found Swithin; and to him, Mrs. Septimus Small, and Aunt Hester, he recounted the whole proceedings, eating two muffins not altogether in the intervals of speech.</content>
<content>"Soames did very well," he ended; "he's got his head screwed on the right way.</content>
<content>This won't  please Jolyon.</content>
<content>It's a bad business for that young Bosinney; he'll go bankrupt, I shouldn't  wonder," and then after a long pause, during which he had stared disquietly into the fire, he added:
"He wasn't  there - now why?"</content>
<content>There was a sound of footsteps.</content>
<content>The figure of a thick-set man, with the ruddy brown face of robust health, was seen in the back drawing-room.</content>
<content>The forefinger of his upraised hand was outlined against the black of his frock coat.</content>
<content>He spoke in a grudging voice.</content>
<content>"Well, James," he said, "I can't  - I can't  stop," and turning round, he walked out.</content>
<content>It was Timothy.</content>
<content>James rose from his chair.</content>
<content>"There!"</content>
<content>he said, "there!</content>
<content>I knew there was something wro...." He checked himself, and was silent, staring before him, as though he had seen a portent.</content>
<content>CHAPTER VI - SOAMES BREAKS THE NEWS
In leaving the Court Soames did not go straight home.</content>
<content>He felt disinclined for the City, and drawn by need for sympathy in his triumph, he, too, made his way, but slowly and on foot, to Timothy's in the Bayswater Road.</content>
<content>His father had just left; Mrs. Small and Aunt Hester, in possession of the whole story, greeted him warmly.</content>
<content>They were sure he was hungry after all that evidence.</content>
<content>Smither should toast him some more muffins, his dear father had eaten them all.</content>
<content>He must put his legs up on the sofa; and he must have a glass of prune brandy too.</content>
<content>It was so strengthening.</content>
<content>Swithin was still present, having lingered later than his wont, for he felt in want of exercise.</content>
<content>On hearing this suggestion, he 'pished.'</content>
<content>A pretty pass young men were coming to!</content>
<content>His own liver was out of order, and he could not bear the thought of anyone else drinking prune brandy.</content>
<content>He went away almost immediately, saying to Soames: "And how's your wife?</content>
<content>You tell her from me that if she's dull, and likes to come and dine with me quietly, I'll give her such a bottle of champagne as she doesn't  get every day."</content>
<content>Staring down from his height on Soames he contracted his thick, puffy, yellow hand as though squeezing within it all this small fry, and throwing out his chest he waddled slowly away.</content>
<content>Mrs. Small and Aunt Hester were left horrified.</content>
<content>Swithin was so droll!</content>
<content>They themselves were longing to ask Soames how Irene would take the result, yet knew that they must not; he would perhaps say something of his own accord, to throw some light on this, the present burning question in their lives, the question that from necessity of silence tortured them almost beyond bearing; for even Timothy had now been told, and the effect on his health was little short of alarming.</content>
<content>And what, too, would June do?</content>
<content>This, also, was a most exciting, if dangerous speculation!</content>
<content>They had never forgotten old Jolyon's visit, since when he had not once been to see them; they had never forgotten the feeling it gave all who were present, that the family was no longer what it had been - that the family was breaking up.</content>
<content>But Soames gave them no help, sitting with his knees crossed, talking of the Barbizon school of painters, whom he had just discovered.</content>
<content>These were the coming men, he said; he should not wonder if a lot of money were made over them; he had his eye on two pictures by a man called Corot, charming things; if he could get them at a reasonable price he was going to buy them - they would, he thought, fetch a big price some day.</content>
<content>Interested as they could not but be, neither Mrs. Septimus Small nor Aunt Hester could entirely acquiesce in being thus put off.</content>
<content>It was interesting - most interesting - and then Soames was so clever that they were sure he would do something with those pictures if anybody could; but what was his plan now that he had won his case; was he going to leave London at once, and live in the country, or what was he going to do?</content>
<content>Soames answered that he did not know, he thought they should be moving soon.</content>
<content>He rose and kissed his aunts.</content>
<content>No sooner had Aunt Juley received this emblem of departure than a change came over her, as though she were being visited by dreadful courage; every little roll of flesh on her face seemed trying to escape from an invisible, confining mask.</content>
<content>She rose to the full extent of her more than medium height, and said: "It has been on my mind a long time, dear, and if nobody else will tell you, I have made up my mind that...."
Aunt Hester interrupted her: "Mind, Julia, you do it...." she gasped - "on your own responsibility!"</content>
<content>Mrs. Small went on as though she had not heard: "I think you ought to know, dear, that Mrs. MacAnder saw Irene walking in Richmond Park with Mr.</content>
<content>Bosinney."</content>
<content>Aunt Hester, who had also risen, sank back in her chair, and turned her face away.</content>
<content>Really Juley was too - she should not do such things when she - Aunt Hester, was in the room; and, breathless with anticipation, she waited for what Soames would answer.</content>
<content>He had flushed the peculiar flush which always centred between his eyes; lifting his hand, and, as it were, selecting a finger, he bit a nail delicately; then, drawling it out between set lips, he said: "Mrs. MacAnder is a cat!"</content>
<content>Without waiting for any reply, he left the room.</content>
<content>When he went into Timothy's he had made up his mind what course to pursue on getting home.</content>
<content>He would go up to Irene and say:
"Well, I've  won my case, and there's an end of it!</content>
<content>I don't  want to be hard on Bosinney; I'll see if we can't  come to some arrangement; he shan't  be pressed.</content>
<content>And now let's turn over a new leaf!</content>
<content>We'll let the house, and get out of these fogs.</content>
<content>We'll go down to Robin Hill at once.</content>
<content>I - I never meant to be rough with you!</content>
<content>Let's shake hands - and - " Perhaps she would let him kiss her, and forget!</content>
<content>When he came out of Timothy's his intentions were no longer so simple.</content>
<content>The smouldering jealousy and suspicion of months blazed up within him.</content>
<content>He would put an end to that sort of thing once and for all; he would not have her drag his name in the dirt!</content>
<content>If she could not or would not love him, as was her duty and his right - she should not play him tricks with anyone else!</content>
<content>He would tax her with it; threaten to divorce her!</content>
<content>That would make her behave; she would never face that.</content>
<content>But - but - what if she did?</content>
<content>He was staggered; this had not occurred to him.</content>
<content>What if she did?</content>
<content>What if she made him a confession?</content>
<content>How would he stand then?</content>
<content>He would have to bring a divorce!</content>
<content>A divorce!</content>
<content>Thus close, the word was paralyzing, so utterly at variance with all the principles that had hitherto guided his life.</content>
<content>Its lack of compromise appalled him; he felt - like the captain of a ship, going to the side of his vessel, and, with his own hands throwing over the most precious of his bales.</content>
<content>This jettisoning of his property with his own hand seemed uncanny to Soames.</content>
<content>It would injure him in his profession: He would have to get rid of the house at Robin Hill, on which he had spent so much money, so much anticipation - and at a sacrifice.</content>
<content>And she!</content>
<content>She would no longer belong to him, not even in name!</content>
<content>She would pass out of his life, and he - he should never see her again!</content>
<content>He traversed in the cab the length of a street without getting beyond the thought that he should never see her again!</content>
<content>But perhaps there was nothing to confess, even now very likely there was nothing to confess.</content>
<content>Was it wise to push things so far?</content>
<content>Was it wise to put himself into a position where he might have to eat his words?</content>
<content>The result of this case would ruin Bosinney; a ruined man was desperate, but - what could he do?</content>
<content>He might go abroad, ruined men always went abroad.</content>
<content>What could they do - if indeed it was 'they' - without money?</content>
<content>It would be better to wait and see how things turned out.</content>
<content>If necessary, he could have her watched.</content>
<content>The agony of his jealousy  for all the world like the crisis of an aching tooth  came on again; and he almost cried out.</content>
<content>But he must decide, fix on some course of action before he got home.</content>
<content>When the cab drew up at the door, he had decided nothing.</content>
<content>He entered, pale, his hands moist with perspiration, dreading to meet her, burning to meet her, ignorant of what he was to say or do.</content>
<content>The maid Bilson was in the hall, and in answer to his question: "Where is your mistress?"</content>
<content>told him that Mrs. Forsyte had left the house about noon, taking with her a trunk and bag.</content>
<content>Snatching the sleeve of his fur coat away from her grasp, he confronted her:
"What?"</content>
<content>he exclaimed; "what's that you said?"</content>
<content>Suddenly recollecting that he must not betray emotion, he added: "What message did she leave?"</content>
<content>and noticed with secret terror the startled look of the maid's eyes.</content>
<content>"Mrs. Forsyte left no message, sir."</content>
<content>"No message; very well, thank you, that will do.</content>
<content>I shall be dining out."</content>
<content>The maid went downstairs, leaving him still in his fur coat, idly turning over the visiting cards in the porcelain bowl that stood on the carved oak rug chest in the hall.</content>
<content>Mr. and Mrs. Bareham Culcher.</content>
<content>Mrs. Septimus Small.</content>
<content>Mrs. Baynes.</content>
<content>Mr. Solomon Thornworthy.</content>
<content>Lady Bellis.</content>
<content>Miss Hermione Bellis.</content>
<content>Miss Winifred Bellis.</content>
<content>Miss Ella Bellis.</content>
<content>Who the devil were all these people?</content>
<content>He seemed to have forgotten all familiar things.</content>
<content>The words 'no message - a trunk, and a bag,' played a hide-and-seek in his brain.</content>
<content>It was incredible that she had left no message, and, still in his fur coat, he ran upstairs two steps at a time, as a young married man when he comes home will run up to his wife's room.</content>
<content>Everything was dainty, fresh, sweet-smelling; everything in perfect order.</content>
<content>On the great bed with its lilac silk quilt, was the bag she had made and embroidered with her own hands to hold her sleeping things; her slippers ready at the foot; the sheets even turned over at the head as though expecting her.</content>
<content>On the table stood the silver-mounted brushes and bottles from her dressing bag, his own present.</content>
<content>There must, then, be some mistake.</content>
<content>What bag had she taken?</content>
<content>He went to the bell to summon Bilson, but remembered in time that he must assume knowledge of where Irene had gone, take it all as a matter of course, and grope out the meaning for himself.</content>
<content>He locked the doors, and tried to think, but felt his brain going round; and suddenly tears forced themselves into his eyes.</content>
<content>Hurriedly pulling off his coat, he looked at himself in the mirror.</content>
<content>He was too pale, a greyish tinge all over his face; he poured out water, and began feverishly washing.</content>
<content>Her silver-mounted brushes smelt faintly of the perfumed lotion she used for her hair; and at this scent the burning sickness of his jealousy seized him again.</content>
<content>Struggling into his fur, he ran downstairs and out into the street.</content>
<content>He had not lost all command of himself, however, and as he went down Sloane Street he framed a story for use, in case he should not find her at Bosinney's.</content>
<content>But if he should?</content>
<content>His power of decision again failed; he reached the house without knowing what he should do if he did find her there.</content>
<content>It was after office hours, and the street door was closed; the woman who opened it could not say whether Mr. Bosinney were in or no; she had not seen him that day, not for two or three days; she did not attend to him now, nobody attended to him, he....
Soames interrupted her, he would go up and see for himself.</content>
<content>He went up with a dogged, white face.</content>
<content>The top floor was unlighted, the door closed, no one answered his ringing, he could hear no sound.</content>
<content>He was obliged to descend, shivering under his fur, a chill at his heart.</content>
<content>Hailing a cab, he told the man to drive to Park Lane.</content>
<content>On the way he tried to recollect when he had last given her a cheque; she could not have more than three or four pounds, but there were her jewels; and with exquisite torture he remembered how much money she could raise on these; enough to take them abroad; enough for them to live on for months!</content>
<content>He tried to calculate; the cab stopped, and he got out with the calculation unmade.</content>
<content>The butler asked whether Mrs. Soames was in the cab, the master had told him they were both expected to dinner.</content>
<content>Soames answered: "No.</content>
<content>Mrs. Forsyte has a cold."</content>
<content>The butler was sorry.</content>
<content>Soames thought he was looking at him inquisitively, and remembering that he was not in dress clothes, asked: "Anybody here to dinner, Warmson?"</content>
<content>"Nobody but Mr. and Mrs. Dartie, sir."</content>
<content>Again it seemed to Soames that the butler was looking curiously at him.</content>
<content>His composure gave way.</content>
<content>"What are you looking at?"</content>
<content>he said.</content>
<content>"What's the matter with me, eh?"</content>
<content>The butler blushed, hung up the fur coat, murmured something that sounded like: "Nothing, sir, I'm sure, sir," and stealthily withdrew.</content>
<content>Soames walked upstairs.</content>
<content>Passing the drawing-room without a look, he went straight up to his mother's and father's bedroom.</content>
<content>James, standing sideways, the concave lines of his tall, lean figure displayed to advantage in shirt-sleeves and evening waistcoat, his head bent, the end of his white tie peeping askew from underneath one white Dundreary whisker, his eyes peering with intense concentration, his lips pouting, was hooking the top hooks of his wife's bodice.</content>
<content>Soames stopped; he felt half-choked, whether because he had come upstairs too fast, or for some other reason.</content>
<content>He - he himself had never - never been asked to....</content>
<content>He heard his father's voice, as though there were a pin in his mouth, saying: "Who's that?</content>
<content>Who's there?</content>
<content>What d'you want?"</content>
<content>His mother's: "Here, Felice, come and hook this; your master'll never get done."</content>
<content>He put his hand up to his throat, and said hoarsely:
"It's I - Soames!"</content>
<content>He noticed gratefully the affectionate surprise in Emily's: "Well, my dear boy?"</content>
<content>and James', as he dropped the hook: "What, Soames!</content>
<content>What's brought you up?</content>
<content>Aren't  you well?"</content>
<content>He answered mechanically: "I'm all right," and looked at them, and it seemed impossible to bring out his news.</content>
<content>James, quick to take alarm, began: "You don't  look well.</content>
<content>I expect you've  taken a chill - it's liver, I shouldn't  wonder.</content>
<content>Your mother'll give you...."
But Emily broke in quietly: "Have you brought Irene?"</content>
<content>Soames shook his head.</content>
<content>"No," he stammered, 'she - she's left me!"</content>
<content>Emily deserted the mirror before which she was standing.</content>
<content>Her tall, full figure lost its majesty and became very human as she came running over to Soames.</content>
<content>"My dear boy!</content>
<content>My dear boy!"</content>
<content>She put her lips to his forehead, and stroked his hand.</content>
<content>James, too, had turned full towards his son; his face looked older.</content>
<content>"Left you?"</content>
<content>he said.</content>
<content>"What d'you mean - left you?</content>
<content>You never told me she was going to leave you."</content>
<content>Soames answered surlily: "How could I tell?</content>
<content>What's to be done?"</content>
<content>James began walking up and down; he looked strange and stork-like without a coat.</content>
<content>"What's to be done!"</content>
<content>he muttered.</content>
<content>"How should I know what's to be done?</content>
<content>What's the good of asking me?</content>
<content>Nobody tells me anything, and then they come and ask me what's to be done; and I should like to know how I'm to tell them!</content>
<content>Here's your mother, there she stands; she doesn't  say anything.</content>
<content>What I should say you've  got to do is to follow her.."
Soames smiled; his peculiar, supercilious smile had never before looked pitiable.</content>
<content>"I don't  know where she's gone," he said.</content>
<content>"Don't  know where she's gone!"</content>
<content>said James.</content>
<content>"How d'you mean, don't  know where she's gone?</content>
<content>Where d'you suppose she's gone?</content>
<content>She's gone after that young Bosinney, that's where she's gone.</content>
<content>I knew how it would be."</content>
<content>Soames, in the long silence that followed, felt his mother pressing his hand.</content>
<content>And all that passed seemed to pass as though his own power of thinking or doing had gone to sleep.</content>
<content>His father's face, dusky red, twitching as if he were going to cry, and words breaking out that seemed rent from him by some spasm in his soul.</content>
<content>"There'll be a scandal; I always said so."</content>
<content>Then, no one saying anything: "And there you stand, you and your mother!"</content>
<content>And Emily's voice, calm, rather contemptuous: "Come, now, James!</content>
<content>Soames will do all that he can."</content>
<content>And James, staring at the floor, a little brokenly: "Well, I can't  help you; I'm getting old.</content>
<content>Don't  you be in too great a hurry, my boy."</content>
<content>And his mother's voice again: "Soames will do all he can to get her back.</content>
<content>We won't  talk of it.</content>
<content>It'll all come right, I dare say."</content>
<content>And James: "Well, I can't  see how it can come right.</content>
<content>And if she hasn't  gone off with that young Bosinney, my advice to you is not to listen to her, but to follow her and get her back."</content>
<content>Once more Soames felt his mother stroking his hand, in token of her approval, and as though repeating some form of sacred oath, he muttered between his teeth: "I will!"</content>
<content>All three went down to the drawing-room together.</content>
<content>There, were gathered the three girls and Dartie; had Irene been present, the family circle would have been complete.</content>
<content>James sank into his armchair, and except for a word of cold greeting to Dartie, whom he both despised and dreaded, as a man likely to be always in want of money, he said nothing till dinner was announced.</content>
<content>Soames, too, was silent; Emily alone, a woman of cool courage, maintained a conversation with Winifred on trivial subjects.</content>
<content>She was never more composed in her manner and conversation than that evening.</content>
<content>A decision having been come to not to speak of Irene's flight, no view was expressed by any other member of the family as to the right course to be pursued; there can be little doubt, from the general tone adopted in relation to events as they afterwards turned out, that James's advice: "Don't  you listen to her, follow-her and get her back!"</content>
<content>would, with here and there an exception, have been regarded as sound, not only in Park Lane, but amongst the Nicholases, the Rogers, and at Timothy's.</content>
<content>Just as it would surely have been endorsed by that wider body of Forsytes all over London, who were merely excluded from judgment by ignorance of the story.</content>
<content>In spite then of Emily's efforts, the dinner was served by Warmson and the footman almost in silence.</content>
<content>Dartie was sulky, and drank all he could get; the girls seldom talked to each other at any time.</content>
<content>James asked once where June was, and what she was doing with herself in these days.</content>
<content>No one could tell him.</content>
<content>He sank back into gloom.</content>
<content>Only when Winifred recounted how little Publius had given his bad penny to a beggar, did he brighten up.</content>
<content>"Ah!"</content>
<content>he said, "that's a clever little chap.</content>
<content>I don't  know what'll become of him, if he goes on like this.</content>
<content>An intelligent little chap, I call him!"</content>
<content>But it was only a flash.</content>
<content>The courses succeeded one another solemnly, under the electric light, which glared down onto the table, but barely reached the principal ornament of the walls, a so-called 'Sea Piece by Turner,' almost entirely composed of cordage and drowning men.</content>
<content>Champagne was handed, and then a bottle of James' prehistoric port, but as by the chill hand of some skeleton.</content>
<content>At ten o'clock Soames left; twice in reply to questions, he had said that Irene was not well; he felt he could no longer trust himself.</content>
<content>His mother kissed him with her large soft kiss, and he pressed her hand, a flush of warmth in his cheeks.</content>
<content>He walked away in the cold wind, which whistled desolately round the corners of the streets, under a sky of clear steel-blue, alive with stars; he noticed neither their frosty greeting, nor the crackle of the curled-up plane-leaves, nor the night-women hurrying in their shabby furs, nor the pinched faces of vagabonds at street corners.</content>
<content>Winter was come!</content>
<content>But Soames hastened home, oblivious; his hands trembled as he took the late letters from the gilt wire cage into which they had been thrust through the slit in the door.'</content>
<content>None from Irene!</content>
<content>He went into the dining-room; the fire was bright there, his chair drawn up to it, slippers ready, spirit case, and carven cigarette box on the table; but after staring at it all for a minute or two, he turned out the light and went upstairs.</content>
<content>There was a fire too in his dressing-room, but her room was dark and cold.</content>
<content>It was into this room that Soames went.</content>
<content>He made a great illumination with candles, and for a long time continued pacing up and down between the bed and the door.</content>
<content>He could not get used to the thought that she had really left him, and as though still searching for some message, some reason, some reading of all the mystery of his married life, he began opening every recess and drawer.</content>
<content>There were her dresses; he had always liked, indeed insisted, that she should be well-dressed - she had taken very few; two or three at most, and drawer after drawer; full of linen and silk things, was untouched.</content>
<content>Perhaps after all it was only a freak, and she had gone to the seaside for a few days' change.</content>
<content>If only that were so, and she were really coming back, he would never again do as he had done that fatal night before last, never again run that risk - though it was her duty, her duty as a wife; though she did belong to him - he would never again run that risk; she was evidently not quite right in her head!</content>
<content>He stooped over the drawer where she kept her jewels; it was not locked, and came open as he pulled; the jewel box had the key in it.</content>
<content>This surprised him until he remembered that it was sure to be empty.</content>
<content>He opened it.</content>
<content>It was far from empty.</content>
<content>Divided, in little green velvet compartments, were all the things he had given her, even her watch, and stuck into the recess that contained - the watch was a three-cornered note addressed 'Soames Forsyte,' in Irene's handwriting:
'I think I have taken nothing that you or your people have given me.'</content>
<content>And that was all.</content>
<content>He looked at the clasps and bracelets of diamonds and pearls, at the little flat gold watch with a great diamond set in sapphires, at the chains and rings, each in its nest, and the tears rushed up in his eyes and dropped upon them.</content>
<content>Nothing that she could have done, nothing that she had done, brought home to him like this the inner significance of her act.</content>
<content>For the moment, perhaps, he understood nearly all there was to understand - understood that she loathed him, that she had loathed him for years, that for all intents and purposes they were like people living in different worlds, that there was no hope for him, never had been; even, that she had suffered - that she was to be pitied.</content>
<content>In that moment of emotion he betrayed the Forsyte in him - forgot himself, his interests, his property - was capable of almost anything; was lifted into the pure ether of the selfless and unpractical.</content>
<content>Such moments pass quickly.</content>
<content>And as though with the tears he had purged himself of weakness, he got up, locked the box, and slowly, almost trembling, carried it with him into the other room.</content>
<content>CHAPTER VII - JUNE'S VICTORY
June had waited for her chance, scanning the duller columns of the journals, morning and evening with an assiduity which at first puzzled old Jolyon; and when her chance came, she took it with all the promptitude and resolute tenacity of her character.</content>
<content>She will always remember best in her life that morning when at last she saw amongst the reliable Cause List of the Times newspaper, under the heading of Court XIII, Mr. Justice Bentham, the case of Forsyte v. Bosinney.</content>
<content>Like a gambler who stakes his last piece of money, she had prepared to hazard her all upon this throw; it was not her nature to contemplate defeat.</content>
<content>How, unless with the instinct of a woman in love, she knew that Bosinney's discomfiture in this action was assured, cannot be told - on this assumption, however, she laid her plans, as upon a certainty.</content>
<content>Half past eleven found her at watch in the gallery of Court XIII., and there she remained till the case of Forsyte v. Bosinney was over.</content>
<content>Bosinney's absence did not disquiet her; she had felt instinctively that he would not defend himself.</content>
<content>At the end of the judgment she hastened down, and took a cab to his rooms.</content>
<content>She passed the open street-door and the offices on the three lower floors without attracting notice; not till she reached the top did her difficulties begin.</content>
<content>Her ring was not answered; she had now to make up her mind whether she would go down and ask the caretaker in the basement to let her in to await Mr. Bosinney's return, or remain patiently outside the door, trusting that no one would, come up.</content>
<content>She decided on the latter course.</content>
<content>A quarter of an hour had passed in freezing vigil on the landing, before it occurred to her that Bosinney had been used to leave the key of his rooms under the door-mat.</content>
<content>She looked and found it there.</content>
<content>For some minutes she could not decide to make use of it; at last she let herself in and left the door open that anyone who came might see she was there on business.</content>
<content>This was not the same June who had paid the trembling visit five months ago; those months of suffering and restraint had made her less sensitive; she had dwelt on this visit so long, with such minuteness, that its terrors were discounted beforehand.</content>
<content>She was not there to fail this time, for if she failed no one could help her.</content>
<content>Like some mother beast on the watch over her young, her little quick figure never stood still in that room, but wandered from wall to wall, from window to door, fingering now one thing, now another.</content>
<content>There was dust everywhere, the room could not have been cleaned for weeks, and June, quick to catch at anything that should buoy up her hope, saw in it a sign that he had been obliged, for economy's sake, to give up his servant.</content>
<content>She looked into the bedroom; the bed was roughly made, as though by the hand of man.</content>
<content>Listening intently, she darted in, and peered into his cupboards.</content>
<content>A few shirts and collars, a pair of muddy boots - the room was bare even of garments.</content>
<content>She stole back to the sitting-room, and now she noticed the absence of all the little things he had set store by.</content>
<content>The clock that had been his mother's, the field-glasses that had hung over the sofa; two really valuable old prints of Harrow, where his father had been at school, and last, not least, the piece of Japanese pottery she herself had given him.</content>
<content>All were gone; and in spite of the rage roused within her championing soul at the thought that the world should treat him thus, their disappearance augured happily for the success of her plan.</content>
<content>It was while looking at the spot where the piece of Japanese pottery had stood that she felt a strange certainty of being watched, and, turning, saw Irene in the open doorway.</content>
<content>The two stood gazing at each other for a minute in silence; then June walked forward and held out her hand.</content>
<content>Irene did not take it.</content>
<content>When her hand was refused, June put it behind her.</content>
<content>Her eyes grew steady with anger; she waited for Irene to speak; and thus waiting, took in, with who-knows-what rage of jealousy, suspicion, and curiosity, every detail of her friend's face and dress and figure.</content>
<content>Irene was clothed in her long grey fur; the travelling cap on her head left a wave of gold hair visible above her forehead.</content>
<content>The soft fullness of the coat made her face as small as a child's.</content>
<content>Unlike June's cheeks, her cheeks had no colour in them, but were ivory white and pinched as if with cold.</content>
<content>Dark circles lay round her eyes.</content>
<content>In one hand she held a bunch of violets.</content>
<content>She looked back at June, no smile on her lips; and with those great dark eyes fastened on her, the girl, for all her startled anger, felt something of the old spell.</content>
<content>She spoke first, after all.</content>
<content>"What have you come for?"</content>
<content>But the feeling that she herself was being asked the same question, made her add: "This horrible case.</content>
<content>I came to tell him - he has lost it."</content>
<content>Irene did not speak, her eyes never moved from June's face, and the girl cried:
"Don't  stand there as if you were made of stone!"</content>
<content>Irene laughed: "I wish to God I were!"</content>
<content>But June turned away: "Stop!"</content>
<content>she cried, "don't  tell me!</content>
<content>I don't  want to hear!</content>
<content>I don't  want to hear what you've  come for.</content>
<content>I don't  want to hear!"</content>
<content>And like some uneasy spirit, she began swiftly walking to and fro.</content>
<content>Suddenly she broke out:
"I was here first.</content>
<content>We can't  both stay here together!"</content>
<content>On Irene's face a smile wandered up, and died out like a flicker of firelight.</content>
<content>She did not move.</content>
<content>And then it was that June perceived under the softness and immobility of this figure something desperate and resolved; something not to be turned away, something dangerous.</content>
<content>She tore off her hat, and, putting both hands to her brow, pressed back the bronze mass of her hair.</content>
<content>"You have no right here!"</content>
<content>she cried defiantly.</content>
<content>Irene answered: "I have no right anywhere!</content>
<content>"What do you mean?"</content>
<content>"I have left Soames.</content>
<content>You always wanted me to!"</content>
<content>June put her hands over her ears.</content>
<content>"Don't !</content>
<content>I don't  want to hear anything - I don't  want to know anything.</content>
<content>It's impossible to fight with you!</content>
<content>What makes you stand like that?</content>
<content>Why don't  you go?"</content>
<content>Irene's lips moved; she seemed to be saying: "Where should I go?"</content>
<content>June turned to the window.</content>
<content>She could see the face of a clock down in the street.</content>
<content>It was nearly four.</content>
<content>At any moment he might come!</content>
<content>She looked back across her shoulder, and her face was distorted with anger.</content>
<content>But Irene had not moved; in her gloved hands she ceaselessly turned and twisted the little bunch of violets.</content>
<content>The tears of rage and disappointment rolled down June's cheeks.</content>
<content>"How could you come?"</content>
<content>she said.</content>
<content>"You have been a false friend to me!"</content>
<content>Again Irene laughed.</content>
<content>June saw that she had played a wrong card, and broke down.</content>
<content>"Why have you come?"</content>
<content>she sobbed.</content>
<content>"You've  ruined my life, and now you want to ruin his!"</content>
<content>Irene's mouth quivered; her eyes met June's with a look so mournful that the girl cried out in the midst of her sobbing, "No, no!"</content>
<content>But Irene's head bent till it touched her breast.</content>
<content>She turned, and went quickly out, hiding her lips with the little bunch of violets.</content>
<content>June ran to the door.</content>
<content>She heard the footsteps going down and down.</content>
<content>She called out: "Come back, Irene!</content>
<content>Come back!"</content>
<content>The footsteps died away....
Bewildered and torn, the girl stood at the top of the stairs.</content>
<content>Why had Irene gone, leaving her mistress of the field?</content>
<content>What did it mean?</content>
<content>Had she really given him up to her?</content>
<content>Or had she...?</content>
<content>And she was the prey of a gnawing uncertainty.... Bosinney did not come....
About six o'clock that afternoon old Jolyon returned from Wistaria Avenue, where now almost every day he spent some hours, and asked if his grand-daughter were upstairs.</content>
<content>On being told that she had just come in, he sent up to her room to request her to come down and speak to him.</content>
<content>He had made up his mind to tell her that he was reconciled with her father.</content>
<content>In future bygones must be bygones.</content>
<content>He would no longer live alone, or practically alone, in this great house; he was going to give it up, and take one in the country for his son, where they could all go and live together.</content>
<content>If June did not like this, she could have an allowance and live by herself.</content>
<content>It wouldn't  make much difference to her, for it was a long time since she had shown him any affection.</content>
<content>But when June came down, her face was pinched and piteous; there was a strained, pathetic look in her eyes.</content>
<content>She snuggled up in her old attitude on the arm of his chair, and what he said compared but poorly with the clear, authoritative, injured statement he had thought out with much care.</content>
<content>His heart felt sore, as the great heart of a mother-bird feels sore when its youngling flies and bruises its wing.</content>
<content>His words halted, as though he were apologizing for having at last deviated from the path of virtue, and succumbed, in defiance of sounder principles, to his more natural instincts.</content>
<content>He seemed nervous lest, in thus announcing his intentions, he should be setting his granddaughter a bad example; and now that he came to the point, his way of putting the suggestion that, if she didn't  like it, she could live by herself and lump it, was delicate in the extreme.'</content>
<content>"And if, by any chance, my darling," he said, "you found you didn't  get on - with them, why, I could make that all right.</content>
<content>You could have what you liked.</content>
<content>We could find a little flat in London where you could set up, and I could be running to continually.</content>
<content>But the children," he added, "are dear little things!"</content>
<content>Then, in the midst of this grave, rather transparent, explanation of changed policy, his eyes twinkled.</content>
<content>"This'll astonish Timothy's weak nerves.</content>
<content>That precious young thing will have something to say about this, or I'm a Dutchman!"</content>
<content>June had not yet spoken.</content>
<content>Perched thus on the arm of his chair, with her head above him, her face was invisible.</content>
<content>But presently he felt her warm cheek against his own, and knew that, at all events, there was nothing very alarming in her attitude towards his news.</content>
<content>He began to take courage.</content>
<content>"You'll like your father," he said - "an amiable chap.</content>
<content>Never was much push about him, but easy to get on with.</content>
<content>You'll find him artistic and all that."</content>
<content>And old Jolyon bethought him of the dozen or so water-colour drawings all carefully locked up in his bedroom; for now that his son was going to become a man of property he did not think them quite such poor things as heretofore.</content>
<content>"As to your - your stepmother," he said, using the word with some little difficulty, "I call her a refined woman - a bit of a Mrs. Gummidge, I shouldn't  wonder - but very fond of Jo.</content>
<content>And the children," he repeated - indeed, this sentence ran like music through all his solemn self-justification - "are sweet little things!"</content>
<content>If June had known, those words but reincarnated that tender love for little children, for the young and weak, which in the past had made him desert his son for her tiny self, and now, as the cycle rolled, was taking him from her.</content>
<content>But he began to get alarmed at her silence, and asked impatiently: "Well, what do you say?"</content>
<content>June slid down to his knee, and she in her turn began her tale.</content>
<content>She thought it would all go splendidly; she did not see any difficulty, and she did not care a bit what people thought.</content>
<content>Old Jolyon wriggled.</content>
<content>H'm!</content>
<content>then people would think!</content>
<content>He had thought that after all these years perhaps they wouldn't !</content>
<content>Well, he couldn't  help it!</content>
<content>Nevertheless, he could not approve of his granddaughter's way of putting it - she ought to mind what people thought!</content>
<content>Yet he said nothing.</content>
<content>His feelings were too mixed, too inconsistent for expression.</content>
<content>No - went on June he did not care; what business was it of theirs?</content>
<content>There was only one thing - and with her cheek pressing against his knee, old Jolyon knew at once that this something was no trifle: As he was going to buy a house in the country, would he not - to please her - buy that splendid house of Soames' at Robin Hill?</content>
<content>It was finished, it was perfectly beautiful, and no one would live in it now.</content>
<content>They would all be so happy there.</content>
<content>Old Jolyon was on the alert at once.</content>
<content>Wasn't  the 'man of property' going to live in his new house, then?</content>
<content>He never alluded to Soames now but under this title.</content>
<content>"No" - June said - "he was not; she knew that he was not!"</content>
<content>How did she know?</content>
<content>She could not tell him, but she knew.</content>
<content>She knew nearly for certain!</content>
<content>It was most unlikely; circumstances had changed!</content>
<content>Irene's words still rang in her head: "I have left Soames.</content>
<content>Where should I go?"</content>
<content>But she kept silence about that.</content>
<content>If her grandfather would only buy it and settle that wretched claim that ought never to have been made on Phil!</content>
<content>It would be the very best thing for everybody, and everything - everything might come straight.</content>
<content>And June put her lips to his forehead, and pressed them close.</content>
<content>But old Jolyon freed himself from her caress, his face wore the judicial look which came upon it when he dealt with affairs.</content>
<content>He asked: What did she mean?</content>
<content>There was something behind all this - had she been seeing Bosinney?</content>
<content>June answered: "No; but I have been to his rooms."</content>
<content>"Been to his rooms?</content>
<content>Who took you there?"</content>
<content>June faced him steadily.</content>
<content>"I went alone.</content>
<content>He has lost that case.</content>
<content>I don't  care whether it was right or wrong.</content>
<content>I want to help him; and I will!"</content>
<content>Old Jolyon asked again: "Have you seen him?"</content>
<content>His glance seemed to pierce right through the girl's eyes into her soul.</content>
<content>Again June answered: "No; he was not there.</content>
<content>I waited, but he did not come."</content>
<content>Old Jolyon made a movement of relief.</content>
<content>She had risen and looked down at him; so slight, and light, and young, but so fixed, and so determined; and disturbed, vexed, as he was, he could not frown away that fixed look.</content>
<content>The feeling of being beaten, of the reins having slipped, of being old and tired, mastered him.</content>
<content>"Ah!"</content>
<content>he said at last, "you'll get yourself into a mess one of these days, I can see.</content>
<content>You want your own way in everything."</content>
<content>Visited by one of his strange bursts of philosophy, he added: "Like that you were born; and like that you'll stay until you die!"</content>
<content>And he, who in his dealings with men of business, with Boards, with Forsytes of all descriptions, with such as were not Forsytes, had always had his own way, looked at his indomitable grandchild sadly - for he felt in her that quality which above all others he unconsciously admired.</content>
<content>"Do you know what they say is going on?"</content>
<content>he said slowly.</content>
<content>June crimsoned.</content>
<content>"Yes - no!</content>
<content>I know - and I don't  know - I don't  care!"</content>
<content>and she stamped her foot.</content>
<content>"I believe," said old Jolyon, dropping his eyes, "that you'd have him if he were dead!"</content>
<content>There was a long silence before he spoke again.</content>
<content>"But as to buying this house - you don't  know what you're talking about!"</content>
<content>June said that she did.</content>
<content>She knew that he could get it if he wanted.</content>
<content>He would only have to give what it cost.</content>
<content>"What it cost!</content>
<content>You know nothing about it.</content>
<content>I won't  go to Soames - I'll have nothing more to do with that young man."</content>
<content>"But you needn't ; you can go to Uncle James.</content>
<content>If you can't  buy the house, will you pay his lawsuit claim?</content>
<content>I know he is terribly hard up - I've  seen it.</content>
<content>You can stop it out of my money!"</content>
<content>A twinkle came into old Jolyon's eyes.</content>
<content>"Stop it out of your money!</content>
<content>A pretty way.</content>
<content>And what will you do, pray, without your money?"</content>
<content>But secretly, the idea of wresting the house from James and his son had begun to take hold of him.</content>
<content>He had heard on Forsyte 'Change much comment, much rather doubtful praise of this house.</content>
<content>It was 'too artistic,' but a fine place.</content>
<content>To take from the 'man of property' that on which he had set his heart, would be a crowning triumph over James, practical proof that he was going to make a man of property of Jo, to put him back in his proper position, and there to keep him secure.</content>
<content>Justice once for all on those who had chosen to regard his son as a poor, penniless outcast.</content>
<content>He would see, he would see!</content>
<content>It might be out of the question; he was not going to pay a fancy price, but if it could be done, why, perhaps he would do it!</content>
<content>And still more secretly he knew that he could not refuse her.</content>
<content>But he did not commit himself.</content>
<content>He would think it over - he said to June.</content>
<content>CHAPTER VIII - BOSINNEY'S DEPARTURE
Old Jolyon was not given to hasty decisions; it is probable that he would have continued to think over the purchase of the house at Robin Hill, had not June's face told him that he would have no peace until he acted.</content>
<content>At breakfast next morning she asked him what time she should order the carriage.</content>
<content>"Carriage!"</content>
<content>he said, with some appearance of innocence; "what for?</content>
<content>I'm not going out!"</content>
